Refuting the First Difficulty: Continuity as Substance
First difficulty and its refutation, with sub-arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that continuity cannot be a substance by distinguishing between the accident of continuity and the substantial continuity that underlies the body, and by arguing that splitting a continuous identity requires a persistent substratum.

Core Idea
Continuity is a substance, not an accident—essential for understanding the body.

This passage addresses the first difficulty raised against the Ash'ari notion of continuity as a substance. The opponent argues that continuity is merely an accident opposite to discontinuity, so it cannot be a substance or part of the body. The author clarifies his intended meaning—a substance whose property is continuity—and then defends his position by countering an objection about the continuous identity being the body itself, distinguishing between perishing of a body and splitting of identity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the logical flow: objection, answer, counter-objection, refined answer.
2
Identify the initial objection
Find the first sentence: the opponent claims that continuity being a substance is 'manifestly false' because continuity is just the accident opposite to discontinuity.
The opponent reduces continuity to an accident alternating with discontinuity.
3
Understand the author's clarification
The author responds by redefining continuity: he means a substance whose inherent property is continuity and extension, not the accident. The reality of the body depends on this substance.
He even notes that those who say body is mere accidents still end up positing a substance when accidents combine.
4
Follow the counter-objection and refutation
Read the 'laa yuqaalu' section: the counter-objection suggests the continuous identity could be the body itself. The author refutes by distinguishing between total perishing (body gone) and splitting into two continuous identities (requires a remaining substratum).
This is the core logical move: splitting implies a persistent locus for the continuous form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: أن كون الاتصال جوهراً أو جزءاً من الجسم ظاهر البطلان . إذ لا يعقل منه إلا ما يقابل الانفصال وهما عرضان يتعاقبان على الجسم، إذا تحققتهما كانا عائدين إلى وحدته وكثرته . وجوابه: أنا لا نعني بالاتصال هذا المعنى بل الجوهر الذي شأنه الاتصال وامتداد العرضي وكونه ظاهر الآنية للجسم موقوفاً تعقل حقيقة الجسم على تعقله مما لم يشك فيه عاقل، ولم ينكره أحد إلا ما نسب إلى البعض من كون الجسم محض الأعراض، على أنه أيضاً قائل بأنها عند الاجتماع تصير جوهراً قائماً بنفسه، وإنما النزاع في كونه واحداً في نفس الأمر لا متحصلاً من اجتماع الأجزاء، وفي كونه جزءاً من الجسم لإتمام حقيقته، فهذا هو الذي يثبت بالبرهان لا يقال: فما ذكره لا يفيد كونه جزءاً لجواز أن تكون تلك الهوية الاتصالية الجوهرية التي يجعلونها صورة حالة في مادة نفس الجسم من غير حلول في جزء آخر، ويكون قبول الانفصال بأن ينعدم، ويحدث هويتان اتصاليتان أخريان . كيف وقد جعلتموها جوهراً قابلاً للأبعاد ومتميزة بالذات، فيكون قيامها بنفسها لا بغيرها. لأنا نقول ضرورة التفرقة بين انعدام جسم بالكلية، وحدوث جسمين، وبين زوال الهوية الاتصالية إلى هويتين [ هي التي شهدت بوجود جزء آخر باق في الحالين، ثم إنهم لم يجعلوا الصورة قائمة به لتنافي جوهريتها ]، بل حالة فيه، وقد سبق أن الحال في الشيء أعم من القائم به، لكن الشأن في لزوم كون ذلك الأمر الباقي محلاً للجوهر الذي سموه الصورة الجسمية وعبروا عنها بالهوية الاتصالية، وفي تصور حلول الجوهر في الشيء مع امتناع قيامه به . فإن قيل: نسبة المقبول إلى القابل اختصاص الناعت، وهو معنى الحلول . قلنا: الكلام في كون الهوية الاتصالية بمعنى الجوهر، الذي شأنه الاتصال مقبولاً وإنما يظهر ذلك في الاتصال العرضي المقابل للانفصال.
The first [difficulty]: That continuity is a substance or part of the body is manifestly false, for nothing is understood from it except what is opposite to discontinuity, and both are accidents that alternate upon the body; if you realize them, they return to its unity and multiplicity. Its answer: We do not mean by continuity this meaning, but rather the substance whose property is continuity and accidental extension, and that it is the apparent 'now' of the body, such that the understanding of the reality of the body depends on understanding it. No rational person has doubted this, and none denied it except what is attributed to some that the body is mere accidents, although he also says that when they come together they become a substance subsisting by itself. Rather, the dispute is about it being one in itself not composed from the aggregation of parts, and about it being a part of the body to complete its reality. This is what is established by demonstration. It cannot be said: What you mentioned does not prove it is a part, for it is possible that that continuous substantial identity which they make a form inhering in matter is the body itself, without inherence in another part, and the acceptance of discontinuity is by perishing and two other continuous identities arising. Moreover, you have made it a substance receptive of dimensions and distinguished by essence, so it subsists by itself not by another. For we say: The necessity of the distinction between the perishing of a body entirely and the arising of two bodies, and between the removal of the continuous identity into two identities, is what testifies to the existence of another part remaining in both states. Then they did not make the form subsisting in it due to the incompatibility of its substance, but rather inhering in it. And it has preceded that the inherence in something is more general than subsisting in it. But the issue is the necessity of that remaining thing being a locus (محل) for the substance which they call the bodily form (الصورة الجسمية) and expressed as the continuous identity, and in conceiving the inherence of a substance in something while it cannot subsist in it. If it is said: The relation of the recipient to the receptacle is the specification of the descriptor, and that is the meaning of inherence. We say: The discussion is about the continuous identity in the sense of the substance, whose property is continuity, being recipient. That appears only in accidental continuity opposed to discontinuity.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage addresses the first difficulty (الأول): the claim that continuity being a substance or part of the body is obviously false, since 'continuity' normally means the accident opposite to discontinuity. The author responds by clarifying that he does not mean the accident of continuity but rather a substance whose inherent property is continuity and extension. This substance is so fundamental that grasping the reality of the body depends on understanding it. He notes that only those who reduce body to mere accidents deny it, but even they admit that accidents when combined become a substance. The real dispute is whether this substance is one in itself (not composed of parts) and whether it is a part of the body. The author then refutes a counter-objection (لا يقال) that the continuous identity might be the body itself without needing another part. He argues by distinguishing between two scenarios: (1) a body perishing entirely and two new bodies arising, and (2) one continuous identity splitting into two. The latter necessarily implies a persisting substratum that receives the continuous form. This substratum is not the continuous identity itself but a locus (محل) for it. The form (الصورة) inheres in this locus, but inherence (حلول) is broader than subsistence (قيام). The passage ends with a final sub-objection about the meaning of inherence, answered by specifying that the discussion concerns the substance whose property is continuity, not accidental continuity that is opposite to discontinuity.

Key Terms
الاتصال (continuity)
جوهر (substance)
الصورة الجسمية (bodily form)
محل (locus)
حلول (inherence)
قيام (subsistence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Confusing the accident of continuity (opposite to discontinuity) with the substance that has continuity as its property.
Correction: The author explicitly states: 'We do not mean by continuity this meaning [the accident] but rather the substance whose property is continuity and accidental extension.'

Checkpoint
What does the author mean by 'continuity' (الاتصال) in the answer to the first difficulty?
A) The accident opposite to discontinuity
B) A substance whose property is continuity and extension
C) A quantity or measurement
D) The form inhering in matter

Key Takeaway
The author defends continuity as a substance by distinguishing it from the accident of continuity and by arguing that splitting a continuous identity requires a persistent substratum, proving the reality of substantial continuity.

Second Difficulty: Discontinuity Does Not Need a Locus
The second difficulty argues that discontinuity does not require a locus because it is non-existential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that discontinuity, being non-existential, does not require a remaining locus.

Core Idea
Discontinuity is not an existing thing—it's just the absence of continuity.

The author's position assumes that discontinuity needs a persisting substratum. But the second difficulty challenges this: if discontinuity is merely the non-existence of continuity, then it has no need for a locus at all. This passage presents that objection clearly, though the author's answer is not yet given.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and its literal translation. Note the concise style.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage states that discontinuity would only need a remaining locus 'if it were existent' (لو كان وجودياً). But it is not; it is 'the non-existence of continuity and its removal'.
3
Understand the logical consequence
Since discontinuity is non-existential, the objection says it cannot require a locus, because non-existents do not inhere in substances.
4
Note the incomplete argument
This is only the objection. The author's answer is not included here; it likely follows on the next page. Reflect on why this objection might be a problem for the author.
The answer will reconcile the need for a locus with the non-existential nature of discontinuity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: إن الانفصال إنما يفتقر إلى محل باق لو كان وجودياً وهو ممنوع، بل هو عبارة عن انعدام الاتصال وزواله.
The second [difficulty]: That discontinuity only requires a remaining locus if it is existent, which is denied; rather, it is an expression of the non-existence of continuity and its removal.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents the second difficulty (الثاني) raised against the author's position. The objection argues that discontinuity (الانفصال) does not need a persisting locus (محل باق) because discontinuity is not an existent entity (وجودي); rather, it is simply the non-existence (انعدام) of continuity and its removal (زوال). Therefore, the argument that a substratum must remain to undergo discontinuity is unsound, since the discontinuity is just the absence of continuity and does not inhere in anything. The answer to this objection is not included in the current page window; the author likely continues on the following page.

Key Terms
الانفصال (discontinuity)
وجودي (existent)
انعدام (non-existence)
محل باق (remaining locus)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that everything that happens to a body, including discontinuity, requires a substratum.
Correction: This objection challenges that assumption by pointing out that discontinuity is not an existent thing but the absence of continuity, and thus may not need a locus.

Checkpoint
According to the second difficulty, what is discontinuity (الانفصال) an expression of?
A) An existential quality
B) The non-existence of continuity and its removal
C) A new continuous identity
D) A change in the locus

Key Takeaway
Discontinuity is not an existent attribute; it is merely the absence of continuity, so the objection claims it does not require a locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider an area of your life where you feel a 'discontinuity'—a broken relationship or a missing virtue. Instead of treating it as a fixed flaw, what if you saw it as a mere absence of something positive? How would that change your next step?
?
In your spiritual journey, do you sometimes mistake an absence (like lack of focus in prayer) as a permanent attribute? How does recognizing it as non-existence (انعدام) rather than an existent flaw open a path to change?

Objection and Answer on Substantial Continuity as Part of Body
The author refutes an objection that continuity might be a form in matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that substantial continuity might be a form inhering in matter, and how the author refutes it by distinguishing between complete annihilation and transformation of continuity.

Core Idea
Substantial continuity cannot be a mere form; its change implies a remaining part.

This passage presents an objection against the claim that substantial continuity (al-huwiyya al-ittiṣāliyya) is a part of the body. The objector argues that continuity could be a form inhering in matter, not a separate part. The author responds by clarifying the logical implications that follow when continuity changes. Understanding this exchange deepens our grasp of the body's ontological structure in kalām.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector claims continuity is not a part but a form inhering in matter. Notice the reasoning about discontinuity occurring by the form ceasing and two new continuity-hoods arising.
2
Identify the core distinction
The author distinguishes between complete annihilation of a body and the transformation of one continuity into two, which implies a remaining part.
This distinction is key to the refutation.
3
Understand the concepts of ḥāl and maḥall
The author notes that the form is a state (ḥāl) in the remaining part, not subsisting by it, but the problem is that the remaining part would be a locus (maḥall) for a substance, which is problematic.
The state in a thing is more general than subsistence.
4
Synthesize the answer
The answer shows that the objection fails because it requires a substantial part to persist through change, contradicting the idea of it being a mere form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : فما ذكره لا يفيد كونه جزءاً لجواز أن تكون تلك الهوية الاتصالية الجوهرية التي يجعلونها صورة حالة في مادة نفس الجسم من غير حلول في جزء آخر، ويكون قبول الانفصال بأن ينعدم، ويحدث هويتان اتصاليتان أخريان . كيف وقد جعلتموها جوهراً قابلاً للأبعاد ومتميزة بالذات، فيكون قيامها بنفسها لا بغيرها. لأنا نقول ضرورة التفرقة بين انعدام جسم بالكلية، وحدوث جسمين، وبين زوال الهوية الاتصالية إلى هويتين [ هي التي شهدت بوجود جزء آخر باق في الحالين، ثم إنهم لم يجعلوا الصورة قائمة به لتنافي جوهريتها ] (١) ، بل حالة فيه ، وقد سبق أن الحال في الشيء أعم من القائم به، لكن الشأن في لزوم كون ذلك الأمر الباقي محلاً للجوهر الذي سموه الصورة الجسمية وعبروا عنها بالهوية الاتصالية، وفي تصور حلول الجوهر في الشيء مع امتناع قيامه به .
It should not be said: What he mentioned does not prove it to be a part, because it is possible that that substantial continuity-hood which they make a form (ṣūra) inhering in the matter of the body itself, without indwelling in another part, and the acceptance of discontinuity occurs by its ceasing to exist, and two other continuity-hoods come to be. How? You have made it a substance (jawhar) receptive of dimensions and distinct in essence, so it subsists by itself, not by another. For we say: There is necessarily a distinction between the complete annihilation of a body and the coming-to-be of two bodies, and between the removal of one continuity-hood into two continuity-hoods [which testify to the existence of another part remaining in both states. Then they did not make the form subsisting by it because that would contradict its substantiality], but rather it is a state (ḥāl) in it, and it has preceded that the state in a thing is more general than what subsists by it, but the concern is the necessity that that remaining thing be a locus (maḥall) for the substance which they named the corporeal form and expressed by continuity-hood, and in conceiving the indwelling (ḥulūl) of a substance in a thing while subsistence (qiyām) by it is impossible.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector argues that the author's argument does not prove that continuity (ittiṣāl) is a part of the body, because the substantial continuity-hood (al-huwiyya al-ittiṣāliyya al-jawhariyya) could be a form (ṣūra) inhering in the matter of the body without being a part, and discontinuity (al-infiṣāl) occurs by the form ceasing and two new continuity-hoods arising. The objector points out that the author has considered this continuity a substance (jawhar) receptive of dimensions and distinct in essence, so it subsists by itself, not by another. The answer draws a necessary distinction between the complete annihilation of a body and the generation of two bodies, and the removal of one continuity-hood into two continuity-hoods, which implies a remaining part. The author notes that the form is not subsisting by that part but is a state (ḥāl) in it; a state in a thing is more general than what subsists by it. The difficulty then is in the necessity of that remaining thing being a locus for the substance, and in conceiving the indwelling (ḥulūl) of a substance while subsistence by it is impossible.

Key Terms
الهوية الاتصالية
الصورة
الجوهر
الحلول
القيام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that substantial continuity can be merely a form in matter without being a part.
Correction: The author shows that even if it were a form, its transformation into two continuity-hoods requires a remaining part to serve as a locus, thus it still implies a part.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the objection in this passage?
A) That continuity is not a part because it is an accident.
B) That continuity could be a form inhering in matter without being a part.
C) That continuity is impossible.
D) That the body is composed of atoms.

Key Takeaway
Substantial continuity implies a part that persists through change, contradicting the idea of it being a mere form inhering in matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you repent from a major sin, do you believe your old sinful self is completely annihilated, or is there a part of you—your fitrah—that remains and allows for a new continuity of righteousness? The lesson's distinction between annihilation and continuity-hood split forces you to choose: either you are a completely new creation, or there is a part that persists. Which is it, and how does that affect your accountability for past wrongs?
?
The author argues that substantial continuity cannot be a mere form; it implies a part that stays. Are you living as if your faith (īmān) is a mere form—a label that inheres in your life without a core commitment—or is it a substantial part that remains even when your actions fluctuate? Looking at your current state, what evidence is there that your īmān is a continuous substance rather than a passing form?
?
The objector's scenario would make discontinuity a simple ceasing of form without remainder. In your life, when you go through trials that shake your faith, do you treat your previous faith as completely gone, as if it never happened? The lesson says that discontinuity hints at a remaining part that witnesses to the past. Do you honestly believe your past moments of certainty still have a witness in your heart, or have you allowed them to die without trace?

The Objection on Ratio and Its Answer
Objection and answer on the ratio of accepted to acceptor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between substantial continuity and accidental continuity in the context of the accepted (maqbūl) and the acceptor (qābil), and why the objection based on indwelling (ḥulūl) does not apply.

Core Idea
Substantial continuity as a maqbūl is not subject to the objection of ḥulūl.

This lesson presents a brief objection-and-answer exchange. The objector tries to reduce the ratio of the accepted to the acceptor to the concept of indwelling (ḥulūl). The answer clarifies that the discussion is about substantial continuity, not accidental continuity, thus sidestepping the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice the structure: 'If it is said... We say...' This is a classic kalam objection-response format.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify the terms: al-maqbūl (the accepted), al-qābil (the acceptor), al-ḥulūl (indwelling), al-ittiṣāl al-ʿaraḍī (accidental continuity). Understand their roles in the argument.
3
Grasp the distinction between substantial and accidental continuity
The answer makes a key distinction: the discussion concerns continuity in the sense of substance (jawhar), not accidental continuity. The objection about ḥulūl applies only to accidental continuity.
Substantial continuity is the nature of the substance itself.
4
See how the objection is refuted
The objector's claim that the ratio is ḥulūl is negated by specifying that the accepted is substantial continuity, not accidental. Thus the objection is irrelevant to the author's doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : نسبة المقبول إلى القابل اختصاص الناعت، وهو معنى الحلول . قلنا : الكلام في كون الهوية الاتصالية بمعنى الجوهر، الذي شأنه الاتصال مقبولاً وإنما يظهر ذلك في الاتصال العرضي المقابل للانفصال.
For if it is said: The ratio of the accepted (al-maqbūl) to the acceptor (al-qābil) is the specification of the describer, and that is the meaning of indwelling (ḥulūl). We say: The discussion concerns the continuity-hood (al-huwiyya al-ittiṣāliyya) in the sense of the substance whose nature is continuity being an accepted thing, and that appears only in accidental continuity (al-ittiṣāl al-ʿaraḍī) opposite to discontinuity.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a second objection and answer. The objector claims that the ratio of the accepted to the acceptor is the specification of the describer (ikhtiṣāṣ al-nāʿit), which is the meaning of indwelling (ḥulūl). The answer clarifies that the discussion is about the continuity-hood (al-huwiyya al-ittiṣāliyya) in the sense of the substance whose nature is continuity being an accepted thing, not the accidental continuity. The notion of indwelling pertains to accidental continuity, not to substantial continuity.

Key Terms
المقبول
القابل
الحلول
الاتصال العرضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the accepted (maqbūl) always implies indwelling (ḥulūl) in the acceptor.
Correction: The passage clarifies that in this context, the accepted is substantial continuity, which does not entail ḥulūl. Ḥulūl only pertains to accidental continuity.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, in what sense is continuity-hood a 'maqbūl' (accepted thing)?
A) In the sense of accidental continuity.
B) In the sense of substantial continuity.
C) In the sense of being a form.
D) In the sense of being an accident.

Key Takeaway
The objection that the ratio of accepted to acceptor implies indwelling is inapplicable because the accepted here is substantial continuity, not accidental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that every relationship of dependence (like creature to Creator) implies imperfection or inherence? How does this lesson's distinction between substantial and accidental continuity challenge you to reconsider that assumption? Will you actively examine your beliefs about dependence and essentiality in your faith?
?
In your own life, do you tend to view your core identity (your 'substance') as something that can be reduced to changing circumstances and accidents? How can understanding that your essential self is not merely a collection of accidents change how you live today?
?
The objection here is subtle but dangerous: it reduces a profound truth to a mere accident. What is one area in your worship or understanding of God where you might have committed a similar reduction, and how will you correct it starting now?

The Second Objection: Discontinuity Requires a Remaining Locus
The author presents and answers the second objection against atomism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why discontinuity cannot be mere non-existence but involves a transition requiring a persisting subject.

Core Idea
Discontinuity is not just absence; it is the replacement of one continuity by two.

This lesson tackles the second objection to the argument for atoms. The objector claims discontinuity is simply the non-existence of continuity, not an existent requiring a subject. The author counters by showing that discontinuity is specifically the loss of a single continuity and the emergence of two, which demands a persisting substrate that can accept both states. This clarifies the metaphysical necessity of a continuing locus across change.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: first the objection, then 'And the answer' (والجواب).
The objector's claim is that discontinuity is merely non-existence (ʿadam).
2
Identify the key distinction
The answer specifies that discontinuity is not non-existence absolutely (مطلقاً), but non-existence of that whose nature is continuity. This is a crucial refinement.
The 'remaining acceptor' (القابل الباقي) is introduced as the thing that undergoes change.
3
Understand the transition
Discontinuity is described as the removal of one continuity-hood and the generation of two continuity-hoods. This requires a subject that can accept both states at different times.
Without a persisting locus, the change from one continuity to two would be unintelligible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن الانفصال إنما يفتقر إلى محل باق لو كان وجودياً وهو ممنوع ، بل هو عبارة عن انعدام الاتصال وزواله (۲) . والجواب : أنه ليس عدم الاتصال مطلقاً، بل عما من شأنه الاتصال وهو المعنى بالقابل الباقي بل هو عدم اتصال إلى اتصالين أي زوال هوية اتصالية، وحدوث هویتین اتصاليتين، فلا بد من أمر قابل للاتصال تارة. وللاتصالين أخرى.
Second: That discontinuity requires a remaining locus only if it were existent, and that is not granted; rather it is the expression for the non-existence of continuity and its removal. And the answer: It is not non-existence of continuity absolutely, but rather of that whose nature is continuity, and that is the meaning of the remaining acceptor; rather it is the non-existence of one continuity into two continuities, i.e., the removal of one continuity-hood and the coming-to-be of two continuity-hoods; thus there must be a thing that accepts continuity at one time and two continuities at another.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and its answer. The objector argues that discontinuity (al-infiṣāl) requires a remaining locus only if it were an existent thing, and that is not granted; rather it is merely the non-existence (ʿadam) of continuity and its removal. The answer clarifies that discontinuity is not non-existence of continuity absolutely (muṭlaqan), but rather non-existence of that whose nature is continuity, which is the remaining acceptor. It is the removal of one continuity-hood and the generation of two continuity-hoods. Therefore, there must be a thing that accepts continuity at one time and two continuities at another.

Key Terms
الانفصال
محل باق
اتصالين
هوية اتصالية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Discontinuity is pure privation or non-existence requiring no subject.
Correction: Discontinuity is not absolute non-existence but a transition from one continuity to two, which demands a persisting acceptor that undergoes the change.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what does discontinuity entail?
A) The absolute non-existence of continuity.
B) The removal of one continuity-hood and generation of two.
C) The annihilation of the body.
D) The existence of a separate locus.

Key Takeaway
Discontinuity is not mere absence but a shift from one continuity to two, requiring a persisting substrate.

The Infinite Regress Objection and Its Refutation
The author refutes the infinite regress argument about matter accepting discontinuity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that matter would entail infinite regress if acceptance of discontinuity required matter, and how the answer distinguishes continuous and non-continuous substrates to avoid the regress.

Core Idea
Only what is continuous in itself needs a substrate; hyle does not, so no regress.

In this passage, the author addresses a potential objection against the concept of hyle as a substrate for change. The critic argues that if every acceptance of discontinuity points to a material substrate, then that substrate itself, being susceptible to discontinuity, would need another substrate, leading to an infinite regress. The author's reply clarifies that only what is inherently continuous requires a distinct substrate; hyle, being neither continuous nor discontinuous in itself, avoids this regress completely.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. The objector claims that if accepting discontinuity requires matter, then matter itself would need more matter infinitely, because it also accepts discontinuity.
This is a classic infinite regress argument.
2
Identify the Key Distinction
The answer says the necessity only applies to what is continuous in itself (muttāṣil bi-dhātihi), like form (ṣūra) and body (jism). Hyle (hayūlā) is not like that. Note that hyle is the subject of continuity and discontinuity, not itself continuous.
This distinction is crucial to block the regress.
3
Grasp the Verification
The verification explains that what is continuous in itself can receive discontinuity, so it needs a thing that is neither continuous nor discontinuous in itself, but receives both. That thing (hyle) remains identical in both states, becoming one by unity and multiple by multiplicity, without needing another thing.
Hyle is the unchanging substrate underlying change.
4
Reflect on the Solution
Consider how this reasoning preserves the concept of matter (mādda) without infinite regress. The substrate does not need a further substrate because it is not inherently continuous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو كان قبول الانفصال محوجاً إلى المادة لاحتاجت المادة إلى مادة أخرى لا إلى نهاية ضرورة قبولها الانفصال. وجوابه : إن المحوج هو قبول الانفصال فيما يكون متصلاً بذاته، كالصورة والجسم وليست الهيولي كذلك، وتحقيقه أن ما يكون متصلاً في ذاته الانفصال، فيفتقر إلى أمر لا يكون متصلاً في ذاته وإلا منفصلاً ، بل يتوارد عليه الاتصال والانفصال وهو هو بعينه في الحالين يصير واحداً متصلاً بعروض الوحدة والاتصال ومتعدداً منفصلاً بعروض الكثرة (۱) والانفصال من غير افتقار إلى ورود أمر آخر.
Third: If the acceptance of discontinuity necessitated matter, then matter would need another matter ad infinitum because it accepts discontinuity. And its answer: That which necessitates is the acceptance of discontinuity in what is continuous in itself (muttāṣil bi-dhātihi), like the form (ṣūra) and the body (jism); hyle (hayūlā) is not like that. Its verification is that what is continuous in itself accepts discontinuity, so it needs a thing that is not continuous in itself nor discontinuous, but rather continuity and discontinuity occur to it; it itself in both states becomes one and continuous by the accident of unity and continuity, and multiple and discontinuous by the accident of multiplicity and discontinuity, without needing the occurrence of another thing.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is the third objection and its answer. The objector argues that if acceptance of discontinuity requires matter, then matter itself would need another matter ad infinitum because it too accepts discontinuity. The answer clarifies that the necessity for a substrate arises only for what is continuous in itself (muttāṣil bi-dhātihi), such as the form (ṣūra) and the body (jism); hyle (hayūlā) is not like that. The verification is that what is continuous in itself can accept discontinuity, so it needs a thing that is neither continuous nor discontinuous in itself, but rather has continuity and discontinuity occur to it. That thing remains identical in both states, becoming one and continuous by the accident of unity, and multiple and discontinuous by the accident of multiplicity, without needing another thing to occur.

Key Terms
قبول الانفصال
مادة
متصل بذاته
هيولي
عروض الكثرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If discontinuity requires a substrate, then that substrate would also require another, leading to infinite regress.
Correction: The author shows that the substrate (hyle) is not continuous in itself, so it does not need a further substrate. The necessity only applies to things continuous in themselves.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what kind of thing requires a substrate for discontinuity?
A) Everything that accepts discontinuity.
B) Only what is continuous in itself.
C) Only matter (hayūlā).
D) Only accidents.

Key Takeaway
The substrate for change (hyle) does not need a further substrate because it is not inherently continuous; it remains identical while receiving continuity or discontinuity.

Answering Objections on the Persistence of Matter
Response to two objections on the persistence of matter and the celestial sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the concept of matter as pure potentiality and responds to two objections.

Core Idea
Matter is pure potentiality; its unity follows form, not itself.

This passage addresses two challenges to the theory of matter (mādda). The first concerns whether the two matters of a separated body continue to exist; the second questions whether the proof for matter applies to the celestial sphere. The author's answers rely on the nature of matter as pure potentiality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice that the author responds to two separate objections.
2
Understand the first objection and answer
The objection claims that after separation, the matters would cease to exist. The answer states they existed with unity, now with multiplicity, and as pure potentiality they need no new matter.
Key concept: matter is pure potentiality, not a substance.
3
Grasp the nature of pure potentiality
Matter is neither one nor multiple in itself; it is pure potentiality that follows the form. This is central to the argument.
4
Examine the second objection
The second objection challenges the proof for matter in the celestial sphere. The author points out that imaginary division does not require an actual recipient, and promises an answer later.
This objection is not fully answered here.
5
Connect to the broader context
These discussions are part of the larger debate about prime matter (hayūlā) and its role in physical bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوابه : أن المادتين كانتا موجودتين لكن بصفة الواحدة لوحدة الاتصال. والآن بصفة التعدد لتعدده ولا يلزم من تعددها بعد الوحدة انعدامها وافتقارها إلى مادة أخرى لما سبق من أنها استعداد محض ليس بمتصل واحد في نفسه، كما ليس بمتعدد، وإنما يفرض له ذلك ببقاء تبعاً للصورة، فمناظر هذا الإشكال وإن أطنب فيه الإمام الرازي راجع إلى الثالث ، وههنا إشكال آخر، وهو أن المطلوب ثبوت المادة لكل جسم، وهذا الدليل لا يتم في الجسم الذي يمتنع عليه الانفصال الانفكاكي، كالفلك . إذ قبول الانقسام الوهمي لا يستدعي قابلاً في الخارج، وسيجيء جوابه في فروع الهيولي.
And its answer: The two matters were existing [before], but with the attribute of unity due to the unity of connection. And now with the attribute of multiplicity due to its multiplicity. And it does not follow from their multiplicity after unity that they cease to exist and need another matter, because of what preceded, that it (matter) is pure potentiality (isti'dad mahd), neither being one continuous thing in itself nor multiple, but rather that is assumed for it by remaining following the form. So the counterpart of this objection, even if Imam al-Razi elaborated on it, goes back to the third [issue]. And here is another objection, namely that what is required is the confirmation of matter for every body, and this proof is not complete for a body that cannot undergo disjoining separation, like the celestial sphere (falak). Because accepting imaginary division does not require a recipient in the external world, and its answer will come in the branches of hyle (furūʿ al-hayūlā).
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection. The objection likely argued that when a body separates, the two matters (the matter of the separated parts) must cease to exist and require new matter. The author responds: the two matters existed previously but in a state of unity due to the unity of the connection. After separation, they exist in a state of multiplicity due to its multiplicity. It does not follow that they cease to exist, because matter is pure potentiality (isti'dad mahd) that is neither actually one nor multiple; its unity or multiplicity is only assumed in accordance with the form. The author then notes that this objection, even if Imam al-Razi elaborated on it, goes back to a third issue. Then he introduces another objection: the proof for matter from divisibility does not work for bodies that cannot undergo disjoining separation, such as the celestial sphere (falak). Accepting imaginary division does not require an external recipient. He promises that the answer will come in the branches of hyle (furūʿ al-hayūlā).

Key Terms
مادة (matter)
استعداد (potentiality)
صورة (form)
انفصال (separation)
اتصال (connection)
فلك (celestial sphere)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Matter is a continuous substance that remains identical through change.
Correction: Matter is pure potentiality (isti'dad mahd) that is neither one nor multiple in itself; its unity or multiplicity follows the form.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the nature of matter (mādda)?
A) It is a continuous substance that remains identical through change.
B) It is pure potentiality (istiʿdād maḥḍ) that is neither one nor multiple in itself.
C) It is the form that determines the body.
D) It is a composite of potentiality and actuality.

Key Takeaway
Matter (mādda) is pure potentiality that does not possess intrinsic unity or multiplicity but follows the form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Matter is described as having no intrinsic identity—its unity or multiplicity comes only from the form it follows. What 'forms' in your life—roles, possessions, relationships—are you treating as if they define who you are, rather than as temporary expressions of your deeper potential? What would it mean to see yourself as pure potential before God, ready to receive whatever form He gives you today?
?
The passage teaches that when a body separates, the matter does not cease to exist but only changes in unity or multiplicity. Think of a time when your life felt 'broken' or 'separated'—a loss, a failure, a change in circumstance. Do you believe that your core self (your 'matter') persisted through that change, or did you feel like you ended and needed a new start? How can trusting in God's continuous creation change how you face such moments?
?
Imam al-Razi raised objections, but the author responds that they go back to a deeper issue. When you face intellectual or spiritual doubts, do you tend to get stuck on surface-level objections, or do you look for the root issue? What is one doubt you have been avoiding that might actually be resolved by understanding a deeper truth about yourself or God? What step will you take today to address it?

The Second Argument Against Hyle: Extension's Single Nature
Second argument against hyle: extension's uniform nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second argument uses the unity of extension to deny hyle as a separate substance.

Core Idea
Extension is a single nature; it cannot be partly substance and partly accident.

The author continues his refutation of hyle by presenting a second argument grounded in the nature of extension. He asserts that extension is uniform in its ontological status, so it cannot be divided into substantial and accidental parts. This challenges the claim that there is a distinct substance underlying bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the author's concise style in stating the argument.
Focus on the key claim that extension is a single nature.
2
Identify the core premise
The argument hinges on the premise that extension is a single nature. Ask yourself what it means for something to be a 'single nature' in this context.
The author implies that a single nature cannot have individuals belonging to different categories (substance vs accident).
3
Trace the conclusion
The author concludes that there is no substance distinct from the body itself. Reflect on how this conclusion follows from the premise.
Think about why denying hyle is the logical outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن الامتداد طبيعة واحدة فيمتنع كون بعض أفراده أو أصنافه جوهراً والبعض عرضاً، وإن وقع الاصطلاح على تسمية بعض الجواهر بذلك. فلا نسلّم أن في المتميز جوهراً غير نفس الجسم .
الثالث : أنه لو كان في الجسم امتدادان أحدهما جوهري، والآخر عرضي، فإن فضل أحدهما على الآخر وقع القدر الذي به التفاضل لا في مادة وهو محال ، إذ لا عرض بدون الموضوع ولا صورة بدون الهيولي.
وبالجملة لا حال بدون المحل، وإن لم يفضل بل تساويا في جميع الأقطار ارتفع الامتياز والأثنينية ، لأن امتياز أفراد الطبيعة الواحدة الحالة إنما يكون بحسب المحل، وهذا مدفوع بأنهما متميزان بالحقيقة مع أن محل العرضي أن محل العرضي هو الجوهري أو الجسم ، ومحل الجوهري هو المادة وإن أريد عدم الامتياز في الحس فلا ضير.
Second: That extension is a single nature, so it is impossible for some of its individuals or types to be substance and some accident, even if terminology has occurred by naming some substances as such. So we do not concede that in the distinct there is a substance other than the body itself.
Third: That if there were in the body two extensions, one substantial and the other accidental, then if one exceeds the other, the amount by which the excess occurs would be without matter, which is impossible, since there is no accident without a subject and no form without hyle.
In summary, there is no state without a substrate; and if they do not exceed but are equal in all dimensions, then distinction and duality are removed, because the distinction of individuals of a single nature that inheres is only by virtue of the substrate. And this is rebutted by [the fact that] they are distinct in reality, while the substrate of the accidental is the substantial or the body, and the substrate of the substantial is matter, and if what is meant is the lack of distinction in sense, then there is no harm.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments against the existence of hyle (matter) as a separate substance underlying bodies. The second argument asserts that extension (imtidad) is a single nature. Since it is one nature, it cannot be that some instances of it are substances and others accidents; therefore, there is no substance distinct from the body itself. The third argument supposes that if a body contained two extensions—one substantial and one accidental—then if one exceeds the other, the excess would occur without any matter, which is impossible because an accident needs a subject and form needs hyle. If they are equal, then no distinction between them remains, since individuals of a single nature that inhere are distinguished only by their substrate. The author then gives a rebuttal: the two extensions are distinct in reality; the substrate of the accidental is the substantial or body, while the substrate of the substantial is matter. If the objection is about sensory indistinguishability, that is not a problem.

Key Terms
امتداد
جوهر
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that extension can be partly substance and partly accident because it has different instances.
Correction: The author insists that extension is a single nature; its instances cannot belong to different ontological categories.

Checkpoint
What does the second argument assert about extension (imtidad)?
A) It is composed of substance and accident.
B) It is a single nature that cannot be partly substance and partly accident.
C) It requires a substrate to exist.
D) It is divisible into substantial and accidental parts.

Key Takeaway
The second argument uses the unity of extension to deny the existence of a separate hyle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own body, do you ever treat it as two separate things: a physical machine and a soul that merely ‘uses’ it? How does the argument that extension is a single nature challenge you to see your body as an undivided sign of Allah’s creation?
?
If extension cannot be partly substance and partly accident, then your actions (accidents) are inseparable from your nature (substance). What does this mean for how you approach your daily habits—are they trivial accidents or expressions of your very being?
?
The author rejects any substance ‘other than the body itself.’ In your faith, do you sometimes imagine that your ‘real self’ is something separate from your body, a hidden substance? How does this argument call you to take full responsibility for your embodied worship?

The Third Argument Against Hyle: Two Extensions in One Body
Third argument with rebuttal against hyle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument's two cases and the rebuttal about the distinction of extensions.

Core Idea
Two extensions in one body lead to absurdity; equality removes distinction, excess lacks substrate.

The third argument considers a scenario where a single body contains two extensions—one substantial and one accidental. The author shows that this assumption leads to contradictions, whether the extensions differ or are equal. He then addresses a potential rebuttal based on the substrates of the extensions.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic and its translation. Notice that the argument has two parts: the consequence of unequal extensions and the consequence of equal extensions, plus a rebuttal.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Analyze the case of excess
If one extension exceeds the other, the excess would be without matter, which is impossible. Understand why every accident needs a subject and every form needs hyle.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Analyze the case of equality
If the two extensions are equal, then they are indistinguishable, so duality disappears. The author traces why equality removes distinction.
This is another impossible outcome for the hypothesis.
4
Examine the rebuttal
The author addresses an objection: the two extensions are distinct in reality because their substrates differ. He accepts this but notes that if the objection is about sensory indistinguishability, it is harmless.
The rebuttal clarifies the argument's intent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن الامتداد طبيعة واحدة فيمتنع كون بعض أفراده أو أصنافه جوهراً والبعض عرضاً، وإن وقع الاصطلاح على تسمية بعض الجواهر بذلك. فلا نسلّم أن في المتميز جوهراً غير نفس الجسم .

Second: That extension is a single nature, so it is impossible for some of its individuals or types to be substance and some accident, even if terminology has occurred by naming some substances as such. So we do not concede that in the distinct there is a substance other than the body itself.

And this is rebutted by [the fact that] they are distinct in reality, while the substrate of the accidental is the substantial or the body, and the substrate of the substantial is matter, and if what is meant is the lack of distinction in sense, then there is no harm.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments against the existence of hyle (matter) as a separate substance underlying bodies. The second argument asserts that extension (imtidad) is a single nature. Since it is one nature, it cannot be that some instances of it are substances and others accidents; therefore, there is no substance distinct from the body itself. The third argument supposes that if a body contained two extensions—one substantial and one accidental—then if one exceeds the other, the excess would occur without any matter, which is impossible because an accident needs a subject and form needs hyle. If they are equal, then no distinction between them remains, since individuals of a single nature that inhere are distinguished only by their substrate. The author then gives a rebuttal: the two extensions are distinct in reality; the substrate of the accidental is the substantial or body, while the substrate of the substantial is matter. If the objection is about sensory indistinguishability, that is not a problem.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if two extensions are equal, they are indistinguishable, so they are the same and duality is false.
Correction: The author agrees they are indistinguishable in that case, but the rebuttal shows that they can be distinguished by their substrates in reality.

Checkpoint
What does the third argument conclude if the two extensions are equal?
A) They are distinct because of different substrates.
B) Distinction and duality are removed.
C) The excess is without matter.
D) The extensions are neither substance nor accident.

The Dilemma of Hyle's Extension
Sanusi presents a dilemma proof against hyle and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemma-based proof against hyle and the key reply that challenges its assumptions.

Core Idea
A dilemma forces a choice between unacceptable options, but the answer reframes the logic.

This passage presents a classic kalām argument against the existence of hyle (prime matter). The argument traps the opponent in a dilemma: if hyle is extended, it becomes like body; if not, it cannot serve as a substrate. The author's reply questions the necessity of these conclusions, showing that the dilemma is not decisive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice how the author structures the proof as a conditional dilemma.
2
Unpack the two branches
Identify the two main options: hyle is either not extended (first branch) or extended (second branch). The second branch splits into independent or dependent extension.
Each branch leads to a problem for the existence of hyle.
3
Examine the answer
See how the author replies: the lack of independent extension does not necessarily mean hyle inheres in body; it could be the substrate. Also, sharing a property (extension) does not make two things identical.
The answer targets the assumption that 'dependent extension = accident'.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument is important: it shows that the concept of hyle is not easily dismissed by spatial considerations. The debate hinges on what it means to be 'extended'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وقد يستدل) على نفي الهيولي بأنها إن لم تتحيز لم تصلح محلاً لما له اختصاص بالحيز، وإن تحيزت فإما بالاستقلال فكان مثل الجسمية، فلم يجامعها، ولم يكن بالمحلية أولى ولزم استغناء الجسمية عن المادة أو تسلسل المواد. وإما بالتبعية، فكانت صفة للجسمية حالة فيها . ويجاب بأن عدم الاستقلال لا يلزم أن يكون بحلولها بل قد يكون بالحلول فيها على أن الاشتراك في اللازم لا يوجب التماثل
He said: And it may be argued for the negation of hyle: that if it is not spatially extended, it is not fit to be a substrate for that which has specificity to space; and if it is extended, then either independently, then it would be like corporeity, so it would not combine with it, nor would it be more entitled to being a substrate, and it would necessitate either the independence of corporeity from matter or a regress of matters. Or dependently, then it would be an attribute of corporeity inhering in it. And it is answered that the lack of independence does not necessarily entail its inherence [in something], but rather it may be by its being that in which it inheres, given that sharing in a concomitant does not necessitate likeness.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents a proof against the existence of hyle (matter) and its answer. The proof is a dilemma: either hyle is spatially extended (mutahayyiz) or not. If it is not extended, it cannot serve as a substrate for bodies that occupy space. If it is extended, then its extension is either independent (like a body) or dependent (like an accident). If independent, hyle would be similar to corporeity (jismiyya), so it could not combine with it, and it would not be more apt to be a substrate; also, corporeity would either be independent of matter or there would be an infinite regress of matters. If dependent, hyle would be an attribute of corporeity. The answer denies that lack of independence implies that hyle inheres in body; rather, it could be the opposite: body's inherence in hyle is possible. Also, sharing a necessary property (like extension) does not imply the two are alike.

Key Terms
تحيز
استقلال
تبعية
حلول
تماثل
جسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is not independently extended, it must be an accident inhering in a substance.
Correction: The author shows that dependent extension could mean being a substrate that conditions the extension of body, not necessarily an accident.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, if hyle is extended independently, what would it be like?
A) An accident.
B) A body (corporeity).
C) A form.
D) A non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The dilemma against hyle fails because dependent extension does not necessarily imply that hyle is an accident; it can be a substrate that conditions body's extension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a logical dilemma that seems to leave no escape, do you immediately accept it as final, or do you pause to examine its assumptions? Where in your life right now is there a situation where you have accepted a false dilemma, and what would it cost you to challenge it?
?
The lesson distinguishes between being an accident and being a substrate that conditions something else. In your relationship with Allah, do you ever reduce your role to that of a passive accident, rather than actively conditioning your environment for good? What would change if you saw yourself as a substrate for righteousness?
?
The principle that sharing a concomitant does not necessitate likeness is subtle but powerful. Think of a person you have judged as similar to someone else based on a single shared trait. How has that rushed judgment affected your treatment of them? This lesson calls you to reconsider: are you willing to repent from that habit and seek true understanding?

Imam al-Razi's Objection Against Hyle
Imam al-Razi's dilemma against hyle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that Imam al-Razi poses against the existence of hyle.

Core Idea
Hyle cannot exist because it must either be extended or not—both lead to contradiction.

Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi raises a powerful objection against the concept of hyle (prime matter). He structures his argument as a dilemma: if hyle exists, it must be either spatially extended or not. In this lesson, you will explore both horns of this dilemma and see why each leads to an impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Then read the translation carefully.
Notice how the objection starts with a conditional statement.
2
Identify the dilemma
The objection presents two possibilities: hyle is either extended (mutahayyiz) or not. Understand why each is considered impossible.
3
Analyze the first horn
If hyle is not extended, it cannot be a substrate for bodies that are essentially extended and directional. This is because inherence requires some form of spatial co-location.
Consider why abstract entities cannot host bodies.
4
Analyze the second horn
If hyle is extended, its extension is either independent (by itself) or dependent (through something else). Both subcases lead to absurdities: independent extension makes hyle and corporeity similars (impossible to combine or have one as substrate), while dependent extension makes hyle an attribute of body—contradicting the intended role of matter.
The argument about similars and infinite regress is subtle; take your time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إشارة إلى معارضة أوردها الإمام تقريرها أنه لو وجدت الهيولي، فإما أن تكون متحيزة أو لا وكلاهما محال . أما الثاني فلامتناع حلول الجسمية المختصة بالحيز والجهة فيما ليس بمتحيز أصلا، ولهذا لا يقع شك في امتناع كون بعض المجردات محلاً للأجسام، وأما الأول فلان تحيزها إما أن يكون بطريق الاستقلال أو التبعية، ويلزم على الأول أن تكون هي والجسمية مثلين لاشتراكهما في أخص صفات النفس أعني التحيز بالذات فيمتنع [ أن يجتمعا ] لاستحالة اجتماع المثلين وأن يختص إحداهما بالمحلية والأخرى بالحالية، لأن حكم الأمثال ،واحد وأن تختص الجسمية بالافتقار إلى المادة، بل يجب إما استغناؤهما فلا تكون الجسمية حالة في مادة أو افتقارهما فيكون للمادة مادة ويتسلسل، ويلزم على الثاني أن تكون المادة صفة للجسمية حالة فيها دون العكس، لأن معنى الحلول التبعية في التحيز ، ولأنه لو جاز العكس لجاز أن يقال الجسم صفة للون حال فيه . والجواب: أن عدم كون تحيزها بالاستقلال لا يستلزم وصفيتها وحلولها، لجواز أن يكون ذلك باعتبار أن حلول الجسمية فيها شرط لتحيزها، ولا نسلم أن كل ما يكون تميزه مشروطاً بشيء كان هو وصفاً لذلك الشيء حالا فيه، بل ربما يكون بالعكس، على أن الاشتراك في التحيز بالاستقلال لا يستلزم تماثلهما
Reference to an objection raised by the Imam, its formulation being that if hyle existed, then either it is spatially extended or not, and both are impossible. As for the second, it is because it is impossible for corporeity, which is specific to space and direction, to inhere in what is not extended at all; for this reason there is no doubt about the impossibility of some abstract entities being substrates for bodies. As for the first, because its extension is either by way of independence or dependence. And it follows from the first that it and corporeity would be two similars, due to their sharing in the most specific attribute of the self, namely extension by essence; so it is impossible that they combine, due to the impossibility of two similars combining, and that one of them be specifically the substrate and the other the inherence, because the judgment of similars is one, and that corporeity be specifically in need of matter; rather, either both are independent, so corporeity would not be inhering in matter, or both are in need, so matter would have matter, leading to infinite regress. And it follows from the second that matter would be an attribute of corporeity inhering in it and not the opposite, because the meaning of inherence is dependence in extension, and because if the opposite were permissible, it would be permissible to say that body is an attribute of color inhering in it. And the answer: that its not being independently extended does not entail its being an attribute and inhering, because it is possible that that is due to the fact that the inherence of corporeity in it is a condition for its extension; and we do not concede that everything whose distinction is conditioned by something is an attribute of that thing inhering in it; rather, perhaps it is the opposite, given that sharing in independent extension does not necessitate their being similar.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed objection (mu'arada) raised by Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi against the existence of hyle, followed by a refutation. The objection restates the dilemma: if hyle exists, it is either extended or not. If not extended, it cannot be a substrate for bodies which have direction and space. If extended, then either independently or dependently. If independently, hyle and corporeity (jismiyya) would be two similar substances (mithlayn) because both are essentially extended; two similars cannot combine, nor can one be substrate and the other inhering since similars have the same ruling; also, either both are independent (so corporeity does not need matter) or both need a substrate (leading to infinite regress). If dependently, hyle would be an attribute of corporeity, not the reverse, because inherence means dependence in extension, and if it were reversed, one could absurdly say body is an attribute of color. The answer distinguishes: lack of independent extension does not mean hyle is an attribute; body's inherence in hyle could be a condition for its extension. Not everything conditioned by something is an attribute of it; it could be the opposite. Also, sharing independent extension does not imply likeness—the objection falls.

Key Terms
هيولي
متحيز
تحيز
حلول
جسمية
المثلين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the objection, if hyle is extended independently, what problem arises?
A) Hyle would be an accident of body.
B) Hyle and corporeity would be two similars, leading to impossibility of combination.
C) Hyle would not be a substrate.
D) Hyle would be non-extended.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi's objection shows that the concept of hyle leads to logical contradictions regardless of whether it is extended or not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument uses logical dilemmas to disprove a concept. When you hold a belief, do you actively seek out logical contradictions within it, or do you avoid testing it? What belief might you need to subject to such scrutiny today?
?
Imam al-Razi rejects hyle because it leads to contradictions. Are there aspects of your own understanding of the physical world that rely on assumptions that you have never questioned? What would it cost you to examine them honestly?
?
The impossibility of two similars combining is used here. In your life, do you ever try to hold two contradictory positions simultaneously (e.g., wanting both comfort and spiritual growth)? How can this logical principle help you resolve internal conflicts?

Refutation of the Objection: The Author's Answer
The author's refutation of the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes Imam al-Razi's objection by distinguishing between independence and conditionality.

Core Idea
Lack of independent extension does not make hyle an attribute; body's inherence may condition hyle's extension.

The author now responds to Imam al-Razi's powerful objection. He does not reject the logical form but instead challenges the assumption that non-independent extension implies attribute status. In this lesson, you will see the subtle reply that preserves the possibility of hyle.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Focus on the key claim: 'لا يستلزم وصفيتها وحلولها'.
The answer begins with 'والجواب'.
2
Understand the main point
The author argues that hyle's lack of independent extension does not automatically make it an attribute or accident. Instead, the inherence of corporeity in hyle could be a condition (shart) for hyle's extension.
Conditionality is different from attribute-inherence.
3
Address the similars argument
The author also counters the claim that sharing independent extension makes hyle and corporeity similars. He states that sharing independent extension does not necessitate likeness (tamathul).
This undercuts the earlier reasoning about 'two similars'.
4
Synthesize the refutation
Combine the two points: the conditionality argument and the denial of necessary likeness. This preserves the possibility of hyle as a substrate for corporeity.
The refutation is not a rejection of logic but a re-interpretation of the relationship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

?

No. How does this truth settle your heart that you are not a mere accident but a real, substantial being sustained by His condition? What changes in your prayer tonight when you truly internalise that?

Classification of Substance According to the Philosophers
Al-Taftazani reports the philosophers' division of substance into extended and non-extended types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' classification of substance (jawhar) into extended and non-extended types, and the three subtypes of extended substances.

Core Idea
Substance is not just physical bodies; philosophers also recognized non-extended substances.

Al-Taftazani presents the philosophical classification of substance (jawhar), which differs from the common notion that all substances are bodies. This passage introduces two major categories and then a finer subdivision of extended substances, based on the works of earlier philosophers like the author of al-Muhakamat.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage first gives a broad division, then a more detailed one.
2
Identify the two main types of substance
Find the statement that distinguishes extended (mutahayyiz bi al-dhāt) and non-extended substances (al-jawāhir al-mujarrada). Understand that the latter are incorporeal.
3
Examine the three divisions of the extended
Read the quote from al-Muhākamāt. Identify the three kinds: independently extended, dependently extended by inherence in another, and dependently extended by another inhering in it. Note the examples given for each.
The terms istiqlāl (independence) and tabi'iyya (dependence) are key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند الحكماء قد يكون الجوهر متحيزاً بالذات وقد لا يكون متحيزاً أصلا كالجواهر المجردة هكذا يستفاد مما ذكر في شرح المواقف في مقدمة الأمور العامة ومبحث الجوهر والعرض قال صاحب المحاكمات المتحيز ثلاثة أقسام إما ان يكون متحيزا بالاستقلال كالصورة والجسم وإما أن يكون متحيزاً بالتبعية إما على سبيل حلوله في الغير كالأعراض أو على سبيل حلول الغير فيه كالهبوط فإنه متميز بشرط حلول الصورة فيها (راجع كشاف اصطلاحات الفنون ج ٢ ص ٤٢)
According to the philosophers, substance (jawhar) may be essentially extended (mutahayyiz bi al-dhāt) and may not be extended at all, like the incorporeal substances (al-jawāhir al-mujarrada). Thus it is derived from what was mentioned in Sharh al-Mawāqif in the introduction to general matters and the section on substance and accident. The author of al-Muhākamāt said: The extended (al-mutahayyiz) is three divisions: either it is extended independently (bi al-istiqlāl) like the form (al-ṣūra) and the body (al-jism), or it is extended dependently (bi al-tabi'iyya), either by way of its inherence (ḥulūl) in another, like the accidents (al-a'rāḍ), or by way of another inhering in it, like al-habūṭ (the descent/place), for it is distinguished on condition that the form inheres in it. (Refer to Kashshāf Iṣṭilāḥāt al-Funūn, vol. 2, p. 42).
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' classification of substance (jawhar). It first distinguishes between two broad types: substances that are inherently extended (mutahayyiz bi al-dhāt), such as bodies, and substances that are completely non-extended, like the incorporeal substances (al-jawāhir al-mujarrada). The passage then cites a more detailed division of the extended (al-mutahayyiz) from the author of al-Muhākamāt, who divides it into three kinds: (1) independently extended, like the form and the body; (2) dependently extended by way of inherence in another, like accidents; (3) dependently extended by way of another inhering in it, like the place (al-habūṭ), which is distinguished by the inherence of form in it. This classification helps in understanding the ontological categories used in kalām.

Key Terms
جوهر
متحيز
الجواهر المجردة
المتحيز
الاستقلال
التبعية
حلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All substances are extended (physical bodies).
Correction: The philosophers explicitly state that some substances are not extended at all, like incorporeal substances (al-jawāhir al-mujarrada).

Checkpoint
According to the author of al-Muhākamāt, into how many divisions is the extended (al-mutahayyiz) classified?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
The philosophers defined substance as including both extended bodies and non-extended entities, and they further divided extended substances into three categories based on independence and inherence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage mentions 'incorporeal substances' (al-jawāhir al-mujarrada) as non-extended realities. Have you ever seriously considered that you possess a soul—an incorporeal substance—that is distinct from your physical body? How does recognizing that your true substance may be non-extended challenge the way you prioritize your life? Commit to one concrete action this week that treats your soul as more important than your body.
?
The classification shows that some things are 'dependently extended,' relying on others for their existence. Your body depends on countless factors, but your soul depends ultimately on Allah. In moments of distraction or pride, you forget this dependence. Will you today remember that you are not independent, and make a silent dhikr thanking Allah for sustaining your existence every moment?

The Fourth Investigation: Disagreements on the Indivisible Atom
Opens the fourth investigation and summarizes disagreements on the atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main points of disagreement among kalam scholars regarding the nature of the indivisible atom (jawhar fard).

Core Idea
The atom's properties—shape, size, and capacity for life—were all debated by classical theologians.

This passage opens the fourth investigation of al-Maqasid, focusing on the various doctrinal branches concerning the indivisible atom. Al-Sanusi lists the key areas of disagreement: whether the atom can receive life, whether it can lie on a continuous line, whether a line of atoms can form a circle, and whether it has a shape. He then summarizes the different opinions on its shape and notes a widespread agreement on its lack of length and width. This lesson will guide you through these debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
2
Identify the four points of disagreement
The passage lists four specific disagreements about the atom. Find each one: life reception, placement on a line, forming a circle, and shape.
Notice that these are all properties that seem contradictory to the atom's indivisibility.
3
Examine the opinions on shape
Scholars who affirmed shape disagreed on what shape it is: sphere, triangle, or square/cube. Note the reasoning for cube: being surrounded by six atoms.
4
Understand the agreement on no length/width
The passage states a general agreement that the atom has no length or width, with exceptions attributed to al-Salihi and Ibn al-Rawandi. Then it clarifies a transmitted agreement about a 'line of area' based on interpretation.
This shows how subtle distinctions can lead to apparent contradictions.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these debates matter for kalam theology. They attempt to define the fundamental building block of the physical world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع في تفاريع المذاهب. أما القائلون بالجزء فقد اختلفوا في أنه هل يقبل الحياة والأعراض المشروطة بها، وفي أنه هل يمكن وقوع الجزء على متصل الجزءين، وفي أنه هل يمكن جعل الخط المؤلف من الأجزاء دائرة، وفي أنه هل له شكل، واختلف المثبتون فقيل شبه الكرة، وقيل المثلث، وقيل المربع أي المكعب، ليكن كونه محفوفاً بجواهر ستة، واتفقوا على أنه لاحظ له في الطول والعرض، إلا ما نسب إلى الصالحي وابن الراوندي. أما ما نقل من الاتفاق على أن له خطاً من المساحة فمبني على أنها اسم للتحيز والجرم الموجب للتكاثف بانضمام الأمثال على أن المنقول عن الجبائي خلافه.
The fourth investigation: On the branches of doctrines. As for those who affirm the atom, they have disagreed: whether it accepts life and the accidents conditioned by it; whether an atom can fall on a continuous line of two parts; whether a line composed of atoms can be made into a circle; and whether it has a shape. Those who affirm shape differed: some said it is like a sphere, some said a triangle, some said a square, i.e., a cube, because it is surrounded by six atoms. And they agreed that it has no share of length and width, except what is attributed to al-Salihi and Ibn al-Rawandi. As for what is transmitted of agreement that it has a line of area, it is based on [the claim] that it is a name for occupying space and for the body that necessitates compactness by the joining of similars, although what is transmitted from al-Jubba'i is the opposite.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage opens the fourth investigation on the ramifications of the doctrines concerning the indivisible atom. It lists the main points of disagreement: whether the atom can receive life and its conditional accidents, whether it can lie on a continuous line, whether a line of atoms can form a circle, and whether it has a shape. Then it mentions the different opinions on its shape (sphere, triangle, square/cube) and notes the agreement that it has no length or width, except for a reported exception. It ends with a note about the transmitted agreement on the atom having a line of area, clarifying that this is based on a certain interpretation regarding occupancy and bulk, although al-Jubba'i disagreed.

Key Takeaway
The fourth investigation catalogues four key debates about the indivisible atom: its capacity for life, its placement, its ability to form a circle, and its shape, with agreement that it lacks length and width except for a few exceptions.

This lesson examines a specific branch of the kalam doctrine of atomism: whether the indivisible atom (jawhar fard) can receive life and the accidents conditioned by it, such as knowledge and will. The passage presents the positions of al-Ash'ari and early Mu'tazila who affirmed it, and later Mu'tazila who denied it. Atom on a Continuous Line
Al-Ash'ari's denial and the opposing allowance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational disagreement over whether an atom can be placed on a continuous line connecting two atoms, including why al-Ash'ari rejected it.

Core Idea
Placing an atom on a continuous line would imply the atom is divisible.

In the atomist framework, atomic indivisibility is fundamental. When al-Ash'ari considered the possibility of an atom resting on a line that connects two other atoms, he saw a logical problem: the atom would have to occupy part of the line, implying it has parts. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and the opposing view of Abu Hashim and al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the disagreement is framed as a conditional: 'whether it is possible...'
The passage uses the term 'جزء' for atom.
2
Understand al-Ash'ari's objection
Al-Ash'ari denied the possibility because it would necessitate division (انقسام). Ask: division of what? Of the atom or of the line? The commentary clarifies it entails division of the atom itself.
This is key to the atomist debate.
3
Compare the opposing view
Abu Hashim and al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar allowed it. Consider what assumption might allow this without violating indivisibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها اختلافهم في أنه هل يمكن وقوع جزء على متصل الجزئين، فأنكره الأشعري لاستلزامه الانقسام، وجوزه أبو هاشم والقاضي عبد الجبار. ومنها اختلافهم في أنه هل يمكن جعل الخط المؤلف من الأجزاء دائرة، فأنكره الأشعري وجوزه إمام الحرمين. وقد سبق بيانهما.
And among them is their disagreement over whether an atom can fall on a continuous line of two atoms: al-Ash'ari denied it because it entails division, while Abu Hashim and al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar allowed it. And among them is their disagreement over whether a line composed of atoms can be made into a circle: al-Ash'ari denied it, while Imam al-Haramayn allowed it. The explanation of both has preceded.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents two related disagreements. The first: can an atom be placed on a continuous line that connects two other atoms? Al-Ash'ari denies it because it would cause division of the continuous line, implying the atom would itself be divisible; but Abu Hashim and al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar allow it. The second: can a straight line made of atoms be curved into a circle? Al-Ash'ari again denies, while Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) allows. The author notes these discussions have appeared earlier in the text.

أبو هاشم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The debate is about physical geometry or empirical observation.
Correction: It is a logical argument within the atomist model about whether the concept of an atom falling on a continuous line is coherent, not about actual physical placement.

Checkpoint

A) Because it would make the line divisible
B) Because it would entail the division of the atom itself
C) Because he believed atoms cannot touch

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari argued that placing an atom on a continuous line would require the atom to have parts, contradicting its indivisibility.
