Line of Atoms Forming a Circle
Al-Ash'ari's denial and Imam al-Haramayn's allowance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement over whether a line composed of atoms can be curved into a circle, and why al-Ash'ari denied it while Imam al-Haramayn allowed it.

Core Idea
Curving a straight line of atoms into a circle is logically possible even if atoms are indivisible.

The second debate asks whether a straight line built from atoms can be reshaped into a circle. Al-Ash'ari again denies, perhaps because he sees circularity as requiring a continuous curve that atoms cannot form. Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) permits it, likely because he views the circle as merely an arrangement of the same atoms. This lesson examines both positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

Analyze al-Ash'ari's denial

The passage does not give his reason explicitly.
3
Consider Imam al-Haramayn's allowance
He allowed it. Think about how a set of discrete atoms might be arranged in a circular pattern without breaking atomic indivisibility.
4
Connect to the earlier discussion
The author says this has been explained before. Reflect on how the atomist model handles geometric transformations.

Does the Atom Have a Shape? – Proof and Refutation
Debates whether the atom has shape and what shape it resembles, with a proof and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different positions on whether the indivisible atom has a shape, the arguments for its shape, and the refutation distinguishing senses of finitude.

Core Idea
The atom's shape debate hinges on what 'finite' means.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid explores one of the subtle questions of kalam: does the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) have a shape? The author reports the positions of al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila, then notes that the real disagreement is not over the existence of shape but over which shape it resembles. A proof from finitude is offered and then refuted, clarifying the concept of a boundary. Understanding this debate sharpens one's grasp of how kalam thinks about the fundamental constituents of matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic and its literal English translation. Notice the technical terms for shapes and the logical flow of the argument.
Key terms: كرة (sphere), مثلث (triangle), مربع (square), مكعب (cube), متناه (finite), نهاية (end/boundary).
2
Identify the reported opinions
The passage lists who denied shape (al-Ash'ari) and who affirmed it (most Mu'tazila). Then it notes that the real disagreement is about which shape the atom resembles. Note the four candidates: sphere, triangle, square/cube, and the majority view favoring square/cube.
The author also mentions al-Amidi's report of a consensus on negation, but clarifies that this was about shape entailing division.
3
Understand the proof from finitude
The proof argues that since the atom is finite, it must have a boundary that encloses it. If that boundary is one, it is a sphere; if multiple, it is polygonal. This seems to force a shape on the atom.
This proof is presented as a possible argument, not necessarily the author's own view.
4
Grasp the refutation
The refutation distinguishes two meanings of 'finite'. If it means 'not extending infinitely', that is granted but does not imply a distinct boundary. If it means 'enclosed by a boundary', that is denied because the atom itself is the endpoint—it is the boundary, not something bounded.
This subtle point shows that the atom can be considered the ultimate part without requiring an external shape.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها اختلافهم في أن الجوهر الفرد هل له شكل؟ فأنكره الأشعري، وأثبته أكثر المعتزلة، كذا ذكره الإمام. ونقل الأمدي اتفاق الكل على نفيه لاقتضائه محيطاً ومحاطاً فينقسم، وإنما الخلاف في أنه هل يشبه شيئاً من الأشكال؟ فقال القاضي: لا، وقال غيره: نعم. ثم اختلفوا فقيل يشبه الكرة لأن في المضلع اختلاف جوانب، وقيل المثلث لأنه أبسط الأشكال المضلعة، وقيل المربع لأنه الذي يمكن تركب الجسم منه بلا فرج، وهذا قول الأكثرين. قال الإمام: والحق أنهم شبهوه بالمكعب لأنهم أثبتوا له جوانب ستة، وزعموا أنه يمكن أن يتصل به جواهر ستة جوانب ستة، وإنما يكون ذلك في المكعب. وقد يستدل على من وجوب الشكل له بأنه متناه ضرورة فتكون له نهاية وحد يحيط به، إما واحد فيكون كرة، أو أكثر فيكون مضلعاً. ويجاب بأنه إن أريد بكونه متناهياً أنه لا يمتد إلى غير نهاية فمسلم، ولا يلزم إحاطة حديه به مغاير للمحاط، وإن أريد أنه يحيط به نهاية وينتهي إلى جزء لا جزء وراءه فممنوع، بل هو نفس النهاية. أعني الجزء الذي إليه ينتهي كل متناه.
And among them is their disagreement over whether the indivisible atom has a shape. Al-Ash'ari denied it, while most of the Mu'tazila affirmed it, as the Imam mentioned. Al-Amidi reported the agreement of all on its negation because it would necessitate a surrounding and a surrounded, thus division. Rather, the disagreement is whether it resembles any shape. Al-Qadi said no, others said yes. Then they differed: some said it resembles a sphere because a polygon has varying sides; some said a triangle because it is the simplest polygonal shape; some said a square because it allows the composition of a body without gaps, and this is the view of the majority. The Imam said: The truth is that they likened it to a cube because they affirmed for it six sides and claimed that six atoms can be attached to its six sides, and that is only possible with a cube. One may argue for the necessity of shape for it on the grounds that it is finite necessarily, so it has an end or boundary that surrounds it. If that boundary is one, it is a sphere; if more, it is polygonal. The response is that if by 'finite' is meant that it does not extend indefinitely, then that is granted, but it does not entail that its boundaries surround it as something distinct from the surrounded. If it is meant that a boundary surrounds it and it ends at a part beyond which there is no part, then it is denied; rather, it is itself the boundary, meaning the part to which every finite thing ends.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage addresses the fourth branch: whether the indivisible atom has a shape. It first reports that al-Ash'ari denied shape, while most Mu'tazila affirmed it. Al-Amidi's report of a consensus on negation is clarified as actually being about the impossibility of shape due to the need for a boundary causing division. The real disagreement, the author says, is about which shape the atom resembles. The views are listed: sphere (because polygonic sides vary), triangle (simplest polygon), and square/cube (allows body composition without gaps), the last being the majority view. The Imam (likely al-Razi) states the truth is the cube because they gave the atom six sides to attach to six neighboring atoms. Then a proof for shape is presented: the atom is finite, so it must have a boundary that encloses it, which either one (sphere) or many (polygon). The refutation distinguishes two senses of finitude: if it means not infinite, that is granted but does not imply a distinct boundary; if it means a boundary encloses it, that is denied because the atom itself is the boundary at which finitude ends.

Key Terms
شكل
كرة
مثلث
مربع
مكعب
متناه
نهاية
محاط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The atom must have a shape because it is finite.
Correction: The finitude of the atom does not entail a shape; the atom itself can be the endpoint, not something enclosed by a boundary.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the majority opinion on the shape of the indivisible atom?
A) Sphere
B) Triangle
C) Square/cube
D) No shape at all

Key Takeaway
The indivisible atom, if finite, does not necessarily have a shape distinct from itself; it is the endpoint of division, making shape an attribute that requires further premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This debate dissects what it means for something to be finite. In your own spiritual life, do you ever confuse 'finitude' with 'limitation' in a negative sense, when in fact being finite before Allah is a sign of your need for Him? How can you today turn the awareness of your own 'ending' into gratitude rather than anxiety?
?
The refutation shows that careful definition prevents false conclusions. Where in your practice of Islam have you assumed a meaning of a term (like 'tawakkul' or 'sabr') that led you to misunderstand your duty? What would it look like to recover the precise meaning from revelation?
?
The atom is argued to be its own endpoint, not needing an external boundary to define it. Reflect on your identity: do you rely on external markers (wealth, status, others' approval) to define who you are, or can you rest in the fact that your ultimate worth is determined by your relationship with Allah—a self-sufficient definition? What concrete step will you take today to affirm that identity?

The Indivisible Atom: Dimensions and the Line of Area
Discusses the consensus on the atom having no length/width and the debate on its area line.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalām consensus that the atom has no length or width, the dismissal of a contrary view attributed to al-Salihi, and the ambiguous concept of a 'line of area' for the atom.

Core Idea
The atom has no length or width, or else it would be divisible; its 'line of area' is a debated term.

In classical kalām, the indivisible atom (al-juz' alladhī lā yatajazzā) is a fundamental building block of matter. Theologians agreed that the atom itself has no dimensions like length or width, because if it did, it could be divided further. This lesson examines that consensus, a dissenting attribution to al-Salihi, and a later debate about whether the atom has a 'line of area' (khaṭṭ min al-misāḥa). The passage clarifies what is agreed upon and what remains ambiguous.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully once, then read the translation. Notice the structure: consensus, dismissal, reported view, then al-Amidi's report.
The Arabic uses technical terms like 'طول' and 'عرض'.
2
Identify the key claims
List the main points: (a) atom has no length/width, (b) denial of contrary attribution is ignorance, (c) al-Salihi's actual view, (d) consensus on line of area, (e) ambiguity of interpretation.
Focus on what is agreed and what is disputed.
3
Understand the conceptual issue
Why is the line of area problematic? If the atom has no length/width, how can it have a line? The passage notes that interpreting it as volume does not clarify.
Consider how this relates to the indivisibility of the atom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنهم اتفقوا على أنه لاحظ له من الطول والعرض بمعنى أنه لا يتصف بشيء من ذلك، وإلا لكان منقسماً ضرورة، وإنكار ذلك على ما نسب إلى أبي الحسين الصالحي من قدماء المعتزلة جهالة. والمحكى في كلام المعتزلة عن الصالحي أنه كان يقول: الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ جسم لا طول له ولا عرض ولا عمق وليس بذي نصف، وأن الجسم ما احتمل الأعراض. ونقل الآمدي اتفاق الكل على أن للجزء خطاً من المساحة، وحمله على أن له حجماً ما على ما في المواقف لا يزيل الاشتباه.
And among them is that they agreed that it has no share of length and width, meaning it is not described by any of that, otherwise it would necessarily be divisible. Denial of that, as attributed to Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi of the early Mu'tazila, is ignorance. What is reported in the speech of the Mu'tazila from al-Salihi is that he used to say: the indivisible atom is a body with no length, width, or depth, and it is not half, and that body is what receives accidents. Al-Amidi reported agreement of all that the atom has a line of area, but interpreting that as meaning it has some volume, as in al-Mawaqif, does not remove the confusion.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage discusses the consensus that the atom has no share of length or width, meaning it does not possess these dimensions, because if it did, it would be divisible. It dismisses the attribution to al-Salihi of the early Mu'tazila who allegedly denied this, calling it ignorance. Al-Salihi's actual view, as reported by the Mu'tazila, is that the indivisible atom is a body but without length, width, or depth, and it is not half; body is defined as that which receives accidents. The passage then addresses al-Amidi's report of a consensus that the atom has a 'line of area' (khatt min al-misaha). Interpreting this line as meaning it has some volume (hajm), as done in al-Mawaqif, does not resolve the ambiguity.

Key Terms
طول
عرض
عمق
مساحة
خط
حجم
الصالحي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The atom has a physical line of area like a drawn line.
Correction: The 'line of area' is a technical term for minimal spatial extension, but the author says its interpretation remains ambiguous.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the atom had length or width according to the passage?
A) It would be invisible
B) It would be divisible
C) It would become a line
D) It would become a cube

Key Takeaway
The kalām consensus holds that the indivisible atom has no length or width, but the concept of a 'line of area' remains debated and ambiguous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author dismisses a view attributed to al-Salihi as 'ignorance,' yet later shows al-Salihi's actual statement is nuanced. Is there a theological position you have dismissed without fully understanding its reasoning? Will you commit to investigating such positions more deeply before passing judgment?
?
The passage notes that interpreting the 'line of area' as volume does not remove confusion. Are there theological terms you use (e.g., 'mercy,' 'justice') that remain conceptually confusing to you? What would it look like to patiently seek clarity rather than accepting an easy but ambiguous explanation?

The Disagreement on Whether the Atom Has a Shape
Sanusi presents the positions and consensus on shape existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial disagreement between al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila on whether the atom has a shape, and the reported consensus that it does not.

Core Idea
The atom's shape was denied by al-Ash'ari but affirmed by most Mu'tazila, with a consensus that shape implies divisibility.

This lesson introduces the central dispute over the atom's shape. Al-Ash'ari denied it, while most Mu'tazila affirmed it. Al-Amidi later reported a consensus against shape because it would imply a surrounding and a surrounded, leading to divisibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the positions
Read the Arabic passage stating al-Ash'ari's denial and the Mu'tazila's affirmation. Notice the mention of al-Imam and al-Amidi.
Al-Imam is likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi.
2
Understand the consensus argument
The passage says al-Amidi reported consensus on negation because shape would necessitate a surrounding and a surrounded, making the atom divisible. Think about why that is problematic.
Divisibility contradicts the atom's indivisibility.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: If the atom had a shape, would it necessarily be divisible? This is the key logical step in the consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها اختلافهم في أن الجوهر الفرد هل له شكل؟ فأنكره الأشعري، وأثبته أكثر المعتزلة، كذا ذكره الإمام، ونقل الأمدي اتفاق الكل على نفيه لاقتضائه محيطاً أو محاطاً فينقسم، وإنما الخلاف في أنه هل يشبه شيئاً من الأشكال؟ فقال القاضي: لا وقال غيره : نعم، ثم اختلفوا فقيل يشبه الكرة، لأن في المضلع اختلاف جوانب ، وقيل : المثلث لأنه أبسط الأشكال المضلعة ، وقيل : المربع لأنه الذي يمكن تركب الجسم منه بلا فرج، وهذا قول الأكثرين. قال الإمام والحق أنهم شبهوه بالمكعب، لأنهم أثبتوا له جوانب ستة ، وزعموا أنه يمكن أن يتصل به جواهر ستة جوانب ستة ، وإنما يكون ذلك في المكعب، وقد يستدل على من وجوب الشكل له بأنه متناه ضرورة فتكون له نهاية وحد يحيط به، إما واحد فيكون كرة، أو أكثر فيكون مضلعاً ويجاب بأنه إن أريد بكونه متناهياً أنه لا يمتد إلى غير نهاية فمسلم ، ولا يلزم إحاطة حديه به مغاير للمحاط، وإن أريد أنه يحيط به نهاية، وينتهي إلى جزء لا جزء وراءه فممنوع، بل هو نفس النهاية. أعني الجزء الذي إليه ينتهي كل متناهي.
Among them is their disagreement on whether the individual atom has a shape. Al-Ash'ari denied it, while most of the Mu'tazila affirmed it, as al-Imam mentioned. Al-Amidi reported the consensus of all on its negation because it would necessitate a surrounding and a surrounded, thus it would be divisible. The disagreement is only on whether it resembles something from the shapes. Al-Qadi said no, and others said yes. Then they differed: some said it resembles a sphere because in a polygon the sides differ; some said a triangle because it is the simplest of polygonal shapes; some said a square because it is that from which the body can be composed without gaps, and this is the opinion of the majority. Al-Imam said: the truth is that they likened it to a cube because they affirmed for it six sides and claimed that six atoms could attach to it from six sides, and that is only possible in a cube. One might argue for the necessity of shape for it by saying that it is finite necessarily, so it has a limit and a boundary encompassing it: either one, so it is a sphere; or more, so it is polygonal. The answer is: if by its being finite is meant that it does not extend infinitely, then it is accepted, but that does not necessitate that its boundary encompasses it as distinct from the encompassed; and if it is meant that a boundary encompasses it and it ends at a part beyond which there is no part, then it is denied; rather, it is the boundary itself, meaning the part to which every finite thing ends.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the disagreement on whether the atom (jawhar fard) has a shape. It begins by stating the positions: al-Ash'ari denied shape, while most Mu'tazila affirmed it. Al-Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) reports this, and al-Amidi adds that there is consensus against shape because it would imply a surrounding and a surrounded, making the atom divisible. The real disagreement is over which shape it resembles. Al-Qadi (Abd al-Jabbar) denied any resemblance; others affirmed. Then various opinions are listed: some say sphere (for simplicity), some triangle (simplest polygon), some square (enables composition without gaps). Al-Imam says the truth is that they likened it to a cube because they claimed six sides for contact. Then a proof for shape is presented: the atom must have a limit, so a single limit implies sphere, multiple limits imply polygon. The answer distinguishes two senses of 'finite': if it means not infinite, that is accepted but does not necessitate a distinct boundary; if it means being enclosed by a boundary, that is denied because the atom itself is the boundary—the part where everything finite ends.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد
شكل
محيط
محاط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila agreed on the shape issue.
Correction: They disagreed: al-Ash'ari denied shape, while most Mu'tazila affirmed it.

Checkpoint
Who denied that the atom has a shape?
A) Al-Ash'ari
B) Most Mu'tazila
C) Al-Amidi
D) Al-Imam

Key Takeaway
The initial dispute on the atom's shape involves al-Ash'ari's denial versus the Mu'tazila's affirmation, with a reported consensus that shape would compromise indivisibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological disagreement like this one, do you immediately side with one scholar without understanding the rational argument? How can you train yourself to evaluate arguments before accepting positions?
?
Al-Amidi's reported consensus relies on a logical deduction. In your own life, do you accept claims based on authority or do you seek to understand the reasoning? Are you willing to change your view when you see a logical contradiction?
?
This passage discusses the nature of atoms – something Allah has hidden from our direct perception. How does accepting that some realities are beyond our sensory grasp affect your reliance on reason in matters of faith?

Disagreement on Resemblance to Specific Shapes
Sanusi discusses the dispute on which shape the atom resembles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the differing opinions on which shape the atom resembles, including sphere, triangle, square, and cube.

Core Idea
Theologians debated whether the atom resembles a sphere, triangle, square, or cube.

After establishing that the shape issue is more about resemblance than existence, this lesson explores the various opinions: some said sphere (for simplicity), some triangle (simplest polygon), some square (allows composition without gaps), and al-Imam favored the cube due to the atom's six sides.

Guided Path
1
Identify the different shapes proposed
Read the passage and list the four shapes mentioned: sphere, triangle, square, cube. Notice the reasoning for each.
The cube is presented as the truth according to al-Imam.
2
Understand the reasoning for each shape
For sphere: because polygon sides differ. For triangle: simplest polygon. For square: allows composition without gaps. For cube: because atom has six sides for contact.
The majority opinion was square.
3
Reflect on why shape resemblance matters
Consider that this debate is about the atom's geometric properties, which affect how atoms combine to form bodies.

The Proof for Shape Necessity and Its Refutation
Sanusi presents a proof for shape necessity and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the atom must have a shape because it is finite, and the counter-argument that distinguishes different meanings of finitude.

Core Idea
The atom's finitude does not necessarily imply a distinct surrounding boundary.

This lesson presents a proof that the atom must have a shape: since it is finite, it has a limit, which must be either one (sphere) or multiple (polygon). The answer refutes this by distinguishing whether 'finite' means 'not infinite' (accepted but no distinct boundary) or 'enclosed by a boundary' (denied, as the atom itself is the endpoint).

Guided Path
1
Read the proof for shape
The proof argues: the atom is finite, so it has a limit; if the limit is one, it's a sphere; if more, it's a polygon.
This seems logical but is countered.
2
Understand the two senses of 'finite'
The answer distinguishes: if 'finite' means 'does not extend infinitely', that is accepted but does not imply a boundary distinct from the atom. If it means 'enclosed by a boundary', that is denied.
The atom itself is the boundary—the part where everything finite ends.
3
Reflect on the implication
Think about why this distinction is important. It preserves the atom's indivisibility while acknowledging its finitude.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Atom's Lack of Length, Width, and Dispute over Area
Sanusi explains the consensus on no length/width and the disagreement on area.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus among theologians that the atom has no length or width, and the nuanced disagreement about whether it possesses area (misaha) based on differing definitions of area as composition (ta'lif) or spatial occupation (tahayyuz).

Core Idea
The atom's dimensions are defined not by measurement but by logical necessity and theological consensus.

Sanusi delves into the intricate discussions surrounding the nature of the atom (al-jawhar al-fard). While there is widespread agreement that the atom cannot be described by length or width, as that would imply divisibility, a lively debate emerges over the concept of 'area' (misaha). This passage navigates the positions of key Mu'tazili thinkers, revealing how definitions of fundamental terms lead to differing conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note the technical terms such as طول, عرض, مساحة, تأليف, تحيز, جرم, تكاثف.
The passage is dense with multiple positions; read slowly.
2
Identify the consensus
The first part states that all agreed the atom has no share of length or width, otherwise it would be divisible. This is a logical argument.
This consensus is foundational for the rest of the discussion.
3
Examine the dispute over area
Focus on al-Amidi's report about a consensus on 'line of area' and its interpretation. Then note the divergence among Mu'tazila: Ibn al-Rawandi attributes length, while Abu Ali and Abu Hashim agree on no length but differ on area.
The key difference is whether area is defined as composition (ta'lif) or spatial occupation (tahayyuz/jirm).
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how the definition of area affects the atom's nature. For Abu Hashim, area is inherent to the atom's occupation of space; for Abu Ali, area is relational through composition.
This shows how theological physics depends on conceptual definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنهم اتفقوا على أنه لاحظ له من الطول والعرض بمعنى أنه لا يتصف بشيء من ذلك، وإلا لكان منقسماً ضرورة، وإنكار ذلك على ما نسب إلى أبي الحسين الصالحي من قدماء المعتزلة جهالة . والمحكى في كلام المعتزلة عن الصالحي أنه كان يقول الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ جسم لا طول له ولا عرض ولا عمق وليس بذى نصف وأن الجسم ما احتمل الأعراض. ونقل الآمدي اتفاق الكل على أن للجزء خطاً من المساحة، وحمله على أن له حجماً ما على ما في المواقف لا يزيل الاشتباه، ولزوم قبول الانقسام، بل ربما كان ذلك في الحجم أظهر لأنه اسم لما له امتداد ومقدار ما، بحيث إذا كان ذلك في الجهات كان جسماً، وإن أريد أن له مدخلاً في الحجمية والمساحة حيث يزيد ذلك بزيادة الأجزاء، فكذا في الطول والعرض، والمذكور في كلام المعتزلة أن له حظاً من المساحة، ومن الطول عند ابن الراوندي، واتفق أبو علي وأبو هاشم، على أن لاحظ له من الطول، لأن مرجعه إلى التأليف الذي تذهب به الأجزاء في جهة مخصوصة، ثم اختلفا فذهب أبو علي إلى أن لاحظ له من المساحة لأنها أيضاً باعتبار التأليف، وذهب أبو هاشم إلى أن له حظاً من المساحة لأنها اسم لتحيز الجوهر وجرمه الموجب لتكاثفه عند انضمام أمثاله إليه.
Among them is that they agreed that it has no share of length and width, meaning it is not described by any of that, otherwise it would necessarily be divisible. Denial of that as attributed to Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi from the early Mu'tazila is ignorance. And what is reported in the speech of the Mu'tazila from al-Salihi is that he used to say: the indivisible part is a body that has no length, no width, no depth, and is not possessed of a half, and that body is what sustains accidents. Al-Amidi reported the consensus of all that the part has a line of area (khatt min al-misaha), and interpreting that as meaning it has a certain volume (hajm) as in al-Mawaqif does not remove the ambiguity, nor the necessity of accepting division; rather, perhaps that is more apparent in volume because it is a name for what has extension and some magnitude, such that if that is in directions it is a body. And if it is meant that it has an input in voluminousness and area such that it increases with increase of parts, then likewise for length and width. And what is mentioned in the speech of the Mu'tazila is that it has a share of area, and of length according to Ibn al-Rawandi. Abu Ali and Abu Hashim agreed that it has no share of length because its reference is to composition (ta'lif) by which parts go in a specific direction. Then they differed: Abu Ali held that it has no share of area because it also is by consideration of composition, while Abu Hashim held that it has a share of area because it is a name for the tahayyuz (occupation of space) of the atom and its jirm (corporeality) which necessitates its densification when its likes are joined to it.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the agreement that the atom has no length or width, and the subsequent disagreement about whether it has 'area' (misaha). It begins by stating the consensus: the atom is not described by length or width because that would imply divisibility. Denying this is called ignorance, yet it is attributed to an early Mu'tazili, al-Salihi, who reportedly considered the atom a body without dimensions. Then al-Amidi reports a consensus that the atom has 'a line of area', which is interpreted as volume. However, the author notes this does not remove ambiguity about division. The point is made that if area is understood as extension in directions, then having area would make it a body. If it means input into volume, then by the same token it would have length and width. The Mu'tazila are described as holding that the atom has a share of area, and Ibn al-Rawandi even said length. Then Abu Ali and Abu Hashim are presented: both agree no length, but differ on area: Abu Ali says no area (since area depends on composition), while Abu Hashim says yes (since area is the atom's tahayyuz and jirm, which cause densification when joined to similar atoms).

مساحة

تكاثف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The consensus that the atom has no length or width means it is completely dimensionless with no extension whatsoever.
Correction: While the atom lacks length and width, there is disagreement over whether it possesses area (misaha). According to Abu Hashim, area is a property of the atom's spatial occupation (tahayyuz), so it does have a kind of extension.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the fundamental reason Abu Hashim says the atom has a share of area (misaha) while Abu Ali denies it?
A) Abu Hashim defines area as the atom's occupation of space (tahayyuz) and corporeality (jirm), while Abu Ali defines it as composition (ta'lif).
B) Abu Hashim believes in infinite division, while Abu Ali does not.
C) Abu Hashim follows al-Ash'ari while Abu Ali follows the Mu'tazila.
D) Abu Hashim thinks area is necessary for vision, while Abu Ali disagrees.

Key Takeaway
The debate over the atom's dimensions hinges on the definition of area – whether it is a property of composition (ta'lif) or of spatial occupation (tahayyuz).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read a detailed debate about the dimensions of an invisible atom. How does the fact that scholars disagreed—not over faith, but over how to define a single term like 'area'—challenge the way you hold your own beliefs? Are there areas in your life where you cling to a position without understanding the definitions behind it?
?
The consensus that the atom has no length or width is agreed, yet sharp disagreement remains on what 'area' even means. When you face disagreements in your own community about matters of practice or theology, do you rush to a conclusion, or do you first ask: 'What do we mean by the words we are using?'
?
The atom's 'occupation of space' (tahayyuz) gives it reality without requiring dimension. In your spiritual life, what occupies space in your heart—what 'takes up room'—that may not have obvious dimensions but is real? Is your relationship with Allah a reality that 'occupies' you, or is it a mere concept without substance?

Further Disputes on the Atom's Attributes
Sanusi enumerates further disagreements on the atom's attributes, then dismisses them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the range of additional disputes about the atom that Sanusi chooses not to detail, and appreciate his method of dismissing fruitless discussions.

Core Idea
Sanusi lists seven atom disputes, then dismisses them as unbeneficial.

After discussing shape and area of the atom, Sanusi turns to other disputed attributes. He enumerates seven questions, from whether the atom has directions to whether it can hold many accidents. But instead of delving into each, he dismisses them as lacking benefit and refers to longer works. Let's read how he handles these remaining disagreements.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice that Sanusi starts with 'ومنها اختلافهم' and then lists several questions. Pay attention to his tone at the end.
The passage is a single sentence in Arabic.
2
Identify the seven questions
Find each of the seven disagreements. They are introduced with 'هل' or 'أن'. Write them down in English.
3
Notice Sanusi's dismissal
After listing, Sanusi says the details are in longer works and he does not care because his book is concise and these are fruitless. Understand his pedagogical choice.
He ends with a prayer for those who worked on these topics.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Why would Sanusi mention these if he considers them fruitless? It shows he is aware of the debates but chooses to focus on what he deems beneficial. This is a common scholarly practice.

Among them is their disagreement on whether the single atom is described by directions? And on whether it is permissible to see it? And on whether it can become like the weight of a mountain? Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of further disagreements about the single atom. After having discussed shape and area, the author lists seven additional questions: whether the atom can be described by directions, whether it can be seen, whether it can attain the weight of a mountain, how many atoms can meet it, whether God can create it in isolation, whether motion and rest can alternate in it, and whether many accidents can reside in it. The author then notes that the details are found in longer works, and he declines to elaborate because his book is too concise for what he considers fruitless discussions. He ends with a prayer of thanks for those who have worked to clear the dust from the field of kalam. This passage serves as a summary of remaining minor disputes that the author chooses not to explore.

Shapes of Imaginary Parts: The Kalam Dispute
Sanusi reports the dispute on shapes of parts divisible only in imagination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the range of opinions among mutakallimun regarding the shapes of parts that are divisible only in imagination (al-inqisam al-wahmi) and the theological implications of those shapes.

Core Idea
The shape of an imaginary atom determines whether you must accept the existence of void.

This passage presents a fascinating debate: if you believe that bodies are composed of minute parts that can only be divided in the mind, what shape are those parts? The majority chose spheres for simplicity, but that choice forced them to accept the existence of empty space (void) between atoms. Other scholars proposed cubes, triangles, squares, or a mix of shapes for different elements.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify its structure
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Note that it has two parts: a brief list of five opinions and then a more detailed report citing al-Imam (al-Razi).
The passage is cut off at the end due to page break.
2
Note the majority opinion and its consequence
The majority held that the imaginary parts are spheres because spheres are the simplest shape. They consequently accepted the existence of void (empty space) to allow for movement.
Why does sphericity require void? Because spheres cannot tessellate space without gaps.
3
Examine the alternative opinions
Other opinions include cubes, triangles, squares, or a system of five shapes assigned to the four elements. For example, fire has a tetrahedron (four triangles), earth a cube, and air another shape (cut off).
This echoes Platonic solid theory.
4
Reflect on the theological stakes
Consider why the shape of imaginary parts matters: it affects whether one must posit the existence of void, which has implications for God's creative power and the nature of space.
Void was a controversial topic in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأما القائلون) بالأجزاء القابلة للانقسام الوهمي دون العقلي، وقد اختلفوا في أشكالها، فقيل كرات، وقيل مكعبات وقيل مثلثات وقيل مربعات وقيل مختلفات. ذكر الإمام أن القائلين بكون الجسم من أجزاء صغار قابلة للانقسام الوهمي دون الفعلي اختلفوا في أشكالها، فذهب الأكثرون إلى أنها كرات لبساطتها، والتزموا القول بالخلاء وقيل : مكعبات وقيل مثلثات وقيل : مربعات، وقيل: على خمسة أنواع من الأشكال فللنار ذو أربع مثلثات، وللأرض مكعب، وللهواء
He said: (As for those who say) by parts divisible in imaginary division (wahmi) not intellectual (aqli), they differed on their shapes. It is said: spheres, and it is said: cubes, and it is said: triangles, and it is said: squares, and it is said: various. Al-Imam mentioned that those who say the body is composed of small parts divisible in imaginary division not actual division differed on their shapes: the majority held that they are spheres for their simplicity, and they committed to the acceptance of void. And it is said: cubes, and it is said: triangles, and it is said: squares, and it is said: on five types of shapes, so for fire a figure with four triangles, for earth a cube, and for air
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the shapes attributed to the parts that are divisible only in imagination (wahmi) not in actuality or intellect. The author first reports a list: spheres, cubes, triangles, squares, or various. Then he expands citing al-Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi): the majority said spheres because of simplicity, and they consequently accepted the existence of void (khala'). Other opinions: cubes, triangles, squares, or five types, with specific shapes for elements: fire has a figure with four triangles, earth a cube, and air (cut off). The passage presents the range of opinions among those who accept imaginary division, noting that the majority opted for spheres due to simplicity, which required them to posit void to allow for movement through gaps.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might conflate 'imaginary division' with actual physical division.
Correction: Imaginary division (al-inqisam al-wahmi) is a conceptual division that does not entail actual separation into smaller parts. The parts are indivisible in reality but considered in the mind as having shape.

Shapes of Atoms in Estimation Divisibility
Enumerates the differing views on the shapes of atoms that are divisible in estimation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the differing opinions on the shapes of atoms that are divisible in estimation, including the majority view of spheres and the five-element theory.

Core Idea
Atoms divisible in estimation were imagined as spheres, cubes, or tailored to elements.

This passage presents a fascinating debate within kalam: if atoms are only divisible in estimation (not actually), what shapes could they have? Scholars proposed various geometric forms, from simple spheres to element-specific shapes like tetrahedrons and cubes. Let's explore these views as presented by the author.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the list of shapes at the beginning.
The passage begins with a general list then gives a detailed account.
2
Identify the majority view
The text attributes to 'the Imam' that the majority held atoms to be spheres due to simplicity. Note the consequence: they had to postulate void.
Void (al-khala') is necessary for spheres to exist without overlapping.
3
Analyze the five-element theory
Another view assigns five specific shapes to the elements: fire, earth, air, water, and the celestial sphere. Match each element to its shape from the passage.
This theory reflects an influence from Greek natural philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأما القائلون) بالأجزاء القابلة للانقسام الوهمي دون العقلي، وقد اختلفوا في أشكالها، فقيل كرات، وقيل مكعبات وقيل مثلثات وقيل مربعات وقيل مختلفات . ذكر الإمام أن القائلين بكون الجسم من أجزاء صغار قابلة للانقسام الوهمي دون الفعلي اختلفوا في أشكالها، فذهب الأكثرون إلى أنها كرات لبساطتها، والتزموا القول بالخلاء وقيل : مكعبات وقيل مثلثات وقيل : مربعات، وقيل: على خمسة أنواع من الأشكال فللنار ذو أربع مثلثات، وللأرض مكعب، وللهواء ذو ثماني قواعد مثلثات، وللماء ذو عشرين قاعدة مثلثات، وللفلك ذو اثني عشر قاعدة مخمسات، وذكر في الشفاء أنهم يقولون إنها مختلفة الأشكال، وبعضهم يجعلها الأنواع الخمسة
He said: 'As for those who say [that bodies are composed] of parts divisible in estimation (wahmi) but not in reason ('aqli), they differed concerning their shapes. It was said: spheres, and it was said: cubes, and it was said: triangles, and it was said: squares, and it was said: various shapes.' The Imam mentioned that those who hold the body to be composed of small parts divisible in estimation but not actually differed on their shapes. The majority held that they are spheres because of their simplicity, and they committed to the existence of void. And it was said: cubes, and it was said: triangles, and it was said: squares. And it was said: [there are] five types of shapes: for fire, a [tetrahedron] with four triangles; for earth, a cube; for air, an [octahedron] with eight triangular bases; for water, an [icosahedron] with twenty triangular bases; and for the celestial sphere, a [dodecahedron] with twelve pentagonal bases. And it was mentioned in al-Shifa that they say they are of different shapes, and some make them the five types.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the views of the second group concerning the composition of bodies: those who say atoms are divisible in estimation (al-taqsim al-wahmi) but not in actual physical division. They disagreed on the shapes of these atoms. The author first lists possible shapes: spheres, cubes, triangles, squares, and various. Then he attributes to 'the Imam' (likely al-Ghazali or Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) a more detailed account: the majority held them to be spheres due to simplicity, which required postulating void between them. Others said cubes, triangles, or squares. Another view assigned five specific shapes to different elements: fire (tetrahedron), earth (cube), air (octahedron), water (icosahedron), and the celestial sphere (dodecahedron). The author notes that al-Shifa (Ibn Sina's work) also mentions that these atoms are of different shapes, with some adopting these five types.

Correction: The passage explicitly states that the division is only in estimation (al-taqsim al-wahmi), not actual (al-fi'li). It is a thought experiment, not a real physical splitting.

Universality of Hyle for All Bodies
Sanusi argues for the universal presence of hyle in all bodies using the uniform nature of corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why kalam proponents of hylomorphism argue that hyle (matter) is present in every body, including celestial bodies, based on the specific nature of corporeality.

Core Idea
Hyle is universal because corporeality is one specific nature with uniform necessary concomitants.

This passage presents the first branch of the hylomorphism thesis: that matter pervades every body, even those that never actually separate, like the celestial spheres. The author grounds this claim in the nature of corporeality itself. Before we examine his proof, we need to grasp the key distinction between the nature of a thing and its accidental properties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Pay attention to the key terms: هيولي (hyle), جسمية (corporeality), and طبيعة نوعية (specific nature).
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the core argument
Notice how the author argues that corporeality is a specific nature, meaning it does not differ in its necessary concomitants. Since matter is a necessary concomitant of corporeality in bodies that can separate, it must also be present in bodies that cannot separate.
The key phrase: 'لأن الجسمية طبيعة نوعية فلا يخالف في اللوازم'
3
Reflect on celestial bodies
Consider why celestial bodies, though indivisible in actuality, are said to still possess matter. The author uses the uniformity of necessary concomitants to extend the conclusion from separable to inseparable bodies.
This counters the misconception that indivisibility implies lack of matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأما القائلون) بالهيولي والصورة فقد اتفقوا على فروع : الأول : عموم الهيولي لكل جسم، وإن لم يقبل الانفكاك كالفلكيات، لأن الجسمية طبيعة نوعية فلا يخالف في اللوازم وتحقيقه أنه قد ثبت لزوم المادة للجسمية مع قطع النظر عن تشخصاتها، والأسباب المنفصلة عنها، ثم إنها ليست طبيعة عرضية، أو جنسية، يقع على معروضات أو ماهيات مختلفة اللوازم كالوجود، والحيوانية، بل نوعية، لكونه أمراً متحصلاً بنفسه، إذ لا يختلف إلا بما يلحقه من حرارة وبرودة، وما يقارنه من طبيعة فلكية، أو عنصرية، ونحو ذلك مما هو خارج عنها متميزة بحسب الوجود، ولهذا لم يكن الجواب عن الكل والبعض إلا جوهراً متصل الذات، وهذا لا ينافي كون الجسم جنساً يؤخذ مبهماً لا يتحصل إلا بما ينضاف إليه من الفصول، وقد يقرر بأن كل جسم يقبل الانفكاك في ذاته، وإن امتنع لعارض، وبأن الانفصال في الوهم كاف في ثبوت المادة . ذكر من فروع القول بتركب الجسم من الهيولي والصورة خمسة : الأول: ثبوت ذلك لكل جسم، وإن لم يكن قابلاً للانفصال الانفكاكي كالفلكيات وذلك لأن الجسمية. أعني الامتداد الجوهري طبيعة نوعية، إذ لا تختلف حيث تختلف إلا بالعوارض والمشخصات دون الفصول، وقد ثبت أنها فيما يقبل الانفصال الانفكاكي مفتقرة إلى المادة، نظراً إلى ذاتها الاتصالية من غير اعتبار بالتشخصات، والأسباب الخارجة، فكذا فيما لا يقبله، لأن لازم الماهية لا يختلف، ولا يتخلف وتحقيق ذلك ما ذكر في الشفاء إن جسمية إذا خالفت جسمية أخرى تكون لأجل أن هذه حارة، وتلك باردة، وهذه لها طبيعة فلكية، وهذه طبيعة عنصرية.
He said: As for those who affirm hyle and form, they agreed on branches: The first: the universality of hyle for every body, even if it does not admit separation, like the celestial bodies, because corporeality is a specific nature, so it does not differ in its necessary concomitants. Its verification: that the necessity of matter for corporeality has been established, disregarding its individuations and the separate causes from it. Then it is not an accidental or generic nature that applies to different substrates or essences with different concomitants, like existence and animality, but rather specific, because it is a self-subsistent thing, since it differs only by what attaches to it of heat and cold, and what accompanies it of celestial or elemental nature, and the like, which are external to it distinguished according to existence. For this reason, the answer about the whole and part is nothing but a substance continuous in essence. And this does not contradict the body being a genus taken vaguely, not becoming determinate except by what is added to it of differentiae. And it may be established that every body admits division in its essence, even if prevented by an accident, and that separation in the imagination is sufficient for proving matter. He mentioned from the branches of the view that the body is composed of hyle and form five: The first: the establishment of that for every body, even if it is not capable of actual separation, like the celestial bodies, and that because corporeality—I mean the substantial extension—is a specific nature, since it does not differ where it differs except by accidents and individuators, not differentiae. And it has been established that in what admits actual separation, it depends on matter, in view of its extensional essence, without consideration of individuations and external causes. So too in what does not admit it, because the necessary concomitant of the essence does not differ nor lag. And its verification is what was mentioned in al-Shifa: that if one corporeality differs from another, it is because this one is hot and that one is cold, and this one has a celestial nature and that one has an elemental nature.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents the first branch of the proponents of hylomorphism: that hyle (matter) is universal for every body, including celestial bodies that do not actually separate. The reasoning is that corporeality is a specific nature, meaning it does not differ in its necessary concomitants; matter is a necessary concomitant of corporeality in bodies that can separate, so it must also be in bodies that cannot separate, because the nature is the same. The text then elaborates that corporeality is not an accidental or generic nature but a specific one, as it is self-subsistent and differs only by external accidents. It further states that every body is capable of division in its essence, even if actually prevented, and that imaginary division suffices to prove the existence of matter. The summary repeats the first branch and cites al-Shifa to illustrate that differences in corporeality are due to accidents like heat, cold, and celestial vs. elemental nature.

Key Terms
هيولي
صورة
جسمية
طبيعة نوعية
لوازم
انفكاك
مادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Celestial bodies, because they are actually indivisible, do not contain matter.
Correction: The passage argues that matter is a necessary concomitant of corporeality itself, not of actual divisibility. Since corporeality is a specific nature, its concomitants are uniform across all bodies, even those that never separate.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must hyle be present in celestial bodies?
A) Because they are actually divisible
B) Because corporeality is a specific nature with uniform necessary concomitants
C) Because they have a different nature from elemental bodies
D) Because they are composed of matter and form by definition

Key Takeaway
The uniform nature of corporeality ensures that matter, as a necessary concomitant, exists in all bodies, whether separable or not.

?

Body as Genus vs Corporeality as Specific Nature
Objection that body is a genus and answer distinguishing vague body from determinate corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the crucial distinction between 'body' as a logical genus and 'corporeality' as a determinate specific nature, and why this distinction is needed in kalam arguments about matter.

Core Idea
Body is a logical genus; corporeality is a determinate specific nature.

In this objection-and-answer passage, the author addresses a common confusion: if 'body' is universally accepted as a genus in logic, how can corporeality be a specific nature? The answer reveals a key distinction that avoids conflating logical categories with physical realities. Understanding this distinction is essential for arguments about the need for matter in bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Notice the objector's claim: body is a genus, so corporeality cannot be a specific nature. Also note the second point about generic natures having unfailing concomitants.
The objector uses two arguments: (1) body is a genus, so corporeality is generic; (2) even if generic, its concomitants would still be universal, so proving specificity is unnecessary.
2
Identify the answer's core distinction
The answer distinguishes between 'body' as a vague logical concept (not determinate without differentiate) and 'corporeality' as a determinate external reality perceived by sense. This is the key move.
3
Understand the purpose of proving specificity
The answer explains that proving corporeality is a specific nature shows that its need for matter is essential, not due to individuation or differentiate. This refutes the idea that corporeality could be a genus with species differing in their need for matter.
If corporeality were a genus like animality, different species would have different needs, but all bodies need matter essentially.
4
Relate to kalam methodology
Reflect on how this distinction fits into broader kalam arguments about bodies and matter. It demonstrates careful use of logical categories without conflating them with physical realities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لا خفاء ولا خلاف في أن الجسم جنس تحته أنواع بل أجناس، وإنما الكلام في أنه جنس عال أو فوقه جنس الجوهر ، فكيف يصح القول بأن الجسمية طبيعة نوعية؟ ثم أي حاجة إلى ذلك في إثبات المطلوب. ومعلوم أن لوازم الطبيعة الجنسية أيضاً لا يختلف ولا يتخلف . قلنا : فرق بين الجسم الذي يؤخذ أمراً مبهماً، لا يتحصل إلا بما ينضاف إليه من الفصول وبين الجسمية المتحصلة في الخارج بحكم الحس، واحتيج إلى بيان نوعيتها ليعلم أن احتياجها إلى المادة كما أنه ليس من جهة تشخصها، أعني كونها هذه الجسمية، أو تلك التشخص بالبعض دون البعض كذلك ليس من جهة فصول بعض الأقسام أو ماهياتها بأن تكون الجسمية طبيعة جنسية، تحتها جسميات مختلفة الحقائق بالفصول ممكنة الافتراق في اللوازم، كالحيوانية، أو عرضاً عاماً لجسميات كذلك كالوجود .
For it is said: It is not hidden and there is no dispute that body (al-jism) is a genus under which are species, indeed genera. The discussion is only whether it is a highest genus or above it is the genus of substance (jawhar). So how is it valid to say that corporeality is a specific nature? Then what need is there for that in proving the desired point? And it is known that the necessary concomitants of a generic nature also do not differ nor fail. We say: There is a difference between body which is taken as a vague thing, not determinate except by what is added to it of differentiate, and corporeality which is determinate externally by the judgment of sense. And there was need to demonstrate its specificity to know that its need for matter—just as it is not due to its individuation, i.e., its being this corporeality or that individuation of some without others—so likewise it is not due to the differentiate of some divisions or their essences, such that corporeality were a generic nature with bodies differing in realities by differentiate possibly diverging in concomitants, like animality, or a universal accident for such bodies, like existence.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer sequence. The objector argues: Everyone agrees that 'body' is a genus, not a species. So how can you claim that corporeality (jismiyya) is a specific nature? Moreover, even if it were generic, the necessary concomitants of a generic nature are also universal and unfailing, so why go to the trouble of proving it is specific? The answer is that there is a crucial distinction between 'body' as a vague logical concept (which becomes determinate only with differentiate) and corporeality as a determinate entity existing externally, perceived by sense. The need to prove its specificity is to show that its need for matter is not due to accidents or individuations nor due to differentiate of various kinds (as would happen if corporeality were a genus with species differing by differentiate). Rather, the need is essential to corporeality itself. If corporeality were a genus like 'animality', then different species (human, horse) would have different realites and possibly different necessary concomitants. But the need for matter is common to all bodies, so it must be a property of the specific nature of corporeality, not of a genus.

Key Terms
جسم (jism)
جنس (jins)
نوع (naw')
طبيعة نوعية (tabi'a naw'iyya)
فصل (fasl)
عرض (arad)
جوهر (jawhar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because 'body' is a logical genus, corporeality must also be a generic nature.
Correction: The passage shows that 'body' in logic is a vague concept, while corporeality as a physical reality is a determinate specific nature. Logical generality does not imply ontological generality.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what is the key distinction that resolves the objection?
A) The difference between celestial and elemental bodies.
B) The difference between body as a logical genus and corporeality as a determinate external reality.
C) The difference between substance and accident.
D) The difference between form and matter.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between body as a logical genus and corporeality as a specific nature resolves the objection and shows that the need for matter is essential to corporeality itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the difference between logical categories and real essences, where in your own understanding of Allah's attributes or creation have you confused a vague abstraction for a concrete reality? How might that confusion affect your worship or certainty?
?
The passage shows that the need for matter is intrinsic to corporeality itself, not due to external differentiations. In your life, are there duties or truths you have attributed to circumstances (like your role, education, or society) when they are actually required by your very nature as a human being? What would change if you recognized them as essential?
?
The objection treated a logical category as if it determined reality. Are there areas where you accept a logical argument or common opinion without testing it against the concrete realities of revelation and sense? How can you guard against this in your aqeedah?

Bodily Nature vs. Quantity
First part explaining how bodily nature differs from quantity in determinacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how bodily nature differs from quantity in that bodily nature is already determinate externally, while quantity requires differentiae to become determinate.

Core Idea
Bodily nature is concrete and determinate; quantity is abstract and requires specification.

Before we can address the objection that body is a genus, we must first clarify the nature of bodily nature itself. This passage distinguishes between bodily nature (jismiyya) as it exists in external reality and quantity (miqdariyya) as an abstract concept that only becomes concrete through additional differentiae. Understanding this distinction is crucial for the subsequent argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall contrast between bodily nature and quantity.
Pay attention to the terms 'jismiyya' and 'miqdariyya'.
2
Identify the key characteristics
Notice that bodily nature is described as existing externally and being a determinate thing without addition, while quantity is not determinate until diversified by differentiae like line or surface.
The text says: 'al-jismiyya fi al-kharij mawjuda' and 'al-miqdar laysa fi nafsihi shayan muhassalan'.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: Why does this distinction matter? It sets the stage for the objection and response about body being a genus.
Think about how the concrete determinate nature of bodily nature relates to its need for matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدارية وتلك لها طبيعة عنصرية، وهي أمور لا تلحق الجسمية من خارج، فإن الجسمية في الخارج موجودة، والطبيعة الفلكية موجودة أخرى، وقد انضاف إلى تلك الطبيعة القائمة المشار إليها هذه الطبيعة الأخرى في الخارج، بخلاف المقدار الذي هو ليس في نفسه شيئاً محصلاً ما لم يتنوع بأن يكون خطاً أو سطحاً إذ ليست المقدارية موجودة، والخطية موجودة أخرى، بل الخطية نفسها هي المحمولة عليها، فالجسمية مع كل شيء يفرض بشيء متقرر هو جسمية فقط من غير زيادة، وأما المقدار فلا مقدار فقط بل لا بد من فصول حتى يوجد ذاتاً متقررة إما خطاً أو سطحاً. فإن قيل : لا خفاء ولا خلاف في أن الجسم جنس تحته أنواع بل أجناس، وإنما الكلام في أنه جنس عال أو فوقه جنس الجوهر ، فكيف يصح القول بأن الجسمية طبيعة نوعية؟ ثم أي حاجة إلى ذلك في إثبات المطلوب. ومعلوم أن لوازم الطبيعة الجنسية أيضاً لا يختلف ولا يتخلف . قلنا : فرق بين الجسم الذي يؤخذ أمراً مبهماً، لا يتحصل إلا بما ينضاف إليه من الفصول وبين الجسمية المتحصلة في الخارج بحكم الحس، واحتيج إلى بيان نوعيتها ليعلم أن احتياجها إلى المادة كما أنه ليس من جهة تشخصها، أعني كونها هذه الجسمية، أو تلك التشخص بالبعض دون البعض كذلك ليس من جهة فصول بعض الأقسام أو ماهياتها بأن تكون الجسمية طبيعة جنسية، تحتها جسميات مختلفة الحقائق بالفصول ممكنة الافتراق في اللوازم، كالحيوانية، أو عرضاً عاماً لجسميات كذلك كالوجود .
Quantity [al-miqdariyya] has an elemental nature, and these are matters that do not attach to bodily nature [al-jismiyya] from outside, for bodily nature exists externally, and the celestial nature is another existence, and this other nature has been added externally to that standing indicated nature, unlike quantity [al-miqdar] which is not in itself a determinate thing unless it is diversified as being line or surface, since quantity [al-miqdariyya] does not exist, and line-nature is another existence, but rather line-nature itself is what is predicated of it. So bodily nature with everything that is supposed is a determinate thing which is bodily nature only without addition. As for quantity, there is no quantity alone, but rather there must be differentiae until it exists as a determinate essence, either line or surface. For it is said: It is not hidden nor disputed that body is a genus under which are species, rather genera, and the discussion is only whether it is a highest genus or above it is the genus of substance. So how can it be correct to say that bodily nature is a specific nature? Then what need is there for that in establishing the desired point? It is known that the correlates of a generic nature also do not differ and do not fail. We said: There is a difference between body taken as an ambiguous thing, which is not realized except by the differentiae added to it, and bodily nature which is realized externally by the judgment of sense. And it was needed to explain its specific nature so that it is known that its need for matter, just as it is not from the aspect of its individuation (i.e., being this bodily nature or that individuation by some without others), likewise it is not from the aspect of the differentiae of some divisions or their quiddities, such that bodily nature would be a generic nature under which are bodily natures of different realities by differentiae possibly separated in correlates, like animality, or a universal accident over such bodily natures like existence.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. It begins by describing the relationship between quantity (miqdariyya) and bodily nature (jismiyya). Quantity requires differentiae (like line or surface) to become determinate, while bodily nature is already determinate externally. The objector then argues that body is a genus, so bodily nature cannot be a specific nature. The responder distinguishes between body taken as an abstract ambiguous concept and bodily nature as it exists concretely in external reality by sense. The need to prove that bodily nature is a specific nature is to show that its need for matter is not due to its being a genus with different species, but rather due to its own essence. The response concludes that bodily nature is not a generic nature with independent species like animality, nor a universal accident like existence, but a specific nature that requires matter intrinsically.

الجسمية

Checkpoint

A) Bodily nature requires differentiae, quantity does not.

C) Both are equally abstract and indeterminate.
D) Quantity exists externally, bodily nature does not.

Key Takeaway
Bodily nature is always already determinate externally, while quantity only becomes determinate through additional differentiae.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

After reading this passage, consider one area in your life where you treat an abstraction as if it were a determinate essence. What would change if you started seeing it as something that needs proper specification to be meaningful? Commit to one action this week that grounds that abstraction in a concrete reality.
?
The passage distinguishes between something that is inherently determinate (bodily nature) and something that only becomes determinate through external additions (quantity). In your own life, do you treat your core identity—your soul, your faith, your character—as inherently determinate and fixed by Allah, or as something that you constantly construct and redefine based on circumstances? Reflect on a situation where you have acted as if your identity were fluid rather than fixed. What would it look like to live out the truth that your fitrah is determinate and needs no external props? Choose one habit that affirms your fixed identity before Allah.
?
This fragment of kalam forces you to ask: What is truly real in itself, and what is merely a mental abstraction? The author argues that quantity is not real until it becomes line or surface. Compare that to your own spiritual life: is your connection to Allah (e.g., your prayers, your dhikr) a concrete reality that exists externally, or is it an abstract concept that only becomes real when you add conditions like focus, time, or emotion? Refutation of Body as a Genus
Objection and response regarding body as a genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between body as an abstract genus and bodily nature as a specific nature that exists concretely, and how this refutes the objection that body is a genus.

Core Idea
Body taken abstractly is a genus; bodily nature concretely is a specific nature.

The objector claims that body is a genus with species, so how can bodily nature be a specific nature? The response provides a crucial distinction: the abstract concept of body taken ambiguously is indeed a genus, but bodily nature as it exists concretely in external reality is a specific nature. This clarification is essential for understanding why bodily nature intrinsically needs matter without being a genus.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the objector's claim: body is a genus, so bodily nature cannot be a specific nature. Also note the mention of the need for this in proving the desired point.
The objection begins with 'fa in qila'.
2
Understand the response's distinction
The responder distinguishes between two senses of 'body': abstract body (jism mu'khadh amran mubhaman) and concrete bodily nature (jismiyya mutahassila fi al-kharij). This distinction solves the problem.
The response starts with 'qulna'.
3
Reflect on the purpose
The passage explains why it is necessary to show that bodily nature is a specific nature: to prove that its need for matter is intrinsic, not due to being a genus with different species.
The text says: 'wa ihtija ila bayan naw'iyyatiha li yu'lama anna ihtiyajaha ila al-madda...'

Source Text

?

Why Matter Cannot Exist Without Form
Proof that matter cannot exist without form using a dilemma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that matter (hayula) cannot exist stripped of form, based on the impossibility of it having position or being uncaused.

Core Idea
Matter without form is either a body or an uncaused particularization—both impossible.

This passage presents a foundational argument in the kalam discussion of hylomorphism. It shows that matter (hayula) cannot be conceived as an independent substrate without form, by considering whether it can be pointed to or not. The argument leads to a dilemma that undercuts the idea of formless matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall flow. Don't analyse yet; just absorb the main claim and the two options.
Notice the bracketed objection and response at the start.
2
Follow the two-part dilemma
The argument says matter without form must either have position (pointable) or not. If it has position, it becomes a body—contradicting matter's distinct nature. If it has no position, when form attaches, the composite ends up in a location without any cause, which is absurd.
The commentary explains 'position' as being able to be pointed to as 'here' or 'there'.
3
Understand the refutation and its limits
The bracketed note mentions a refutation: the particularizer is not limited to form. But the proof is defended elsewhere. For now, grasp the main argument.
This shows the dialectical nature of kalam; objections are raised and answered.
4
Review key terms
Identify the key Arabic terms: hayula (matter), sura (form), dhat wad' (having position), etc. See how they are used in the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[قال (الثاني) امتناع الهيولي بدون الصورة لأنها إن كانت مشاراً إليها كانت جسماً لامتناع الجوهر الفرد، وإلا فعند حصول الصورة تكون في بعض الأحياز ضرورة وهو تخصيص بلا مخصص، ورد بمنع انحصار المخصص في الصورة ] . من فروع القول بالهيولي أنها تمتنع أن توجد مجردة عن الصورة لأنها حينئذ إما أن تكون ذات وضع أو لا . والمراد بالوضع ههنا كون الشيء بحيث يمكن أن يشار إليه بأنه ههنا أو هنالك ، فإن كانت ذات وضع كان جسماً لكونه جوهراً متحيزاً قابلاً للانقسام في الجهات لما علم في بحث نفي الجزء من أنه يمتنع أن يوجد جوهر متحيز لا ينقسم أصلاً بمنزلة النقطة () أو ينقسم في جهة دون جهة بمنزلة الخط أو السطح وإنما لم يقل كان جسماً أو حالاً في جسم كالأعراض والصور، لأن الكلام في جوهر قابل للصور، وإن لم تكن ذات وضع ولا محالة تصير محلاً للصورة في الجملة .
He said (the second): The impossibility of hyle [al-hayula] without form, because if it is pointed to, it would be a body due to the impossibility of the indivisible atom; otherwise, when form occurs, it would be in some location necessarily, which is a particularization without a particularizer. And it was refuted by denying that the particularizer is confined to form. Among the branches of the doctrine of hyle is that it is impossible for it to exist stripped of form, because then it is either something having position [dhat wad'] or not. What is meant by position here is that the thing can be pointed to as 'here' or 'there'. If it has position, it would be a body because it is a substance occupying space, divisible in directions, as was established in the discussion negating the part (atom) that it is impossible for a divisible [actually 'occupying space'] substance to exist that does not divide at all like a point, or divides in one direction but not another like a line or surface. And he did not say it would be a body or an accident in a body like accidents and forms, because the discussion is about a substance capable of receiving forms. And if it does not have position, then inevitably it becomes a substrate for form in general.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (the second topic) that matter (hayula) cannot exist without form. The argument: matter either has position (can be pointed to) or not. If it has position, it would be a body, conflicting with the doctrine that matter is a distinct substrate from body. If it does not have position, then when form attaches to it, the composite would be in a particular location, requiring a cause for that particular location; but if matter had no position beforehand, the particularization would be without a cause, which is impossible. The commentator explains the meaning of 'position' and clarifies that something with position that is a divisible substance is a body, not a point or line. If matter is not something having position, it would become a substrate for form when form attaches. The response to the objection in the brackets notes that the particularizer is not restricted to form; the proof is defended elsewhere. Overall, this establishes that matter cannot subsist independently of form, which is a key doctrine in the theory of hylomorphism.

?

Answering the Objection on Imaginary Division Proving Matter
Objection and answer on imaginary division as proof for matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam scholars defend the proof of matter (hylè) from imaginary division against the objection that it only occurs in the mind.

Core Idea
Imaginary division reveals a real potential for parts, pointing to an underlying matter.

In this passage, the author addresses a key objection to using imaginary division as proof for matter. Critics argue that imagining a body divided does not mean it can actually be divided externally. The response clarifies that the real possibility of such division, not just a mental trick, is what proves matter. Let's follow the logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of the proof
The proof from imaginary division argues that because we can imagine a body divided, there must be a substrate (matter) that persists. This sets up the debate.
The objection challenges whether imaginary division implies external reality.
2
Read the objection carefully
The objection states that imaginary division only removes connection in the mind, not externally, so it does not prove external matter.
This is the core challenge to the proof.
3
Analyze the answer
The answer redefines the possibility of imaginary division: it means the body actually admits of such division in itself, based on a truthful judgment of the imagination, not a false one. This implies real potential for parts.
The key is that the imagination's judgment is truthful because it is based on real possibility.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The summary concludes that imaginary division, even without actual separation, entails real divisibility and thus the existence of matter in actual fact.
This resolves the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امتنع ذلك لأمر زائد لازم كالصورة الفلكية أو غير ذلك لازم كغاية الصغر ،والصلابة، وفي شرح الإشارات ما يشعر بأن قبول الانفصال الوهمي كاف في إثبات المادة إذ لا بقاء للاتصال الانفصال فلا بد من قابل باق . مع واعترض بأن الانفصال الوهمي إنما يرفع الاتصال في الوهم دون الخارج، فلا يلزم وجود الهيولي في الخارج على ما هو المطلوب. وأجيب : بأن معنى إمكان الانفصال الوهمي هو أن يكون الجسم بحيث يصح حكم الوهم بأن فيه شيئاً غير شيء وجزءاً دون جزء لا من الأحكام الكاذبة الوهمية ، بل الصادقة المبنية على إمكان جزء غير جزء في نفس الأمر وهو معنى الانفصال الخارجي، وحاصله أن القسمة الوهمية، وإن لم تستلزم الانفكاكية لكن قبولها يستلزم قبولها [؟]، وهو يستلزم ثبوت المادة في نفس الأمر.
That is impossible due to an additional necessary cause, like the celestial form or other necessary cause like extreme smallness and hardness. And in the Commentary on the Pointers, there is an indication that the acceptance of imaginary division is sufficient to prove matter, since the connection does not remain after separation, so there must be a substrate that persists. There was an objection: imaginary division only removes connection in the imagination, not externally, so the existence of hylè externally does not follow as is required. And it was answered: the meaning of the possibility of imaginary division is that the body is such that the imagination correctly judges that in it there is something other than something and part other than part—not from false imaginary judgments, but from truthful ones based on the possibility of a part other than a part in actual fact, which is the meaning of external division. The summary is that imaginary division, even if it does not entail actual separation, its acceptance entails its acceptance [?], and that entails the existence of matter in actual fact.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the proof of matter (hylè) from imaginary division. The author first notes that something prevents matter from being without form, such as celestial form or extreme smallness. Then he mentions that the Commentary on the Pointers suggests that the capacity for imaginary division suffices to prove matter, because if connection could be lost, there must be a permanent substrate. The objection states that imaginary division only affects the mental notion of connection, not external reality, so it does not prove external hylè. The answer clarifies that the possibility of imaginary division means the body actually admits of division in itself, not just in the imagination; the imagination's truthful judgment is based on real potential for parts, which implies an underlying matter. The conclusion: imaginary division, even without actual separation, entails real divisibility and thus the existence of matter.

Correction: The possibility of imaginary division is based on the real potential of the body to have parts in actual existence, which is sufficient to prove matter.

Checkpoint
