The Impossibility of Prime Matter Without Form: Core Argument and First Objection
The author proves prime matter cannot exist without form and responds to an objection about specific form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that prime matter cannot exist without form, and how an objection based on specific form is refuted.

Core Idea
Prime matter without form is impossible — it would either be a body or lack specification.

This passage presents the second principle of the discussion on hylè: prime matter cannot exist stripped of form. The author lays out a logical dilemma, introduces an objection, and shows why the objection fails. Pay attention to the role of 'position' and how the refutation avoids infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the key premise: matter without form must either have position or not.
The footnote on points helps clarify what 'indivisible' means in context.
2
Understand the dilemma
If matter has position, it is a body (since indivisible atoms are impossible). If it lacks position, then when form arrives, it would have to be in a specific place without a specifier — which is impossible.
The author defines 'position' as being pointable by sensory indication.
3
Examine the objection
The objection claims that a specific form (e.g., the form of water) could provide the needed specification, so the argument fails.
4
Trace the refutation
The author responds that this merely shifts the problem: why does that specific form choose that particular location? This leads to an infinite regress unless a prior form determines it, as in the example of air becoming water then earth.
The author promises further detail in later sections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[قال (الثاني) امتناع الهيولي بدون الصورة لأنها إن كانت مشاراً إليها كانت جسماً لامتناع الجوهر الفرد، وإلا فعند حصول الصورة تكون في بعض الأحياز ضرورة وهو تخصيص بلا مخصص، ورد بمنع انحصار المخصص في الصورة ] . من فروع القول بالهيولي أنها تمتنع أن توجد مجردة عن الصورة لأنها حينئذ إما أن تكون ذات وضع أو لا . والمراد بالوضع ههنا كون الشيء بحيث يمكن أن يشار إليه بأنه ههنا أو هنالك ، فإن كانت ذات وضع كان جسماً لكونه جوهراً متحيزاً قابلاً للانقسام في الجهات لما علم في بحث نفي الجزء من أنه يمتنع أن يوجد جوهر متحيز لا ينقسم أصلاً بمنزلة النقطة () أو ينقسم في جهة دون جهة بمنزلة الخط أو السطح وإنما لم يقل كان جسماً أو حالاً في جسم كالأعراض والصور، لأن الكلام في جوهر قابل للصور، وإن لم تكن ذات وضع ولا محالة تصير محلاً للصورة في الجملة . (1) النقطة ثلاثة أقسام مادية ورياضية ومتيافيزيقية أما النقطة المادية فهي أصغر شيء ذي وضع يمكن أن يشار إليه بالإشارة الحسية، وأما النقطة الرياضية فهي معنى هندسي أولي لا يمكن تعريفه إلا بنسبته الى غيره كقولنا النقطة ذات غير منقسمة ولها وضع وهي نهاية الخط (راجع رسالة الحدود لابن سينا ص (۹۲) وأما النقطة الميتافيزيقية فهي الذرة أو الموناد (اصطلاح أجنبي). فعند حلول الصورة إما أن تكون في جميع الأحياز وهو تخصيص بلا مخصص ، لأن نسبة الصورة الجسمية إلى جميع الأحياز على السوية . فإن قيل : لعل معها صورة نوعية تقتضي الاختصاص. قلت: فننقل الكلام إلى خصوصية ذلك المظهر أعني الصورة النوعية دون سائر المظاهر ، ثم تعيين هذا الحيز من بين الأحياز التي هي أجزاء حيز كلية ذلك النوع ، ولا يرد النقض بهذا من الأرض مثلاً ، حيث يخصص بهذا الجزء من حيز الأرض لجواز أن يكون ذلك بسبب صورة سابقة مقتضية لهذا الوضع المقتضي (١) بهذا الحيز كما إذا صار جزء من الهواء ماء ثم صار (۳) أرضاً فإنه ينزل على استقامة إلى أن يقع في حيز من الماء ثم الأرض. وسيجيء لهذا زيادة بينان ، وكذا الكلام في وجه اختصاص المادة بما لها من الصورة النوعية على ما نفصله في الفرع الخامس . يرد النقض به
[He said (the second): The impossibility of hylè without form, because if it is pointable it would be a body due to the impossibility of an indivisible atom, otherwise when the form arrives it would necessarily be in some place, which is specification without a specifier. It was refuted by denying that the specifier is confined to form.] From the branches of the doctrine of hylè is that it cannot exist stripped of form, because then it is either with position or not. Position here means that a thing can be pointed to as here or there. If it has position, it is a body because it is a substance occupying space, divisible in dimensions, as established in the discussion negating the part that a substance occupying space cannot exist that is not divisible at all like a point, or divisible in one dimension only like a line or surface. He did not say it is a body or an accident in a body like other accidents and forms, because the discussion is about a substance receptive of forms. If it does not have position, it necessarily becomes a locus for form in general. (1) The point has three kinds: material, mathematical, and metaphysical. As for the material point, it is the smallest thing with position that can be pointed to by sensory indication. As for the mathematical point, it is a primary geometric meaning that cannot be defined except by its relation to others, such as our saying 'the point is indivisible, has position, and is the extremity of a line' (cf. Ibn Sina's Treatise on Definitions, p. 92). As for the metaphysical point, it is the atom or monad (foreign term). So when the form arrives, either it is in all places, which is specification without a specifier, because the relation of the bodily form to all places is equal. If it is said: perhaps there is with it a specific form that necessitates specification. I say: then we transfer the discussion to the particularity of that manifestation, namely the specific form apart from other manifestations, and then the determination of this place among the places that are parts of the universal place of that species. The refutation is not disproven by this, like earth, for example, when it is specified to this part of the place of earth, because it is possible that this is due to a prior form requiring this position that necessitates this place, as when a part of air becomes water then becomes earth; it descends straight until it falls into a place of water then earth. Further explanation will come, and likewise the discussion on the aspect of matter's specification by its specific form as we will detail in the fifth branch. The objection is refuted by it.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage provides the main argument for the impossibility of hylè (prime matter) existing without form, along with an objection and response. It begins with a quoted statement of the second principle: hylè cannot exist without form because if it were pointable it would be a body (since an indivisible substance is impossible), and if not pointable, then when form attaches it would have to be in some particular place without a specifier. The author then explains the principle: matter stripped of form must either have position (making it a body with dimensions) or not. The footnote clarifies the three types of point (material, mathematical, metaphysical) used in the argument. If matter has no position, it becomes a mere locus for form. When form arrives, it cannot occupy all places equally without a specifier. An objection suggests that a specific form (e.g., water, earth) could provide specification. The answer is that this merely shifts the problem to why that specific form chose that particular location, leading to an infinite regress unless a prior form determines it. The example of air becoming water then earth illustrates how a series of forms can determine location. The author promises further explanation.

وضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the argument assumes matter is completely passive, or that a specific form can sufficiently explain specification without further regress.
Correction: The passage refutes the idea that a specific form can suffice for specification, showing that it only shifts the problem and leads to an infinite regress unless prior forms determine location.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why cannot matter exist without form?
A) Because matter would then be indivisible.
B) Because matter without form would either be a body (if pointable) or lack specification (if not), and form would need a specifier.
C) Because form is more fundamental than matter.
D) Because matter is only a mental construct.

Key Takeaway
Prime matter cannot exist without form because its existence would either entail it being a body or lead to an unjustified specification, which even the appeal to specific form cannot resolve without infinite regress.

?
The argument shows that even a specific form cannot fully explain why you are where you are without leading to an infinite regress. In your daily life, do you truly acknowledge the ultimate Cause behind every detail of your existence, or do you stop at secondary causes as if they are sufficient? What would change if you lived with constant awareness of the Creator's direct specification of every moment?
?
The refutation of the objection reveals that appealing to a known cause only pushes the question back. When you attribute success or failure to your own efforts, do you recognize that your actions themselves are specified by the Creator? How can you cultivate a deeper reliance on Allah while still taking responsibility for your choices?

Form Requires Matter: Proof and Objection
Sanusi proves the impossibility of form without matter and responds to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument for why form cannot exist independently of matter and how the objection that form might be neither self-subsistent nor inherent is addressed.

Core Idea
Form cannot be independent; it needs a locus (matter) to exist.

This lesson examines a key principle in philosophical theology: the dependence of form on matter. The author presents a concise proof, then considers a possible objection that challenges the very terms of the argument. To fully grasp the discussion, we also explore the concept of 'abstraction' as defined by the philosophers, which helps clarify what it means for something to be abstract or inherent.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the author's statement of the third principle and its proof: if form subsisted by itself, it would have no need of a locus, so it would not inhere in matter.
2
Understand the objection
Notice the objection: form might be neither self-subsistent nor inherent—there could be a third mode. This challenges the binary assumption of the proof.
The objection is not a denial of the conclusion but a critique of the logic.
3
Learn about abstraction (tajrīd)
Read the footnote defining abstraction linguistically and philosophically. Understand how abstraction relates to the discussion of form and matter.
Ibn Sina's quote highlights that abstraction has different levels.
4
Reflect on the argument's foundation
Consider how the concept of abstraction might be used to defend the principle or to respond to the objection. The commentary will help.
This step connects the two parts of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الثالث امتناع الصورة بدون الهيولي فلا لأنها لو قامت بذاتها استغنت عن المحل فلم تحل فيه . وردّ بأنه يجوز أن لا يكون التجرد ولا الحلول لذاتها . ولهم في بيان ذلك وجوه : (4) التجريد أو التجرد في اللغة التعرية تقول جرد الشيء قشره وجرد الجلد نزع شعره وجرد السيف من غمده سله والتجريد عند الفلاسفة هو انتزاع النفس عنصراً من عناصر الشيء والتفاتها اليه وحده دون غيره مثال ذلك أن العقل يجرد امتداد الجسم من كتلتيه أن هذا الجسم لا تنفك عنه صفتاه في الوجود الخارجي قال ابن سينا ان أصناف التجريد مختلفة ومراتبها متفاوتة. (راجع النجاة ص ٢٧٥)
He said (the third): The impossibility of form without hylè, because if it subsisted by itself it would be independent of a locus and thus would not inhere in it. It was refuted by saying that it is possible that it is neither abstract nor inhering in its essence. And they have ways of explaining that: (4) Abstraction (tajrīd) or absraction (tajarrud) in language means stripping; you say: he stripped the thing of its peel, and stripped the skin of its hair, and drew the sword from its sheath. Abstraction according to the philosophers is the mind's extraction of an element from the elements of a thing and its attention to it alone apart from others. An example: the mind abstracts the extension of a body from its mass, even though the body is not separable from the two attributes in external existence. Ibn Sina said: the kinds of abstraction are different and their levels are varied. (Ref. al-Najat, p. 275)
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents the third principle: form cannot exist without matter. The proof is brief: if form subsisted by itself, it would not need a locus and thus would not inhere in matter. An objection states that form might be neither self-subsistent nor inherent—it could have another mode of existence. The author then notes that the defenders of this principle have ways of explaining it, and provides a footnote clarifying the concept of abstraction (tajrīd), which is relevant to how forms might be considered. The footnote defines abstraction linguistically and philosophically, quoting Ibn Sina.

Key Terms
صورة
هيولي
قيام بذات
محل
تجريد
حلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection asserts that form exists without any relation to matter.
Correction: The objection does not assert that form exists without any relation; it suggests a third possibility that is neither self-subsistence nor inherence.

Checkpoint
According to the third principle, why cannot form exist without matter?
A) Because form is always material.
B) Because if form subsisted by itself it would not need a locus, so it would not inhere in matter.
C) Because matter is more fundamental than form.
D) Because form is an accident of matter.

Key Takeaway
Form cannot exist without matter because it cannot be self-subsistent; any possible third mode is clarified by the concept of abstraction, which shows that form is always in relation to matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage demonstrates that form cannot exist without matter. Consider your own good intentions (a form): are they real if they are not expressed in actions (matter)? How does this lesson urge you to ground your spiritual aspirations in concrete deeds this week?
?
The objection reveals a tendency to think in binaries. In what area of your faith do you assume an either/or when a both/and might be more accurate? What would change if you embraced the third possibility?
?
Abstraction is a useful tool but can deceive us into thinking something exists independently. How might you be mistaking your mental concepts of God, self, or good deeds for the reality that requires embodiment? Commit to one concrete change in how you live out your faith this week.

The Third Proof: Form Cannot Exist Without Matter
Third proof and its refutation regarding form's necessity of matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that form cannot exist without matter and its refutation based on the possibility that neither being immaterial nor inhering is essential to the form.

Core Idea
A form's independence does not prove its inability to inhere; its essence may be neutral.

This lesson presents the third proof from the kalam tradition for the dependence of form on matter. The proof argues that if a form were self-subsistent, it would be independent and thus could not inhere in matter. The refutation challenges this by suggesting that the form's essence might not necessitate either immateriality or inherence—both could come from external causes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the first part of the source text carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: a proof followed by a refutation.
2
Identify the proof's assumption
The proof assumes that if a form is self-subsistent, it must be independent and therefore cannot inhere. Identify why this assumption is challenged.
3
Analyze the refutation
The refutation states that it is possible that neither being immaterial (tajarrud) nor inhering (hulul) is essential to the form. Ask yourself: what does this mean for the proof?
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this refutation does not prove that form is independent, but only that the proof is insufficient. Connect to the larger debate about matter and form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الثالث امتناع الصورة بدون الهيولي فلا لأنها لو قامت بذاتها استغنت عن المحل فلم تحل فيه . وردّ بأنه يجوز أن لا يكون التجرد ولا الحلول لذاتها . ولهم في بيان ذلك وجوه : أخصرها أنها لو وجدت مجردة لكانت مستغنية في ذاتها عن المحل فيمتنع حلولها فيه، لأن ما بالذات لا يزول، وأنها تستلزم قبول الانقسام الوهمي المستلزم لقبول الانقسام الانفكاكي المستلزم للمادة . ورد الأول بأنه يجوز أن لا تقتضي ذاتها التجرد عن المادة، ولا الحلول فيها، بل كل منهما يكون لأمر من خارج .
He said: The third [proof], the impossibility of form without matter. Why? Because if it were self-subsistent (qāmat bi-dhātihā), it would be independent of the subject (al-maḥall), so it would not inhere in it. And it was refuted by [saying] that it is possible that neither being immaterial (al-tajarrud) nor inherence (al-ḥulūl) is essential to it. And they have in explaining that several ways (wujūh): The most concise of them (akhaṣaruhā) is that if it existed independently, it would be self-sufficient in its essence from the subject, so its inherence in it would be impossible, because what is by essence (mā bi-l-dhāt) does not cease; and that it entails the acceptance of imaginary division (al-inqisām al-wahmī) which entails the acceptance of separation division (al-inqisām al-infikākī) which entails matter. And the first [way] was refuted by [saying] that it is possible that its essence does not necessitate being immaterial from matter nor inherence in it, but rather each of them comes from an external cause.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the third proof for the claim that a form cannot exist without matter. The proof is: if a form were self-subsistent (qāmat bi-dhātihā), it would be independent of a subject, so it could not inhere in matter. This proof is immediately refuted by noting that it is possible that neither being immaterial (al-tajarrud) nor inherence (al-ḥulūl) is essential (li-dhātihā) to the form; both could be due to external factors. The author then mentions that proponents of the proof have several ways (wujūh) to support it. The first and most concise (akhaṣaruhā) is given: if the form existed independently, it would be self-sufficient and thus cannot inhere because what is essential does not change; also, it entails the acceptance of imaginary division, which entails separation division, which entails matter. This first way is then refuted: it is possible that the form's essence does not require either being immaterial or inhering; both states could be from an external cause. Thus, the refutation challenges the assumption that the form's essential nature is known.

Key Terms
صورة
هيولي
تجرد
حلول
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation of the third proof proves that form does not need matter.
Correction: The refutation only shows that the proof is insufficient; it does not establish that form is independent of matter.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the refutation of the third proof?
A) Form is essentially immaterial.
B) It is possible that neither tajarrud nor hulul is essential to the form.
C) Form cannot be self-subsistent.
D) The proof is too complicated.

Key Takeaway
The third proof fails because it assumes that self-subsistence necessarily implies independence from inherence, but the form's essence could be neutral.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The refutation shows that a thing's essence may be neutral toward certain attributes. In your own life, have you assumed that a fixed trait (like your temper or a sin) is essential to you? What would change if you believed that trait could be modified by external factors? Commit to one concrete action today to challenge that assumption.
?
The third proof wrongly concluded impossibility from a flawed premise. Where in your relationship with Allah have you concluded that change is impossible (e.g., breaking a habit, improving your prayer) based on a premise that may be flawed? What step can you take this week to test whether the premise is truly valid?
?
Do you ever dismiss someone's argument simply because its reasoning is weak, rather than engaging with the underlying claim? The refutation here does not affirm the opposite claim; it only points out the flaw. How can you apply this humility in discussion with others about faith? Write one person you disagree with and plan to ask them clarifying questions instead of assuming your position is proven.

The First Concise Proof: Form's Entailment of Division and Matter
First concise proof and its refutation concerning form's dependence on matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first concise proof that form cannot exist without matter, based on the entailment of imaginary division leading to separation division and matter, and its refutation that the form's essence does not necessitate these properties.

Core Idea
Imaginary division is taken as evidence for matter, but the form's essence could be indifferent to division.

This lesson examines the most concise of the several ways used to support the third proof. It argues that if form existed independently, it would accept imaginary division, which entails separation division, which entails matter. The refutation, however, points out that the form's essence might not necessitate either immateriality or inherence—both could be from external causes.

3
Understand the refutation

4
Compare with the earlier refutation
Notice that both refutations use the same idea: the form's essence is neutral. See how this pattern appears across the arguments.

The Most Famous Proof: Form Necessitates Matter
Gives the most famous proof for form's dependence on matter and the first objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the most famous kalam proof that bodily form entails shape and shape entails matter, and the first objection to it.

Core Idea
Shape cannot vary without matter — connection and separation demand a material substrate.

This passage presents the centerpiece of the argument for form's dependence on matter. After briefly dismissing a weaker proof, the author introduces the strongest proof: bodily form necessitates shape, and shape necessitates matter. But this proof is not without objections, and we will examine the first objection involving a candle. Let's walk through the proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's structure come through.
Pay attention to the two-part proof and the two objections.
2
Identify the proof's two parts
The proof has two claims: (1) bodily form entails shape, (2) shape entails matter. Find the reasoning for each.
The first part relies on the finitude of extensions; the second on the need for connection and separation for variation.
3
Understand the first objection
The objector uses the example of a candle: its shape and size change without actual separation (e.g., melting). This challenges the necessity of separation for shape variation.
This objection targets the claim that variation requires separation.
4
Note the incomplete second objection
The second objection criticizes the reliance on imaginary division. The text cuts off, so we only have the beginning.
The author notes that even if not logically fatal, the argument is considered inelegant in disputation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: يمنع استلزام قبول الانقسام الوهمي للانفكاكي. وقد مر الكلام فيه . وأشهرها أن الصورة الجسمية مستلزمة للشكل، وهو مستلزم للمادة . أما الأول : فلما سيجيء من تناهي الامتدادات ولا نعني بالشكل إلا هيئة إحاطة نهاية أو نهايات . وأما الثاني : فلأن حصول الشكل لولم يكن بمشاركة من المادة ولم يكن لها دخل في ذلك، فإما أن تكون بمجرد الطبيعة الامتدادية، فيلزم تساوي الأجسام في الأشكال أو بحسب فاعل من خارج ، فيتوقف اختلاف المقادير والأشكال على اتصال وانفصال وعلى قبول وانفعال، وقد سبق أن ذلك بدون المادة محال . واعترض بوجهين : أحدهما : منع لزوم الانفصال، فإنه قد تختلف المقادير والأشكال بدون الانفصال كما في تبدل مقادير الشمعة وأشكالها مع أن امتدادها بحالها، وإن أريد أن إمكان الانفصال الوهمي مستلزم لإمكان الانفكاك المحوج إلى المادة على ما كأن باقي المقدمات مستدركاً في البيان، وهو وإن لم يكن قادحاً في الفرض لكن لا كلام في استقباحه في أدب المناظرة سيما إذا كان بعض المقدمات مر،
And the second [way]: it prevents the entailment of accepting imaginary division for separation division. And the discussion about it has passed. And the most famous of them [is that] bodily form (al-ṣūra al-jismiyya) entails shape (al-shakl), and it entails matter. As for the first [part], it is due to what will come concerning the finitude of extensions (tanāhī al-imtidādāt) — and we do not mean by shape except the configuration (hayʾa) of encompassing an end or ends. And as for the second [part], because the occurrence of shape, if it is not by the participation of matter and matter has no role in it, then either it is by mere extensive nature (al-ṭabīʿa al-imtidādiyya), leading to the equality of bodies in shapes, or by an external agent. Then the variation of quantities and shapes depends on connection (ittiṣāl) and separation (infiṣāl), and on reception (qubūl) and being acted upon (infiʿāl), and it has preceded that this is impossible without matter. And it was objected with two objections: one of them is the prevention of the necessity of separation, for quantities and shapes can vary without separation, as in the change of the quantities and shapes of a candle while its extension remains as it is. And if it is intended that the possibility of imaginary division entails the possibility of separation (al-infikāk) which requires matter, as if the remaining premises are redundant in the explanation — and although it is not a defect in the supposition, there is no doubt about its ugliness in the etiquette of disputation, especially if some of the premises ... [the text cuts off]
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage first briefly mentions the second way (al-thānī), which denies that imaginary division entails actual separation, noting that it has been discussed earlier. Then it presents the most famous proof (ashharuhā): bodily form (al-ṣūra al-jismiyya) necessitates shape (al-shakl), and shape necessitates matter (al-mādda). The proof is explained in two parts. First, shape is defined as the configuration of an encompassing end or ends, implying finitude of extensions. Second, if shape occurs without matter's participation, either it arises from mere extensive nature, which would make all bodies equal in shape, or from an external agent. In either case, variation in quantity and shape would require connection and separation, as well as reception and being acted upon, all of which are impossible without matter. Two objections are raised. The first objects to the necessity of separation, using the example of a candle whose size and shape change without actual separation (e.g., melting). The second objection (incomplete) seems to criticize the reliance on imaginary division, calling it inelegant in disputation even if not fatal. The author notes that even if not destructive, the argument is considered poor in debate etiquette.

Key Terms

شكل
امتداد
انفصال
انفكاك
مادة
قبول
انفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all changes in shape require physical separation of parts.
Correction: The first objection shows that shape variation can occur without actual separation, as in a melting candle — the shape changes while the extension remains continuous.

Checkpoint
According to the most famous proof, why does the occurrence of shape require matter?
A) Because shape is always an accident of matter.
B) Because variation of shapes requires connection and separation, which are impossible without matter.
C) Because the external agent that gives shape needs matter to act upon.
D) Because imaginary division is impossible without matter.

Key Takeaway
The strongest proof for form's dependence on matter is that bodily form necessitates shape, and shape requires matter because variation in shape depends on connection, separation, reception, and being acted upon — all of which are impossible without matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a composite of form and matter that is constantly changing. If every change in your shape and size requires matter, and matter itself is contingent, what does that say about the permanence of your own existence? What are you placing your ultimate trust in?
?
The proof reveals that your body's shape depends on connection and separation at every moment. As you consider your own physical fragility, does this make you feel vulnerable or dependent? How should that feeling translate into your relationship with the One who sustains you?
?
The candle's shape changed without separation, yet it required matter. Similarly, your external roles and appearances shift over time, yet you remain tied to a material body. In what ways are you clinging to temporary shapes — like status, health, or beauty — instead of the eternal reality that lies beyond them?

Two Objections to the Shape Argument and the Answer to the First
Sanusi enumerates two objections to the shape argument and answers the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two objections raised against the famous shape argument and how the first objection is answered through a refined understanding of what 'separation' means.

Core Idea
Shape change does not directly require separation; it requires either separation or affection.

Sanusi now examines two objections to the argument that bodies have a shape partly due to matter. The first objection denies that separation is necessary for shape change, using the example of a candle. The second objection points to simple celestial bodies where parts do not mirror the whole. This passage presents these objections and then gives a detailed answer to the first, clarifying that the argument actually requires either separation or affection, and reinterpreting 'separation' as disconnection between bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections
Read both objections carefully, noting the candle example and the celestial bodies counterexample.
The second objection is not answered in this passage.
2
Identify the first answer
Find the two-part answer to the first objection; note how it shifts from requiring separation alone to requiring either separation or affection.
This reframing is crucial.
3
Understand the second answer
See how the second part redefines separation as disconnection between bodies, not internal division.
4
Reflect on the missing answer
Note that the second objection is not answered here; consider why this might be and what the answer could be.
This is left for later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض بوجهين : أحدهما : منع لزوم الانفصال، فإنه قد تختلف المقادير والأشكال بدون الانفصال كما في تبدل مقادير الشمعة وأشكالها مع أن امتدادها بحالها، وإن أريد أن إمكان الانفصال الوهمي مستلزم لإمكان الانفكاك المحوج إلى المادة على ما كأن باقي المقدمات مستدركاً في البيان، وهو وإن لم يكن قادحاً في الفرض لكن لا كلام في استقباحه في أدب المناظرة سيما إذا كان بعض المقدمات المستدركة في غاية الخفاء كتناهي الأبعاد . وثانيهما: النقض بكل بسيط من الفلكيات والعنصريات حيث كانت طبيعة الكل والجزء واحدة، مع أن الجزء ليس على شكل الكل ومقداره. وأجيب عن الأول بوجهين : أحدهما : أن المراد لزوم أحد الأمرين أعني الانفصال كما في تشكلات الماء تجعله مياها أو مجرد الانفعال كما في الشمع ، وكل منهما يستلزم المادة على ما سبق من برهاني الانفصال والانفعال مع ما عليهما من الإشكال. ولا خفاء في أن هذا مع كونه مخالفاً لظاهر تقرير القوم مشتمل على استدراك، لأن إمكان الانفعال لازم قطعاً، فلا معنى لضم الانفصال إليه، وجعل اللازم أحدهما ، ولا ينبغي أن يحمل على هذا المعنى عبارة شرح الإشارات . حيث قال : هذا الاعتراض ليس بقادح في الفرض، لأنا لم نجعل لزوم المحال مقصوراً على لزوم الفصل والوصل، بل عليه وعلى لزوم الانفعال) ، معناها أنا رتبنا لزوم المحال على لزوم الانفصال، ولزوم الانفعال جميعاً ، فإن ثبت كلا اللزومين فذاك ، وإلا فلا خفاء في لزوم الانفعال وهو كاف في لزوم المادة . وثانيهما: أن ليس المراد انفصال الجسم في نفسه، بل انفصال الأجسام بعضها عن بعض لمعنى عدم الاتصال عما من شأنه الاتصال، فإن هذا المحوج إلى المادة لا مجرد التمايز والافتراق.
And he objected in two ways: The first: denial of the necessity of separation, for magnitudes and shapes may differ without separation, as in the change of the magnitudes and shapes of a candle while its extension remains the same. And if it is intended that the possibility of imaginary separation entails the possibility of disjunction requiring matter, as if the remaining premises are redundant in the explanation, and although it is not fatal to the hypothesis, there is no doubt that it is objectionable in the etiquette of debate, especially if some of the redundant premises are extremely obscure like the finitude of dimensions. The second: objection by every simple thing among the celestial and elemental bodies, where the nature of the whole and the part is one, yet the part is not of the shape and magnitude of the whole. And the first was answered in two ways: The first: that what is meant is the necessity of one of two things, namely separation as in the configurations of water turning it into waters, or mere affection as in wax, and each of them entails matter according to what has preceded of the two proofs of separation and affection, despite the problems they have. And it is no secret that this, although contrary to the apparent formulation of the people, includes redundancy, because the possibility of affection is necessarily required, so there is no meaning to attaching separation to it and making the necessary one of them. And the expression of Sharh al-Isharat should not be interpreted in this sense, where it said: 'This objection is not fatal to the hypothesis, for we did not restrict the necessity of impossibility to the necessity of separation and connection, but rather to it and to the necessity of affection.' Its meaning is that we arranged the necessity of impossibility on the necessity of separation and the necessity of affection together. So if both necessities are established, then that is it; otherwise, there is no doubt about the necessity of affection, and it is sufficient for the necessity of matter. And the second: that what is meant is not the separation of the body in itself, but rather the separation of bodies from each other in the sense of lack of connection from what is supposed to be connected, for this is what necessitates matter, not mere distinction and separation.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents two objections to the famous argument that shape necessitates matter. The first objection denies that separation is necessary for shape change, giving the example of a candle changing shape without separation. It also criticizes the argument for relying on obscure premises. The second objection is a counterexample: simple bodies like celestial spheres have the same nature in part and whole, but the part does not have the whole's shape or magnitude, contradicting the argument's implication. The answer to the first objection is given in two ways: First, the argument actually requires either separation or affection, both of which necessitate matter. It clarifies that the intention is not separation alone but either separation or affection, and that affection alone is sufficient. The second answer reinterprets 'separation' as the separation of bodies from each other (disconnection) rather than internal division, which necessitates matter. The passage ends with this second answer. Note that the second objection is not answered in this passage.

Key Terms
انفصال (separation)
انفعال (affection)
مقادير (magnitudes)
بسيط (simple)
فلكيات (celestials)
عنصريات (elementals)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer to the first objection merely restates the original argument without addressing the criticism.
Correction: The answer reframes the requirement from separation alone to either separation or affection, and then reinterprets separation as disconnection between bodies, thus directly addressing the candle objection.

Checkpoint
What is the second answer to the first objection?
A) That affection alone is sufficient to necessitate matter.
B) That separation means disconnection of bodies, not internal division.
C) That the candle example is invalid.
D) That the argument is completely wrong.

Key Takeaway
The argument for matter's role in shape is refined: shape change requires either separation or affection, and 'separation' means disconnection between bodies, not internal division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage differentiates between separation (disconnection between bodies) and internal division. When you feel distant from Allah, do you treat it as a break in relationship (disconnection) or a fracture within your soul? Which view leads you to seek reconciliation more urgently?
?
Sanusi argues that affection (being passively molded) is enough to necessitate matter—you don't need to break apart to change. In your spiritual life, are you resisting being molded by the Qur'an because you think you need a dramatic separation first? What would it look like to simply let the truth reshape you today?
?
The second objection points to simple bodies where part equals whole in nature. Is your heart simple or divided? Do the various parts of your life (work, family, worship) reflect the same nature, or are they in contradiction? What will you do to make them consistent before the One who sees all?

Refutation of the Argument for Multiple Substantial Forms
The author critiques the argument for multiple substantial forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument for multiple substantial forms in composite substances is considered unsound, including the author's two specific criticisms.

Core Idea
Multiple substantial forms are an unproven idea, not a logical necessity.

This passage deals with a key debate in medieval Islamic philosophy: whether composite substances contain multiple substantial forms (e.g., matter, bodily form, specific form) or a single form. The author argues that the hierarchical model proposed by philosophers lacks proof and rests on shaky assumptions. He presents a concise refutation that draws on inconsistencies in the philosophers' own system.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall argument. Notice the author's structure: he states a known fact, then describes the unproven claim, then presents a counterargument and his response.
2
Identify the starting point
The author begins by affirming that water and fire differ in reality while sharing corporeality, just as man and horse share animality. This is the known fact that any theory must account for.
3
Understand the claimed hierarchy
The philosophers propose layers: matter, bodily form, specific form, and so on through levels of mixture until the last species like man. This implies many substances in a single entity.
The author says this has not been proven.
4
Grasp the refutation
The author then addresses a counterargument that different species require different forms as bases for differentiate. He dismisses it as unsound and gives two reasons.
Focus on the two reasons: intellects/souls as simple substances, and the rational soul not being matter or form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحق أن اثبات الصور الجوهرية سيما النوعية وأن الذي يعلم قطعاً هو أن الماء والنار مثلاً مختلفان بالحقيقة مع الاشتراك في الجسمية كالإنسان والفرس في الحيوانية، وأما أن في كل منهما جوهراً لا يختلف بالحقيقة حالا فيه أيضاً هو المادة، وآخر كذلك حالا في الأول هو الصورة الجسمية، وآخر مختلف بالحقيقة حالا فيه أيضاً هو الصورة النوعية، وهكذا في سائر مراتب امتزاج العناصر إلى أن تنتهي إلى النوع الأخير كالإنسان مثلاً، فيكون في مادته جواهر كثيرة هي صور العناصر والأخلاط والأعضاء، وآخرها صورة نوعية إنسانية حالة غير النفس الناطقة المفارقة فلم يثبت بعد، وما يقال أن الأجزاء العقلية إنما توجد من الأجزاء الخارجية فلا بد في اختلاف أنواع الجنس الواحد من صور مختلفة الحقيقة، هي مأخذ الفصول ليس بمستقيم، لأنهم جعلوا العقول والنفوس أنواعاً بسيطة من جنس الجوهر، ولأن الجزء الخارجي قد لا يكون مادة ولا صورة كالنفس الناطقة، اللهم إلا بمجرد التسمية.
And the truth is that the affirmation of substantial forms, especially specific forms, and that which is known with certainty is that water and fire, for example, differ in reality while sharing in corporeality, like man and horse in animality. As for that in each of them there is a substance that does not differ in reality (present in it also) which is matter, and another likewise present in the first which is the bodily form, and another differing in reality present in it also which is the specific form, and so on in the remaining levels of mixture of elements until they end at the last species, like man for example, so that in his matter there are many substances which are the forms of elements, humors, and organs, and the last of them is a human specific form inherent but not the rational separate soul — this has not been established yet. And what is said that the intellectual parts only come from external parts, so there must be in the differences of types of the same genus forms differing in reality, which are the basis of the differentiate — this is not sound, because they made the intellects and souls simple types of the genus of substance, and because the external part may not be matter nor form, like the rational soul, unless by mere terminology.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is an objection to a Peripatetic argument for the existence of multiple substantial forms in composite substances. The author begins by stating the truth (الحق) regarding the affirmation of substantial forms, especially specific forms (النوعية). He notes that it is known with certainty that water and fire differ in reality while sharing corporeality, just as man and horse share animality. He then describes the claimed hierarchical composition: each substance has matter as one substance, bodily form as another, and specific form as yet another, and so on through levels of element mixing until the last species like man, resulting in many substances (forms of elements, humors, organs) and a final human specific form (inherent but not the rational soul). The author asserts that this hierarchy has not been proven (فلم يثبت بعد). Then he addresses the counterargument that intellectual parts derive from external parts, requiring different forms as bases for differentiate, and dismisses it as unsound (ليس بمستقيم). His reasons are two: 1) The philosophers themselves consider intellects and souls as simple types of the genus substance, contradicting the need for compositional forms. 2) External parts may not be matter or form at all, as in the case of the rational soul — unless the terminology is used loosely.

Key Terms
الصور الجوهرية
الصورة النوعية
المادة
الصورة الجسمية
النفس الناطقة
الفصول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author completely denies substantial forms.
Correction: The author only rejects the unproven claim of multiple hierarchical forms in a single substance; he does not deny the existence of substantial forms per se.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the argument for multiple substantial forms unsound?
A) Because water and fire are not different in reality.
B) Because the philosophers themselves treat intellects and souls as simple substances, contradicting the need for compositional forms.
C) Because the rational soul is material.
D) Because the specific form is the same as the rational soul.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the proof for multiple substantial forms by pointing out its lack of proof and internal inconsistencies, such as the philosophers' own admission of simple substances like intellects and souls.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that a long-held philosophical hierarchy of forms has not been proven. Do you hold any beliefs about the unseen world—about angels, souls, or the nature of existence—that you accept without demanding proof? Would you be willing to examine them with the same rigor the author applies here?
?
The author refutes an argument by pointing out its internal inconsistency. When you defend your own beliefs, do you check whether they contradict other truths you hold? How can you develop the habit of intellectual consistency?
?
Consider the rational soul—the philosophers admit it is simple. Yet they multiply forms elsewhere. Are you consistent in your understanding of the soul? Does your daily life reflect that the soul is a simple, indivisible substance distinct from the body, or do you live as though it were just a complex arrangement of matter?

Interpreting the Human Form as from the World of Command
The author interprets a statement about the human form being from the world of command and refutes an alternative interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible interpretations of the human form being from the 'world of command' and why it cannot be identified with the rational soul.

Core Idea
The human form is akin to immaterial beings but distinct from the rational soul.

In this passage, the author tackles an ambiguous statement from a work on ethics. He clarifies what it means for the human form to be from the 'world of command' — a term suggesting immateriality — and then refutes a common misinterpretation. This lesson will guide you through his analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Get the gist of the author's argument about the human form.
Pay attention to the key terms like 'عالم الأمر' and 'دفعي'.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The author offers two possible meanings for 'from the world of command': resemblance to immaterial beings and instantaneous existence. Note how each is defined.
3
Understand the refutation
The author rejects identifying the human form with the rational soul. Find the evidence he uses to refute this claim.
The refutation relies on the explicit text that the form prepares the body for the soul.
4
Review key terms
Review the Arabic key terms and their meanings to solidify your understanding of the argument.
Terms like 'الهيولي' (gradual) and 'دفعي' (instantaneous) are crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووقع في ديباجة الأخلاق العنصرية ما يشعر بأن على الصورة الإنسانية طراز عالم الأمر أي المجردات، وكأنه أراد أنها لغاية قربها من الكمالات وإعدادها البدن، لقبول تعلق النفس به شبيهة بالمجردات، وإن كانت حالة في المادة أو أراد بكونها من عالم الأمر أن وجودها دفعي لا الهيولي، وما لها من الأطوار في مدارج الاستكمال والاستعداد، وأما ما يقال من أنه أراد بها النفس الناطقة بدليل استشهاده بقوله تعالى: {يلقي الروح من أمره} فيكذبه تصريحه بأنها سبب لاستعداد البدن لتعلق النفس به، وأن النفس مبدأ لوجودها.
And it occurs in the preface of 'al-Akhlaq al-'Unsuriyya' something suggesting that the human form has the pattern of the world of command, i.e., the immaterial entities. It is as if he meant that due to its extreme closeness to perfections and its preparation of the body for the reception of the soul's attachment to it, it resembles immaterial entities, even though it is inherent in matter. Or he meant by its being from the world of command that its existence is instantaneous, not gradual, and it has no stages in the paths of perfection and preparation. As for what is said that he intended thereby the rational soul, as evidence by his citation of His saying: 'He casts the spirit from His command' — it is refuted by his explicit statement that it is the cause for the body's preparation for the soul's attachment, and that the soul is the principle of its existence.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage explains an ambiguous statement found in the preface of a work called 'al-Akhlaq al-'Unsuriyya' (Elementary Ethics) regarding the human form. The author notes that the statement suggests the human form has the pattern (طراز) of the 'world of command' (عالم الأمر), meaning immaterial entities (المجردات). He then offers two possible interpretations: first, that the human form is so close to perfections and prepares the body for the soul's attachment that it resembles immaterial beings despite being inherent in matter; second, that its existence is instantaneous (دفعي) rather than gradual (لا الهيولي), and it undergoes no stages in development. The author then refutes another interpretation that identifies the human form with the rational soul, citing the verse 'He casts the spirit from His command' as evidence. He rebuts this by pointing out that the same text explicitly states that the human form is the cause of the body's preparation for the soul's attachment, and that the soul is the principle of its existence — thus the form cannot be identical to the soul.

Correction: The human form is the preparation of the body for the soul, while the soul is the principle of its existence.

?

The Ruling on the Endurance and Annihilation of Bodies
States two rulings on bodies: endurance and annihilation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that bodies are enduring by a necessary rational judgment and are capable of annihilation, highlighting their contingency.

Core Idea
Bodies endure rationally, not merely by sensation, and are subject to annihilation.

In this brief but crucial passage, the author states the second ruling concerning bodies: they are enduring not merely as a matter of sensory experience but by a rationally necessary judgment. Additionally, bodies are capable of annihilation, meaning their existence is contingent upon God's power. This lesson unpacks these two points and their significance in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Notice the key terms 'باقية', 'ضرورة', 'بقاء', 'حس', and 'فناء'.
The passage is very concise; each word carries weight.
2
Identify the two rulings
The passage makes two distinct claims: (1) bodies are enduring by the judgment of necessity, not by mere sensory persistence; (2) bodies are capable of annihilation. Recognize that these are separate but related.
The first ruling distinguishes rational necessity from sensory observation.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Ask yourself: Why does it matter that bodies are enduring by necessity? Why is it important they are capable of annihilation? How does this relate to God's absolute power?
This ties back to the broader kalam emphasis on contingency and divine creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ومنها) أنها باقية بحكم الضرورة لا بمجرد البقاء في الحس، وقابلة للفناء.
He said: 'And among them (the rulings) is that they (bodies) are enduring by the judgment of necessity, not merely by continued existence in sensation, and they are capable of annihilation.'
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This is a brief passage that states the second ruling concerning bodies: they are enduring (baqiya) by necessity (bi-hukm al-darura), meaning that their continued existence is not merely a matter of sensory persistence but a rational judgment. The phrase 'by the judgment of necessity' indicates that reason confirms their endurance, independent of empirical observation. Additionally, bodies are said to be capable of annihilation (qabila li al-fana'), implying that their existence is not inherently eternal but subject to cessation by divine power. This ruling underscores the contingency of all material beings and aligns with the broader kalam emphasis on God's absolute power over creation.

Correction: The passage clarifies that their endurance is a rationally necessary judgment, not merely a sensory observation.

C) They are eternal and cannot be annihilated.
D) Their endurance is a matter of conventional agreement.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Contemplate the fact that your body is capable of annihilation (fanā'). Does this thought make you anxious, or does it drive you to seek what is permanent? If you were to lose your body tomorrow, what would remain of you that truly matters to God?

The Weak Arguments for Similarity of Bodies
Taftazani lists three weak arguments for the similarity of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main arguments for the similarity of bodies and why they are considered weak.

Core Idea
Similarity of bodies is not proven by weak arguments.

Taftazani begins by presenting the arguments that some have used to claim that all bodies are similar. These arguments are based on shared characteristics like distinctness and receptivity to accidents, but he dismisses them as unconvincing. Let's examine each argument and see why they fail.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the arguments presented.
Notice the phrase 'والكل ضعيف' at the end.
2
Identify the three arguments
Find and list the three distinct arguments Taftazani enumerates for the similarity of bodies.
They are separated by 'وبأن'.
3
Understand the refutation
Note that Taftazani dismisses all three as weak without further elaboration here.
Think about why each argument might be insufficient.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why proving similarity requires stronger evidence than these arguments provide.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخص يستدل بأن الأجسام متساوية في التميز، وقبول الأعراض، وذلك من صفات النفس، وبأن الجسم ينقسم الى الفلكي والعنصري بما لهما من الأقسام، ومورد القسمة مشترك، وبأن الأجسام يلتبس بعضها ببعض على تقدير الاستواء في الأعراض، ولولا تماثلها في نفسها لما كان كذلك، والكل ضعيف . ومن أفاضل الحكماء من توهم أن المراد بتماثلها اتحادها في مفهوم الجسم، وإن كانت هي أنواعاً مختلفة مندرجة تحته فتمسك بأن الحد الدال على ماهية الجسم على اختلاف عباراتهم فيه واحد عند كل قوم من غير وقوع قسمة فيه، فلذلك اتفق الكل على تماثله فإن المختلفات إذا جمعت في حد واحد وقع فيه التقسيم ضرورة كما يقال الجسم هو القابل للأبعاد الثلاثة والمشتمل عليها، فيعم الطبيعي والتعليمي، ومنشأ هذا التوهم استبعاد أن يذهب عاقل إلى أن الماء والنار حقيقة واحدة لا تختلف إلا بالعوارض كالإنسان دون الفصول والمنوعات كالحيوان كيف ولم يسمع نزاع في أن الجسم جنس بعيد ثم قال : وقول النظام يتخلفها لتخالف خواصها إنما يوجب تخالف الأنواع، لا تخالف المفهوم من [؟]
More specifically, it is argued that bodies are equal in distinctness (tamayyuz) and receptivity to accidents (a'rad), and that is from the attributes of the soul; and that the body is divided into celestial and elemental with their respective divisions, and the point of division is common; and that bodies become confused with each other on the assumption of equality in accidents, and if they were not similar in themselves it would not be so. All of this is weak. And among the distinguished philosophers was one who imagined that what is meant by their similarity is their unity in the concept of body, even though they are different species subsumed under it. So he adhered to the fact that the definition (hadd) indicating the quiddity of body is, despite the differences in their expressions, one with each group without any division occurring in it, therefore all agreed on its similarity. For if different things are gathered into one definition, division necessarily occurs in it, as it is said: 'Body is that which is receptive to three dimensions and comprehends them,' so it includes both the natural and the mathematical. The origin of this imagination is the unlikelihood that a rational person would go to the extent that water and fire are one reality, differing only by accidents like a human being, without differentiae and differentiate like animal. How so, and no dispute is heard that body is a remote genus. Then he said: 'The statement of al-Nazzam that they differ because their properties differ only necessitates the difference of species, not the difference of the concept from [?].'
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes arguments for the similarity (tamathul) of bodies. The author begins by stating that some argue for similarity based on bodies being alike in distinctness and in accepting accidents, which are attributes of the soul (i.e., mental considerations). They also argue that body is divided into celestial and elemental with shared divisions, and that bodies would be confused if they were equal in accidents unless they were similar in themselves. The author dismisses all these arguments as weak. Then he discusses a misunderstanding by a group of philosophers who thought that the similarity of bodies means they are identical in the concept of body, even though they are different species. They claim that the definition of body is one for all, without division, so all agree on its similarity. The author refutes this by pointing out that if different things were covered by one definition, division would necessarily occur, as in the definition 'body is receptive to three dimensions' which includes both natural and mathematical bodies. The source of this confusion is the unlikelihood that a rational person would claim that water and fire are the same reality, differing only by accidents like a human being (which differs by form) without specific differentiae. The author notes that no one disputes that body is a remote genus (jins ba'id). Finally, he mentions al-Nazzam's view that differences in properties only necessitate difference in species, not in the concept. The final word 'from [?]' is unclear in the source.

Key Terms
تماثل
جسم
عوارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bodies must be similar because they share some attributes.
Correction: Sharing attributes does not entail essential similarity; they could be accidental.

Checkpoint
What is Taftazani's overall assessment of the three arguments?
A) They are convincing.
B) They are weak.
C) They are partially true.
D) They are from philosophers.

Key Takeaway
The three common arguments for similarity of bodies are all weak and do not establish essential tamathul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a weak argument for a religious belief because it sounded logical? Identify one such belief in your life and commit to seeking stronger evidence.
?
The third argument suggests that if accidents were equal, bodies would be indistinguishable. Do you confuse accidental similarities (like appearance) with essential identity in how you judge others? How will you change this?
?
Taftazani dismisses all three arguments as weak. Are there arguments you rely on for your faith that might be weak? What step will you take to ground them in stronger proofs?

Refuting the Philosopher's Misunderstanding of Similarity
Taftazani refutes a philosopher's claim that similarity means identity in the concept of body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani refutes the philosopher's misconception that similarity means identity in the concept of body.

Core Idea
Similarity in the concept does not imply essential identity.

Taftazani now addresses a specific misunderstanding by a philosopher who thought that 'similarity of bodies' means they are identical in the concept of 'body', even though they are different species. This philosopher argued that the definition of body applies uniformly without division, so all bodies are similar. Taftazani refutes this and clarifies the correct understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosopher's claim
Read the passage where Taftazani presents the philosopher's view that similarity means unity in the concept of body.
Look for the phrase 'من توهم أن المراد بتماثلها اتحادها في مفهوم الجسم'.
2
Understand the refutation
Taftazani argues that if different species were under one definition, division must occur. He gives the example of the definition of body.
Notice the example of natural and mathematical bodies.
3
Analyze the source of error
The philosopher's error stems from thinking water and fire could be identical in essence differing only by accidents, which is unlikely.
Contrast with the human example where form differentiates.
4
Consider al-Nazzam's point
Taftazani mentions that differences in properties only necessitate difference in species, not in the generic concept.
The passage ends with unclear words, but the main idea is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخص يستدل بأن الأجسام متساوية في التميز، وقبول الأعراض، وذلك من صفات النفس، وبأن الجسم ينقسم الى الفلكي والعنصري بما لهما من الأقسام، ومورد القسمة مشترك، وبأن الأجسام يلتبس بعضها ببعض على تقدير الاستواء في الأعراض، ولولا تماثلها في نفسها لما كان كذلك، والكل ضعيف . ومن أفاضل الحكماء من توهم أن المراد بتماثلها اتحادها في مفهوم الجسم، وإن كانت هي أنواعاً مختلفة مندرجة تحته فتمسك بأن الحد الدال على ماهية الجسم على اختلاف عباراتهم فيه واحد عند كل قوم من غير وقوع قسمة فيه، فلذلك اتفق الكل على تماثله فإن المختلفات إذا جمعت في حد واحد وقع فيه التقسيم ضرورة كما يقال الجسم هو القابل للأبعاد الثلاثة والمشتمل عليها، فيعم الطبيعي والتعليمي، ومنشأ هذا التوهم استبعاد أن يذهب عاقل إلى أن الماء والنار حقيقة واحدة لا تختلف إلا بالعوارض كالإنسان دون الفصول والمنوعات كالحيوان كيف ولم يسمع نزاع في أن الجسم جنس بعيد ثم قال : وقول النظام يتخلفها لتخالف خواصها إنما يوجب تخالف الأنواع، لا تخالف المفهوم من [؟]
More specifically, it is argued that bodies are equal in distinctness (tamayyuz) and receptivity to accidents (a'rad), and that is from the attributes of the soul; and that the body is divided into celestial and elemental with their respective divisions, and the point of division is common; and that bodies become confused with each other on the assumption of equality in accidents, and if they were not similar in themselves it would not be so. All of this is weak. And among the distinguished philosophers was one who imagined that what is meant by their similarity is their unity in the concept of body, even though they are different species subsumed under it. So he adhered to the fact that the definition (hadd) indicating the quiddity of body is, despite the differences in their expressions, one with each group without any division occurring in it, therefore all agreed on its similarity. For if different things are gathered into one definition, division necessarily occurs in it, as it is said: 'Body is that which is receptive to three dimensions and comprehends them,' so it includes both the natural and the mathematical. The origin of this imagination is the unlikelihood that a rational person would go to the extent that water and fire are one reality, differing only by accidents like a human being, without differentiae and differentiate like animal. How so, and no dispute is heard that body is a remote genus. Then he said: 'The statement of al-Nazzam that they differ because their properties differ only necessitates the difference of species, not the difference of the concept from [?].'
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes arguments for the similarity (tamathul) of bodies. The author begins by stating that some argue for similarity based on bodies being alike in distinctness and in accepting accidents, which are attributes of the soul (i.e., mental considerations). They also argue that body is divided into celestial and elemental with shared divisions, and that bodies would be confused if they were equal in accidents unless they were similar in themselves. The author dismisses all these arguments as weak. Then he discusses a misunderstanding by a group of philosophers who thought that the similarity of bodies means they are identical in the concept of body, even though they are different species. They claim that the definition of body is one for all, without division, so all agree on its similarity. The author refutes this by pointing out that if different things were covered by one definition, division would necessarily occur, as in the definition 'body is receptive to three dimensions' which includes both natural and mathematical bodies. The source of this confusion is the unlikelihood that a rational person would claim that water and fire are the same reality, differing only by accidents like a human being (which differs by form) without specific differentiae. The author notes that no one disputes that body is a remote genus (jins ba'id). Finally, he mentions al-Nazzam's view that differences in properties only necessitate difference in species, not in the concept. The final word 'from [?]' is unclear in the source.

جنس
تماثل
نظام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Similarity of bodies means they are all identical in essence.
Correction: They share the concept of body at the generic level, but differ as species.

Checkpoint
What example does Taftazani use to show that different species fall under one definition?
A) Water and fire
B) Celestial and elemental bodies
C) Natural and mathematical bodies
D) Human and animal

Key Takeaway
Bodies are similar only in the generic concept of 'body', but they differ as species, so essential identity is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever mistakenly believed that two things are exactly the same because they share a common label or definition, ignoring their fundamental differences? How can Taftazani's argument help you avoid this logical error in your own thinking?
?
When you read about the Oneness of God (tawheed), do you ever unconsciously equate it with the oneness of created things? How does this lesson's distinction between similarity and identity challenge you to refine your understanding of divine unity?

The Definition of Al-Mutanahi (Finite)
Lexical footnote defining 'al-mutanahi' with examples and Ibn Sina's quotations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of 'al-mutanahi' (finite) as it relates to integers, real numbers, and magnitudes, and how it is opposed to the infinite.

Core Idea
Finite means having an end and being measurable — the opposite of the infinite.

This passage defines a key term in Islamic logic and kalam: al-mutanahi (the finite). It goes beyond a simple dictionary definition by showing how finitude is understood in arithmetic (integers, real numbers) and geometry (magnitudes), and concludes with Ibn Sina's authoritative clarification. Understanding finitude is essential before tackling debates about the infinite, especially in arguments about the world's createdness.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation of the opening sentence. Note that 'finite' is defined as having an end (nihaya) and being measurable (yuqas).
2
Identify the three types of finite things
The passage gives examples: finite integers, finite real numbers, and finite magnitudes. See how each is defined in terms of measurement.
Notice the progression from discrete numbers to continuous magnitudes.
3
Consider Ibn Sina's remarks
Read the three quotations from Ibn Sina. They clarify that finitude is about limitation in itself, not just in relation to something else.
The last quotation defines 'end' (nihaya) as the boundary beyond which no increase is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتناهي : ما له نهاية ويمكن قياسه. يقال لعدد صحيح إنه متناه إذا أمكن الحصول عليه باضافة الواحد الى نفسه إما مرة واحدة وإما مرات متكررة تكون احداها ويقال للعدد الحقيقي إنه متناه اذا كان أقل من عدد صحيح متناه، ويقال للمقدار إنه متناه إذا أمكن قياسه بالنسبة الى مقدار من نوعه بعدد حقيقي متناه . والمتناهي : هو المحدود قال ابن سينا وأما السطح فليس هو داخلا في حد الجسم من حيث هو جسم، بل من حيث هو متناه (راجع النجاة ص ۳۲۷) وقال أيضاً: من قال إنه متناه عنى أنه محدود في نفسه . (راجع الشفاء ص ۱۲ - ج۱) قال ابن سينا النهاية هي ما به يصير الشيء ذو الكمية الى حيث لا يوجد وراءه مزاد شيء فيه (راجع رسالة الحدود (۹۲) والمتناهي : نقيض اللامتناهي .
Al-mutanahi (the finite): that which has an end and can be measured. An integer is said to be finite if it can be obtained by adding one to itself either once or repeatedly such that one of them is reached. A real number is said to be finite if it is less than a finite integer. A magnitude is said to be finite if it can be measured relative to a magnitude of its type by a finite real number. And al-mutanahi: it is the limited. Ibn Sina said: 'The surface is not included in the definition of body insofar as it is body, but insofar as it is finite.' (Refer to al-Najat p. 327) And he also said: 'He who says it is finite means that it is limited in itself.' (Refer to al-Shifa p. 12, vol. 1) Ibn Sina said: 'The end is that by which a thing possessing quantity reaches a point beyond which there is no increase of something in it.' (Refer to Risalah al-Hudud (92)) And al-mutanahi is the opposite of al-lamutanahi (the infinite).
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This is a lexical footnote defining the term 'al-mutanahi' (finite). It explains that something is finite if it has an end and can be measured. It gives examples from the domains of integers, real numbers, and magnitudes. It clarifies that 'al-mutanahi' means 'al-mahdud' (limited). It then quotes Ibn Sina from multiple works: from al-Najat clarifying that the surface is not part of the definition of body as body but only as finite; from al-Shifa stating that 'finite' means limited in itself; and from Risalah al-Hudud defining 'end' (al-nihaya) as the point where no further increase can occur in a quantity. It concludes by stating that 'al-mutanahi' is the opposite of 'al-lamutanahi' (the infinite).

Key Terms
متناه
نهاية
محدود
لا متناه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Finite only means 'having an end' without requiring measurability.
Correction: The text states 'that which has an end and can be measured' (ما له نهاية ويمكن قياسه), so measurability is an essential part of the definition.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of al-mutanahi according to the footnote?
A) That which has no end
B) That which has an end and can be measured
C) That which is indivisible
D) That which is uncreated

Key Takeaway
Al-mutanahi (the finite) is defined as that which has an end and can be measured, encompassing integers, real numbers, and magnitudes, and it is the opposite of the infinite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything finite has an end, including your time in this world, what are you doing today that will not last? And what are you doing that will?
?
You believe that Allah alone is infinite. Are there areas of your life where you are acting as if something finite (wealth, relationships, health) is infinite? How would your daily choices change if you truly saw them as limited?
?
Ibn Sina defines end as 'the point beyond which there is no increase.' Is there a pursuit in your life that you're trying to increase without end, but which will never satisfy? What would it look like to acknowledge its limit and turn to the only infinite source?
