Shape and Place: Necessary Attributes of Finite Bodies
Taftazani states necessary truths and contrasts Ash'ari and philosophers' doctrines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational necessity of shape and place for finite bodies and the contrasting views of the Ash'ari school and the philosophers on the source of their specificity.

Core Idea
Every finite body must have a shape and a place, but their precise forms are divinely determined.

Al-Taftazani begins by establishing two necessary truths: every finite body has a shape, and every body requires a place (void) that it occupies. These are considered self-evident but are mentioned here because they are foundational for later discussions and because there is disagreement about the source of their specific forms. The Ash'ari school holds that God, the Powerful Chooser, assigns each body its particular shape and place, while the philosophers maintain that bodies have natural shapes and places determined by their essences.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation to understand the literal meaning.
Notice the definitions: shape is the boundary's form; place is a void occupied.
2
Identify the two schools' positions
Locate where al-Taftazani presents the Ash'ari view (attributing specificities to God) and the philosophers' view (natural shape and place).
The phrase 'ثم استناد خصوصيات الأشكال والأحياز إلى القادر المختار هو المذهب عندنا' is key.
3
Understand the definition of natural shape/place
The philosophers define natural as that which a body takes when free from external constraints. This distinguishes it from forced states.
This definition allows them to argue that shape and place are essential, not accidental.
4
Reflect on the need for this discussion
Al-Taftazani explains that necessary judgments are only discussed when clarification or defense against errors is needed.
This justifies bringing up what seems obvious.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنه متناه على ما سيجيء وكل متناه فله شكل إذ لا معنى له سوى هيئة إحاطة النهاية بالجسم ، وأما الافتقار إلى الحيز بمعنى فراغ يشغله فضروري، وإنما يذكر هو وأمثاله من الأحكام الضرورية في المباحث العلمية من حيث يفتقر إلى تنبيه أو زيادة تحقيق وتفصيل أو تعقيب تفريع، أو يقع فيه خلاف من شرذمة، ثم استناد خصوصيات الأشكال والأحياز إلى القادر المختار هو المذهب عندنا كما سيجيء، وذهبت الفلاسفة إلى أن لكل جسم شكلاً طبيعياً، وحيزاً طبيعياً، لأنه عند الخلو عن جميع القواسر والأسباب الخارجة يكون بالضرورة على شكل معين، في حيز معين، وهو المعنى بالطبيعي، وعلى هذا لا يرد الاعتراض بأنه يجوز أن يقتضي شكلاً ما، وحيزاً ما، ككل جزء من أجزاء الأرض، وتستند الخصوصية إلى سبب خارج كارادة القادر المختار لا يقال لعل من الأسباب ما هو من لوازم ماهيته ، فيكون فرض الخلو عند فرض محال ، فيجوز أن يستلزم محالاً هو الخلو عن الشكل والحيز لأنا نقول : ما يقتضيه لازم الماهية يكون طبيعياً لا قسرياً، وهو ظاهر ولم يريدوا بالحيز ههنا المكان بمعنى السطح الباطن من الحاوي، حتى يرد الاعتراض بأن الجسم قد لا يكون له محل كالمحدد، فضلاً أن يكون طبيعياً، ولا الفراغ الذي يشغله الجسم لما قال ابن سينا: إن كل جسم له حيز طبيعي، فإن كان ذا مكان كان حيزه مكاناً، وقال أيضاً لا جسم إلا وله حيز ، إما مكان، وإما وضع ، وإما ترتيب.
فإن قيل : الاختصاص بالحيز الطبيعي كما أنه ليس معللاً بالأسباب الخارجية ، كذلك ليس معللاً بالجسمية ولوازمها لا بد من خصوصية، فينقل الكلام إليها ويتسلسل قلنا : قد سبق في بحث الصور النوعية ما يزيل هذا الإشكال، واتفقوا على أن الحيز الطبيعي لا يكون إلا واحداً لأن مقتضى الواحد واحد، ولأنه لو تعدد فعند عدم القاسر إما أن يحصل فيهما وهو محال بالضرورة، أو في أحدهما فلا يكون الآخر طبيعياً، وأيضاً إذا بقي خارجاً بالقسر، فعند زوال القاسر إما أن يتوجه إليهما وهو محال، أو إلى أحدهما وفيه ميل عن الآخر فيصير المطلوب بالطبع مهروباً بالطبع ، أو لا يتوجه إلى شيء منهما ، فلا يكون شيء منهما طبيعياً، لا يقال يجوز أن يكون الحصول في أحدهما أو الميل إليه بحسب ما يتفق من الأسباب المخصصة، مانعاً من الآخر، لأنا نقول الكلام فيما إذا فرض خالياً عن الأسباب الخارجة نعم يرد عليه أنه يجوز أن لا يحصل في أحدهما، ولا يتوجه إليه لامتناع الترجح بلا مرجح ، وكون كل مانعاً من الآخر، بل يبقى حيث وجد. وجعل صاحب المواقف إثبات الحيز الطبيعي من فروع القول بالهيولي نظراً إلى أن القائلين بالجزء يجعلون الأجسام متماثلة ، لا تختلف إلا بالعوارض .
Because it is finite as will come, and every finite [body] has a shape, since shape means nothing other than the form of the boundary's encompassing of the body. As for the need for a place (ḥayyiz) in the sense of a void that it occupies, it is necessary; but this and similar necessary judgments are mentioned in scientific investigations only insofar as they require a reminder, or additional verification and detail, or consequential derivation, or there occurs a disagreement by a faction. Then, the attribution of the specificities of shapes and places to the Powerful Chooser (al-Qādir al-Mukhtār) is our school as will come. The philosophers (al-falāsifa) held that every body has a natural shape and a natural place, because when free from all constraints and external causes, it is necessarily in a specific shape and a specific place, and that is what is meant by 'natural'. On this [view] the objection does not apply that it [the body] could have some shape or other and some place or other, like every part of the earth, and the specificity is attributed to an external cause such as the will of the Powerful Chooser. It cannot be said: 'Perhaps among the causes is something that is a necessary concomitant of its essence, so the assumption of freedom [from external causes] would be an assumption of an impossibility, and thus it may entail an impossibility, namely freedom from shape and place,' for we say: What is necessitated by the necessary concomitant of the essence is natural, not forced, and that is evident. And they did not mean by 'place' here 'location' in the sense of the inner surface of the container, such that the objection would apply that a body may have no location like a point, let alone a natural one, nor the void that the body occupies. For Ibn Sīnā said: 'Every body has a natural place; if it has a location, its place is a location.' He also said: 'There is no body except that it has a place — either a location, or a position, or an arrangement.'
If it is said: 'The specification to a natural place, just as it is not caused by external causes, so also it is not caused by body-ness and its concomitants, so there must be a specificity, and the discussion would be transferred to it and it would regress endlessly,' we reply: There has already preceded in the discussion of specific forms what removes this difficulty. And they agreed that natural place can only be one, because the requirement of the one is one; and because if it were multiple, then in the absence of a constraint either it [the body] would occur in both, which is impossible by necessity, or in one of them, then the other would not be natural. Also, if it remains outside by force, then when the force ceases, either it moves toward both, which is impossible, or toward one of them while having an inclination away from the other, then the desired by nature becomes fled from by nature, or it does not move toward either, so none of them is natural. It cannot be said: 'It is possible that the occurrence in one of them or the inclination toward it, according to what happens to be the specifying causes, prevents the other,' because we are speaking about when it is assumed free from external causes. Yes, it is objected that it is possible that it does not occur in either and does not incline toward either because of the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator, and each prevents the other, but it remains where it was. And the author of al-Mawāqif (al-Ījī) made the proof of natural place a branch of the affirmation of hyle (al-hayūlā), considering that the proponents of the atom (al-juz') make bodies similar, not differing except by accidents.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument about natural shape and place. It begins by stating that every finite body has a shape (shakl), as shape is simply the form of the boundary enclosing the body. The need for a place (ḥayyiz) in the sense of a void the body occupies is considered a necessary judgment. These are usually mentioned only when requiring clarification or when there is disagreement. The author then presents the Ash'ari position (our school) that the specificities of shapes and places are attributed to the Powerful Chooser (God), while the philosophers (falāsifa) hold that each body has a natural shape and place determined by its nature, independent of external causes. The author then addresses an objection: one might argue that a body could have any shape or place, with the specific one being from an external cause like God's will; but this is consistent with the philosophers' view because if the natural shape is not specified, then the body would lack a definite nature. The philosophers deny that the essence alone necessitates a specific shape; rather, it is due to a necessary concomitant of the essence (lāzim al-māhiyya). The author clarifies that what is necessitated by a concomitant of the essence is natural, not forced. Then he defines ḥayyiz: it is not merely the inner surface of a container, nor the void (the philosophers, especially Ibn Sīnā, hold that every body has a ḥayyiz—either a place (makān), a position (waḍ‘), or an arrangement (tartīb)). Then there is an objection: if natural place is not caused by external causes nor by the body's nature, then it must be caused by something else, leading to infinite regress. The author replies that this difficulty was resolved earlier in the discussion of specific forms (ṣuwar naw‘iyya). Then he proves that natural place must be unique: because the requirement of the one (body) is one; if there were multiple natural places, the body would occupy impossible contradictions when free from constraints. Finally, al-Ījī (author of al-Mawāqif) is cited, who considers the proof for natural place to depend on the theory of hyle (matter), since atomists view all bodies as similar, differing only by accidents.

حيز
حيز طبيعي
قادر مختار
فلاسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That natural shape and place are inherent properties of the body independent of God's determination.
Correction: The Ash'ari school holds that the specific forms of shapes and places are directly from God, while the philosophers' 'natural' is also ultimately dependent on God as the Creator of essences.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Ash'ari view on the specific shapes and places of bodies?
A) They are determined by the body's natural essence.
B) They are determined by the Powerful Chooser (God).
C) They are purely random.
D) They are determined by the void.

Key Takeaway
Every finite body necessarily has a shape and a place; the Ash'ari school attributes their specific forms to God's will, while philosophers attribute them to the body's nature.

The Uniqueness of Natural Place and Refutation of Objections
Taftazani refutes objections to natural place and proves its uniqueness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments that a natural place must be unique and how al-Taftazani handles objections about causation and infinite regress.

Core Idea
Natural place is unique: a body cannot have two natural places because that would entail contradiction.

Having introduced the concept of natural place, al-Taftazani now addresses potential objections. He answers the charge that the specification of natural place leads to infinite regress, then proves that natural place must be single. He also connects this discussion to the broader debate with atomists who deny the reality of hyle (matter).

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and reply
Read the objection that if natural place is not caused by essence or externals, the specification would regress infinitely. Note the brief reply referencing previous discussion.
The reply is: 'قد سبق في بحث الصور النوعية ما يزيل هذا الإشكال' – this refers to earlier arguments about specific forms.
2
Understand the uniqueness proof
Study the two modal arguments: (1) if two natural places, impossible occurrence in both; (2) argument about inclination when free from constraints.
These arguments assume the body is free from external causes.
3
Consider a possible response and its refutation
The author anticipates and refutes a response that chance or specifying causes could explain multiplicity, but this violates the assumption of freedom from external causes.
4
Reflect on the connection to hyle
Note al-Ījī's point that proving natural place depends on accepting hyle, which atomists reject.
This shows the philosophical commitment underlying the argument.

لأنه متناه على ما سيجيء وكل متناه فله شكل إذ لا معنى له سوى هيئة إحاطة النهاية بالجسم ، وأما الافتقار إلى الحيز بمعنى فراغ يشغله فضروري، وإنما يذكر هو وأمثاله من الأحكام الضرورية في المباحث العلمية من حيث يفتقر إلى تنبيه أو زيادة تحقيق وتفصيل أو تعقيب تفريع، أو يقع فيه خلاف من شرذمة، ثم استناد خصوصيات الأشكال والأحياز إلى القادر المختار هو المذهب عندنا كما سيجيء، وذهبت الفلاسفة إلى أن لكل جسم شكلاً طبيعياً، وحيزاً طبيعياً، لأنه عند الخلو عن جميع القواسر والأسباب الخارجة يكون بالضرورة على شكل معين، في حيز معين، وهو المعنى بالطبيعي، وعلى هذا لا يرد الاعتراض بأنه يجوز أن يقتضي شكلاً ما، وحيزاً ما، ككل جزء من أجزاء الأرض، وتستند الخصوصية إلى سبب خارج كارادة القادر المختار لا يقال لعل من الأسباب ما هو من لوازم ماهيته ، فيكون فرض الخلو عند فرض محال ، فيجوز أن يستلزم محالاً هو الخلو عن الشكل والحيز لأنا نقول : ما يقتضيه لازم الماهية يكون طبيعياً لا قسرياً، وهو ظاهر ولم يريدوا بالحيز ههنا المكان بمعنى السطح الباطن من الحاوي، حتى يرد الاعتراض بأن الجسم قد لا يكون له محل كالمحدد، فضلاً أن يكون طبيعياً، ولا الفراغ الذي يشغله الجسم لما قال ابن سينا: إن كل جسم له حيز طبيعي، فإن كان ذا مكان كان حيزه مكاناً، وقال أيضاً لا جسم إلا وله حيز ، إما مكان، وإما وضع ، وإما ترتيب.
فإن قيل : الاختصاص بالحيز الطبيعي كما أنه ليس معللاً بالأسباب الخارجية ، كذلك ليس معللاً بالجسمية ولوازمها لا بد من خصوصية، فينقل الكلام إليها ويتسلسل قلنا : قد سبق في بحث الصور النوعية ما يزيل هذا الإشكال، واتفقوا على أن الحيز الطبيعي لا يكون إلا واحداً لأن مقتضى الواحد واحد، ولأنه لو تعدد فعند عدم القاسر إما أن يحصل فيهما وهو محال بالضرورة، أو في أحدهما فلا يكون الآخر طبيعياً، وأيضاً إذا بقي خارجاً بالقسر، فعند زوال القاسر إما أن يتوجه إليهما وهو محال، أو إلى أحدهما وفيه ميل عن الآخر فيصير المطلوب بالطبع مهروباً بالطبع ، أو لا يتوجه إلى شيء منهما ، فلا يكون شيء منهما طبيعياً، لا يقال يجوز أن يكون الحصول في أحدهما أو الميل إليه بحسب ما يتفق من الأسباب المخصصة، مانعاً من الآخر، لأنا نقول الكلام فيما إذا فرض خالياً عن الأسباب الخارجة نعم يرد عليه أنه يجوز أن لا يحصل في أحدهما، ولا يتوجه إليه لامتناع الترجح بلا مرجح ، وكون كل مانعاً من الآخر، بل يبقى حيث وجد. وجعل صاحب المواقف إثبات الحيز الطبيعي من فروع القول بالهيولي نظراً إلى أن القائلين بالجزء يجعلون الأجسام متماثلة ، لا تختلف إلا بالعوارض .
Because it is finite as will come, and every finite [body] has a shape, since shape means nothing other than the form of the boundary's encompassing of the body. As for the need for a place (ḥayyiz) in the sense of a void that it occupies, it is necessary; but this and similar necessary judgments are mentioned in scientific investigations only insofar as they require a reminder, or additional verification and detail, or consequential derivation, or there occurs a disagreement by a faction. Then, the attribution of the specificities of shapes and places to the Powerful Chooser (al-Qādir al-Mukhtār) is our school as will come. The philosophers (al-falāsifa) held that every body has a natural shape and a natural place, because when free from all constraints and external causes, it is necessarily in a specific shape and a specific place, and that is what is meant by 'natural'. On this [view] the objection does not apply that it [the body] could have some shape or other and some place or other, like every part of the earth, and the specificity is attributed to an external cause such as the will of the Powerful Chooser. It cannot be said: 'Perhaps among the causes is something that is a necessary concomitant of its essence, so the assumption of freedom [from external causes] would be an assumption of an impossibility, and thus it may entail an impossibility, namely freedom from shape and place,' for we say: What is necessitated by the necessary concomitant of the essence is natural, not forced, and that is evident. And they did not mean by 'place' here 'location' in the sense of the inner surface of the container, such that the objection would apply that a body may have no location like a point, let alone a natural one, nor the void that the body occupies. For Ibn Sīnā said: 'Every body has a natural place; if it has a location, its place is a location.' He also said: 'There is no body except that it has a place — either a location, or a position, or an arrangement.'
If it is said: 'The specification to a natural place, just as it is not caused by external causes, so also it is not caused by body-ness and its concomitants, so there must be a specificity, and the discussion would be transferred to it and it would regress endlessly,' we reply: There has already preceded in the discussion of specific forms what removes this difficulty. And they agreed that natural place can only be one, because the requirement of the one is one; and because if it were multiple, then in the absence of a constraint either it [the body] would occur in both, which is impossible by necessity, or in one of them, then the other would not be natural. Also, if it remains outside by force, then when the force ceases, either it moves toward both, which is impossible, or toward one of them while having an inclination away from the other, then the desired by nature becomes fled from by nature, or it does not move toward either, so none of them is natural. It cannot be said: 'It is possible that the occurrence in one of them or the inclination toward it, according to what happens to be the specifying causes, prevents the other,' because we are speaking about when it is assumed free from external causes. Yes, it is objected that it is possible that it does not occur in either and does not incline toward either because of the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator, and each prevents the other, but it remains where it was. And the author of al-Mawāqif (al-Ījī) made the proof of natural place a branch of the affirmation of hyle (al-hayūlā), considering that the proponents of the atom (al-juz') make bodies similar, not differing except by accidents.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument about natural shape and place. It begins by stating that every finite body has a shape (shakl), as shape is simply the form of the boundary enclosing the body. The need for a place (ḥayyiz) in the sense of a void the body occupies is considered a necessary judgment. These are usually mentioned only when requiring clarification or when there is disagreement. The author then presents the Ash'ari position (our school) that the specificities of shapes and places are attributed to the Powerful Chooser (God), while the philosophers (falāsifa) hold that each body has a natural shape and place determined by its nature, independent of external causes. The author then addresses an objection: one might argue that a body could have any shape or place, with the specific one being from an external cause like God's will; but this is consistent with the philosophers' view because if the natural shape is not specified, then the body would lack a definite nature. The philosophers deny that the essence alone necessitates a specific shape; rather, it is due to a necessary concomitant of the essence (lāzim al-māhiyya). The author clarifies that what is necessitated by a concomitant of the essence is natural, not forced. Then he defines ḥayyiz: it is not merely the inner surface of a container, nor the void (the philosophers, especially Ibn Sīnā, hold that every body has a ḥayyiz—either a place (makān), a position (waḍ‘), or an arrangement (tartīb)). Then there is an objection: if natural place is not caused by external causes nor by the body's nature, then it must be caused by something else, leading to infinite regress. The author replies that this difficulty was resolved earlier in the discussion of specific forms (ṣuwar naw‘iyya). Then he proves that natural place must be unique: because the requirement of the one (body) is one; if there were multiple natural places, the body would occupy impossible contradictions when free from constraints. Finally, al-Ījī (author of al-Mawāqif) is cited, who considers the proof for natural place to depend on the theory of hyle (matter), since atomists view all bodies as similar, differing only by accidents.

تسلسل

جزء
قادر مختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a body could have multiple natural places or that natural place is not definable without infinite regress.
Correction: Natural place must be single; the specification problem is resolved by appealing to specific forms, not infinite regress.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can natural place only be one?
A) Because God's power is limited.
B) Because a body cannot occupy two places at once when free from constraints.
C) Because the philosophers said so.
D) Because place is defined as a void.

Key Takeaway
Natural place is necessarily unique; objections about causation are resolved, and the proof assumes the reality of hyle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that a body cannot have two natural places—doing so leads to logical contradiction. In your spiritual life, do you find yourself pulled towards two contradictory purposes, like seeking both worldly approval and Allah's pleasure? Which one is truly your 'natural place'? What would it take to remove the constraints that keep you from returning to that single, pure inclination?
?
Al-Taftazani argues that when external constraints are removed, a body must return to its unique natural place. When you strip away all worldly distractions, expectations, and peer pressure, what does your heart naturally long for? Are you truly free, or are you held captive by desires that masquerade as natural inclinations?
?
The proof relies on the reality of hyle—a deeper substance beneath accidents. What is the 'hyle' of your soul—the unchanging core that defines who you are before Allah? Have you shaped your life around the accidents of status and achievement, neglecting the essence that only He knows? How will you realign today?

Impossibility of Opposite Attributes in a Body
Al-Taftazani states the impossibility of simultaneous opposites in a body and notes differing opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological claim that a body cannot simultaneously possess an attribute and its opposite, and the varying positions on this issue.

Core Idea
A body cannot hold two opposite attributes at once.

In this passage, al-Taftazani presents a key principle in kalam: a body cannot simultaneously have an attribute and its opposite. He notes that some thinkers allowed this possibility in certain contexts, while others (the majority) rejected it. The lesson begins by laying out the claim and the differing views, setting the stage for the proof that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim and positions
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the core claim and the list of groups who held different views on the timing or scope of the possibility.
The claim is about 'the body' as a whole, not parts.
2
Identify the groups mentioned
Note the philosophers, the Mu'tazila (some absolutely, some only in akwān, some in non-akwān), and the rejectors (al-māniʿūn). Understand the nuance.
The Mu'tazila are divided; not all of them held the same opinion.
3
Link to the proof beginning
The passage ends with the start of the proof: 'the body is never free from motion and rest...' This shows how the rejectors argue from necessity.
The proof is incomplete here, but the direction is clear.
4
Reflect on why this matters
This principle is foundational for arguments about divine attributes and the nature of bodies. Understanding it helps grasp later kalam discussions.

Source Text

قال (ومنها أنه يمتنع) جميع على الجسم من العرض وضده، وجوزه بعض الفلاسفة في الأزل وبمقر المعتزلة، فيما لا يزال مطلقاً، وبعضهم في الأكوان، وبعضهم في غير الأكوان. أحتج المانعون بأنها لا تخلو عن الحركة والسكون وعن الاجتماع
He said: 'And among them is that it is impossible for a body to simultaneously have an attribute and its opposite.' And some philosophers permitted it in eternity, and some Mu'tazila permitted it in the future absolutely, and some of them permitted it in the category of 'being in a state' (akwān), and some of them permitted it in other than 'being in a state.' The rejectors argued that it [the body] is never free from motion and rest and from combination...
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage introduces a new argument (one of the arguments) concerning physical properties. It asserts the impossibility of a body simultaneously having an attribute (ʿaraḍ) and its opposite (ḍidd). The author notes that some philosophers (falāsifa) allowed this possibility in eternity (azal), while some Mu'tazila allowed it in the future (mā lā yazāl) without restriction, and others among them allowed it only for certain types of accidents called akwān (states such as motion, rest, combination, separation), while others allowed it for non-akwān accidents. The supporters of impossibility (al-māniʿūn) then begin their proof: they argue that a body is never free from motion and rest and from combination... (the proof is cut off here as the window ends). This lesson presents the claim and the start of the proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

In your own life, do you ever try to hold contradictory beliefs or commitments, convincing yourself they can coexist? What contradiction in your faith or actions is the lesson's principle calling you to resolve today?

?
The passage shows deep disagreement even among scholars of the same tradition. When you encounter a truth that is contested, do you tend to accept it without examination or reject it without evidence? How can you emulate the rejectors' demand for proof in seeking clarity about your own convictions?

The Three Views on Accidents in Bodies
Classifies three views on accidents in bodies with subgroups.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three positions on the necessity of accidents in bodies and the two Mu'tazilite subgroups that specify particular types of accidents.

Core Idea
Three views: total affirmation, total negation, partial affirmation with two subgroups.

The author begins by classifying the possible positions regarding whether bodies must possess accidents. He identifies three logical possibilities: total affirmation, total negation, and partial affirmation. He then explains that within partial affirmation, two schools of the Mu'tazila specify which accidents are necessary: the Baghdad school specifying colors, and the Basran school specifying spatial states (motion/rest, conjunction/separation). This classification sets the stage for evaluating the proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three views
Read the Arabic passage and locate the three views: total affirmation, total negation, and partial affirmation. Notice how each is defined.
The author presents partial affirmation as the most plausible.
2
Notice the two subgroups
Within partial affirmation, the author mentions two Mu'tazilite groups. Identify which accidents each group requires and their names.
The Baghdad Mu'tazila specify colors; the Basran Mu'tazila specify spatial states.
3
Understand the logical relationship
Reflect on why partial affirmation is a middle position between total affirmation and total negation. Think about what 'partial' means here.
It means every body must have some accident, but not necessarily all types.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمرجع الأول إلى إيجاب كلي - والثاني إلى سلب كلي، والأشبه هو الإيجاب الجزئي بمعنى أنه يجب أن يوجد في كل جسم شيء من الأعراض، إلا أن القائلين بالتفصيل منهم من خصه بالألوان بمعنى أنه يجب أن يوجد فيه شيء من الألوان وهم المعتزلة البغدادية، ومنهم من خصه بالأكوان بمعنى أنه يجب أن يوجد فيه الحركة أو السكون ، والاجتماع، أو الافتراق وهم البصرية، واحتجاج المانعين بأن الجسم متحقق في الزمان ومتكثر بالعدد فلا يخلو عن حركة أو سكون، واجتماع أو اقتراق على تقدير تمامه إنما يفيد هذا الإيجاب الجزئي لا الإيجاب الكلي المدعي. نعم يصلح للرد على القائلين بالسلب الكلي، وعلى البغدادية القائلين بجواز الخلو عما عدا الألوان، وكذا احتجاجهم بأن الشيء لا يوجد بدون التشخص ضرورة، وتشخص الأجسام إنما هو بالأعراض لكونها متماثلة لتألفها من الجواهر المتماثلة، فلو وجدت بدون الأعراض لزم وجود الغير المتشخص وهو محال لا يفيد العموم. أعني امتناع الجسم بدون أحد الضدين من كل عرض، لأن البعض كاف في التشخص . نعم يفيد عموم الأوقات. أعني الأزل، وما لا يزال بخلاف الأول فإنه ربما يمنع امتناع خلو الجسم في الأزل عن الحركة والسكون بل إنما يكون ذلك في الزمان الثاني والثالث وعن الاجتماع والافتراق، بل إنما يكون ذلك على تقدير تحقق جسم آخر، فيحتاج في التعميم إلى قياس ما قبل الاتصاف أعني الأزل على ما بعده، أعني ما لا يزال كما يقاس بعض الأعراض على البعض تعميماً للدليلين في جميع الأعراض، وتقريره أن اتصاف الجوهر بالعرض إما لذاته وإما لقابليته له ونسبة كل منهما إلى جميع الأعراض والأزمان على السوية والجواب منع المقدمتين.
Thus the first reduces to total affirmation, and the second to total negation, and the more plausible is partial affirmation, meaning that it is necessary that in every body something of accidents exist, except that those who make detail – among them some specify it with colors, meaning that it is necessary that something of colors exist in it, and they are the Baghdad Mu'tazila; and among them some specify it with spatial states, meaning that it is necessary that motion or rest, and conjunction or separation, exist in it, and they are the Basran Mu'tazila. And the proof of the objectors that the body is realized in time and multiplied in number, so it is not devoid of motion or rest, and conjunction or separation, upon the assumption of its completion, only yields this partial affirmation, not the total affirmation claimed. Yes, it is suitable for refuting those who affirm total negation, and the Baghdad school who affirm permissibility of being devoid of other than colors. Likewise their proof that a thing does not exist without individuation necessarily, and the individuation of bodies is only by accidents because they are similar, being composed of similar atoms; so if they existed without accidents, there would necessarily be the existence of the unindividuated, which is impossible – this does not yield generality. I mean the impossibility of a body without one of the two opposites from every accident, because some suffice for individuation. Yes, it yields generality of times: I mean pre-eternity and perpetuity, unlike the first, for it perhaps prevents the impossibility of a body being devoid in pre-eternity of motion and rest; rather that only occurs in the second and third time, and of conjunction and separation, rather that only occurs upon the assumption of the existence of another body. So generalization requires analogizing the state prior to qualification, i.e. pre-eternity, to what comes after it, i.e. perpetuity, just as some accidents are analogized to others to generalize both proofs to all accidents. And its formulation is that the attribution of a substance to an accident is either by its essence or by its capacity, and the relation of each to all accidents and times is uniform. And the answer is denial of the two premises.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a classification of views on the necessity of accidents in bodies. The author first states that the first view (mentioned earlier) reduces to total affirmation, the second to total negation, and the most plausible is partial affirmation, meaning that every body must have some accident. He then details two subgroups: the Baghdad Mu'tazila specify colors, while the Basran Mu'tazila specify spatial states (motion/rest, conjunction/separation). Next, he presents the proof of the objectors (those who deny partial necessity) that a body in time must have motion/rest etc., but shows that this only proves partial necessity, not total. He then presents another proof based on individuation (tashakhkhus), showing that bodies need accidents for individuation, but again this only proves partial necessity (some accident suffices). He notes that this proof can be extended to all times and all accidents by analogy, but then concludes that the universal answer to both proofs is the denial of the two premises (man' al-muqaddimatayn). The passage is technical, engaging with Mu'tazilite positions.

سلب كلي
إيجاب جزئي
أعراض

المعتزلة البصرية
الأكوان

What is the most plausible view according to the author regarding the necessity of accidents in bodies?
A) Total affirmation (ījāb kullī).
B) Total negation (salb kullī).
C) Partial affirmation (ījāb juz'ī).
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
The three logical possibilities are total affirmation, total negation, and partial affirmation, with partial affirmation being the most plausible, and two subgroups specifying colors or spatial states.

Objectors' Proofs and Their Limitations
Presents and limits two proofs of objectors, then gives the answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments used by objectors against total affirmation, why they only prove partial necessity, and the final refutation by denying the premises.

Core Idea
Objectors' proofs only establish partial necessity; generalization fails; answer is denial of premises.

The author now presents the arguments of those who deny partial affirmation (the objectors). They offer two proofs: one based on the fact that a body in time must have motion/rest etc., and another based on the necessity of individuation through accidents. The author shows that these proofs only yield partial necessity, not total. He then discusses how generalization might be attempted but concludes by refuting both proofs with the method of denying the premises.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof
Read the argument that a body realized in time and multiplied in number must have motion/rest etc. Notice that the author says this only proves partial necessity.
This proof can refute total negation and the Baghdad school but not prove total affirmation.
2
Read the second proof
Read the argument from individuation: bodies need accidents to be distinct. Again, the author notes it only proves partial necessity.
The proof works for times (pre-eternity and perpetuity) but not for types of accidents.
3
Understand the generalization attempt
See how the author describes analogizing one accident to another and one time to another to extend the proof. But this is not automatically accepted.
4
Recognize the final answer
The overall answer to both proofs is 'denial of the two premises' (man' al-muqaddimatayn), meaning the objectors' assumptions are rejected.
This is a common kalam tactic.

فمرجع الأول إلى إيجاب كلي - والثاني إلى سلب كلي، والأشبه هو الإيجاب الجزئي بمعنى أنه يجب أن يوجد في كل جسم شيء من الأعراض، إلا أن القائلين بالتفصيل منهم من خصه بالألوان بمعنى أنه يجب أن يوجد فيه شيء من الألوان وهم المعتزلة البغدادية، ومنهم من خصه بالأكوان بمعنى أنه يجب أن يوجد فيه الحركة أو السكون ، والاجتماع، أو الافتراق وهم البصرية، واحتجاج المانعين بأن الجسم متحقق في الزمان ومتكثر بالعدد فلا يخلو عن حركة أو سكون، واجتماع أو اقتراق على تقدير تمامه إنما يفيد هذا الإيجاب الجزئي لا الإيجاب الكلي المدعي. نعم يصلح للرد على القائلين بالسلب الكلي، وعلى البغدادية القائلين بجواز الخلو عما عدا الألوان، وكذا احتجاجهم بأن الشيء لا يوجد بدون التشخص ضرورة، وتشخص الأجسام إنما هو بالأعراض لكونها متماثلة لتألفها من الجواهر المتماثلة، فلو وجدت بدون الأعراض لزم وجود الغير المتشخص وهو محال لا يفيد العموم. أعني امتناع الجسم بدون أحد الضدين من كل عرض، لأن البعض كاف في التشخص . نعم يفيد عموم الأوقات. أعني الأزل، وما لا يزال بخلاف الأول فإنه ربما يمنع امتناع خلو الجسم في الأزل عن الحركة والسكون بل إنما يكون ذلك في الزمان الثاني والثالث وعن الاجتماع والافتراق، بل إنما يكون ذلك على تقدير تحقق جسم آخر، فيحتاج في التعميم إلى قياس ما قبل الاتصاف أعني الأزل على ما بعده، أعني ما لا يزال كما يقاس بعض الأعراض على البعض تعميماً للدليلين في جميع الأعراض، وتقريره أن اتصاف الجوهر بالعرض إما لذاته وإما لقابليته له ونسبة كل منهما إلى جميع الأعراض والأزمان على السوية والجواب منع المقدمتين.
Thus the first reduces to total affirmation, and the second to total negation, and the more plausible is partial affirmation, meaning that it is necessary that in every body something of accidents exist, except that those who make detail – among them some specify it with colors, meaning that it is necessary that something of colors exist in it, and they are the Baghdad Mu'tazila; and among them some specify it with spatial states, meaning that it is necessary that motion or rest, and conjunction or separation, exist in it, and they are the Basran Mu'tazila. And the proof of the objectors that the body is realized in time and multiplied in number, so it is not devoid of motion or rest, and conjunction or separation, upon the assumption of its completion, only yields this partial affirmation, not the total affirmation claimed. Yes, it is suitable for refuting those who affirm total negation, and the Baghdad school who affirm permissibility of being devoid of other than colors. Likewise their proof that a thing does not exist without individuation necessarily, and the individuation of bodies is only by accidents because they are similar, being composed of similar atoms; so if they existed without accidents, there would necessarily be the existence of the unindividuated, which is impossible – this does not yield generality. I mean the impossibility of a body without one of the two opposites from every accident, because some suffice for individuation. Yes, it yields generality of times: I mean pre-eternity and perpetuity, unlike the first, for it perhaps prevents the impossibility of a body being devoid in pre-eternity of motion and rest; rather that only occurs in the second and third time, and of conjunction and separation, rather that only occurs upon the assumption of the existence of another body. So generalization requires analogizing the state prior to qualification, i.e. pre-eternity, to what comes after it, i.e. perpetuity, just as some accidents are analogized to others to generalize both proofs to all accidents. And its formulation is that the attribution of a substance to an accident is either by its essence or by its capacity, and the relation of each to all accidents and times is uniform. And the answer is denial of the two premises.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a classification of views on the necessity of accidents in bodies. The author first states that the first view (mentioned earlier) reduces to total affirmation, the second to total negation, and the most plausible is partial affirmation, meaning that every body must have some accident. He then details two subgroups: the Baghdad Mu'tazila specify colors, while the Basran Mu'tazila specify spatial states (motion/rest, conjunction/separation). Next, he presents the proof of the objectors (those who deny partial necessity) that a body in time must have motion/rest etc., but shows that this only proves partial necessity, not total. He then presents another proof based on individuation (tashakhkhus), showing that bodies need accidents for individuation, but again this only proves partial necessity (some accident suffices). He notes that this proof can be extended to all times and all accidents by analogy, but then concludes that the universal answer to both proofs is the denial of the two premises (man' al-muqaddimatayn). The passage is technical, engaging with Mu'tazilite positions.

Key Terms
المانعون
التشخص
منع المقدمتين
إيجاب كلي
إيجاب جزئي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the objectors' proofs prove that every body must have all types of accidents (total affirmation).
Correction: The author explicitly states that both proofs only yield partial affirmation (some accident), not total affirmation. The first proof only proves the need for some accident, and the second only requires some accident for individuation.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what do the objectors' proofs actually establish?
A) Total affirmation (ījāb kullī).
B) Partial affirmation (ījāb juz'ī).
C) Total negation (salb kullī).
D) That bodies cannot exist in pre-eternity.

Key Takeaway
The objectors' proofs only show that bodies need some accident, not all accidents, and the final response is to deny the premises of those proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objectors' proofs seemed convincing until their limitations were exposed. In your own life, what 'proofs' have you accepted without examining their premises? Will you now question them, even if it means losing comforting certainty?
?
Denying the premises was the final answer. Is there a premise in your faith practice—something you've always assumed about worship, sincerity, or community—that you need to reject because it doesn't hold up to the Quran and Sunnah? What would that require from you today?
?
This lesson shows that partial proofs can mislead if taken as total. Where in your character do you mistake partial obedience for full submission? Confess one area where you have been satisfied with 'partial necessity' instead of complete devotion.

Third Proof: Colorlessness of Air
Uses air to argue bodies can be colorless, replies to objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the colorlessness of air serves as a proof in kalam, and how the author refutes objections about perception and obstacles.

Core Idea
Absence of sensation with sound senses proves non-existence, else all knowledge collapses.

This lesson examines a concrete empirical argument used by theologians to show that bodies can be free from accidental properties like color. By analyzing the example of air, which appears colorless, the author demonstrates that if something is naturally perceptible but not sensed, it likely does not exist. He then addresses a skeptical objection that could undermine all empirical knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without analysis. Let the argument flow naturally.
Notice the structure: proof, objection, response, further objection, response, alternative answer.
2
Identify the main argument and the objection
The proof is: air is colorless, so bodies can be colorless. The objector says we only lack sensation, not that color is absent. The author responds with a principle about sensation.
3
Analyze the slippery slope response
The objector then says there might be an unseen obstacle. The author responds that this leads to radical skepticism (mountains and sounds unnoticed).
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Understand the alternative answer about transparency
A different response says transparency is an opposite of color, so air has an accident (transparency) instead of being colorless.
This is a secondary point, not the author's main refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أنه لو لم يجز خلوه عن جميع الألوان لما وقع وقد وقع كالهواء، لا يقال: لا نسلّم خلوه عن اللون، بل غايته عدم الإحساس به، لأنـا نقــول عدم الإحساس بما من شأنه الإحساس به مع سلامة الحاسة وسائر الشرائط دليل على عدمه فإن قيل: من جملة الشرائط انتفاء المانع وتحققه ممنوع. قلنا: فتح هذا الباب يؤدي إلى جواز أن يكون بحضرتنا جبال شامخة، وأصوات هائلة ولا ندركها لمانع. وقد يجاب بأن الشفيف ضد اللون لا عدم.
Third: that if a body's being free from all colors were not permissible, then it would not occur, but it has occurred, like air. It cannot be said: we do not affirm its freedom from color; rather its limit is the absence of sensation of it, because we say: the absence of sensation of that which is by nature sensible, with soundness of the sense and all other conditions, is evidence of its non-existence. For if it were said: among the conditions is the absence of an obstacle, and its existence is not granted. We say: opening this door leads to the possibility that there are towering mountains and tremendous sounds present with us but we do not perceive them due to an obstacle. And it may be answered that transparency is opposite to color, not absence.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof using the empirical example of air. The argument is that air is perceived to be colorless, so if bodies could not be free of all colors, air would have to have some color, but it does not. An objector says that we do not know air is free of color; we only know we do not sense it. The author replies that if something is naturally sensible, and the sense is sound and conditions are met, then lack of sensation indicates non-existence. The objector then raises the possibility of an unknown obstacle (māniʿ). The author responds that this leads to skepticism: we could claim there are mountains and sounds we don't perceive due to obstacles, which is absurd. Finally, an alternative answer is mentioned by others: transparency (ash-shafīf) is an opposite of color, not a mere absence, so air actually has an accident (transparency) rather than being free. This proof and its refutations illustrate the kalām method of empirical argument and refutation by reductio ad absurdum.

Key Terms

الهواء
عدم الإحساس
الحاسة
المانع
الشفيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is not perceived, it must not exist.
Correction: The author only claims non-existence when the object is naturally perceptible, the sense is sound, and conditions (including no obstacle) are met.

Checkpoint
According to the response in the third proof, what does the absence of sensation of something naturally sensible indicate?
A) The presence of an obstacle.
B) The non-existence of the sensible quality.
C) The weakness of the sense.
D) The impossibility of perception.

Key Takeaway
The colorlessness of air demonstrates that bodies can be free from color, and the author uses a principle of sensation and a reductio to defend the argument against skeptical objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed something as nonexistent simply because you did not perceive it? How would the principle in this passage change how you evaluate such situations in your daily life—especially regarding matters of faith?
?
The objector raises the possibility of an unseen obstacle to doubt the evidence. Reflect: Do you sometimes allow hypothetical 'obstacles' to avoid accepting uncomfortable truths? What would the author's reductio say to your heart about the danger of that mindset?
?
If you were to consistently apply the skeptic's logic—that what you don't perceive might still exist behind a hidden obstacle—how would your certainty about the world, your relationships, and your worship be affected? Are you willing to live with that uncertainty, or does this passage call you to a different standard of conviction?

The Reliability of Estimative Judgments in Geometry
Al-Taftazani defends estimative judgments in geometry against an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Taftazani considers estimative judgments in geometry reliable and how they differ from false pure estimative judgments.

Core Idea
Estimative judgments in geometry are as reliable as sense perception.

In this passage, al-Taftazani responds to an objection that the impossibility of a first point in an infinite line is not conceded because the argument involves a false estimative judgment. He clarifies that not all estimative judgments are false; those concerning geometry are correct and almost like sensory data because they are guided by reason. However, pure estimative judgments that impose sensible categories on intelligibles are indeed false.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the objection: the impossibility of a first point is not conceded because the division of motion and angle without end is a judgment of wahm and false.
2
Identify the two types of estimative judgments
Al-Taftazani distinguishes between geometrical estimative judgments and pure estimative judgments. Find the characteristics of each in the text.
Geometrical ones are correct and nearly sensory; pure ones are false when they apply sensible features to intelligibles.
3
Understand why geometrical judgments are reliable
Notice the reason given: they are under the command of reason and no one denies them except through obstinacy. Reflect on why this makes them akin to sense perceptions.
4
Apply the distinction to the objection
How does this distinction answer the objection? The objector claimed the argument relies on a false estimative judgment, but al-Taftazani shows that the relevant judgment belongs to geometry, which is sound.
Thus, the impossibility of a first point in an infinite line remains established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنا لا نسلم استحالة أول نقطة في الخط الغير المتناهي، وما ذكر في بيانه باطل لأن انقسام الحركة والزاوية لا إلى نهاية حكم الوهم وهو كاذب . وجوابه: أن أحكام الوهم فيما يفرض من الهندسيات صحيحة تكاد تجري مجرى الحسيات لكونها على طاعة من العقل بحيث لا يمنع إلا مكابرة. ولهذا لا يقع فيها اختلاف آراء وإنما الكاذب هي الوهميات الصرفة مثل الحكم في المعقولات بما يخص المحسوسات كالحكم بأن كل موجود ذو وضع ) .
And among them is that we do not concede the impossibility of a first point in an infinite line, and what was mentioned in its explanation is false because the division of motion and angle without end is a judgment of the estimative faculty (wahm) and it is false. And its answer: That the judgments of the estimative faculty concerning what is assumed from geometrical matters are correct, almost running in the same course as sensory perceptions because they are under the command of reason, such that no one denies them except through obstinacy. Hence, no difference of opinion occurs regarding them; rather, the false ones are pure estimative judgments (al-wahmiyyāt al-ṣirfah) like judging of intelligibles what is particular to sensibles, such as the judgment that every existing thing has a position.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This is the third objection and its answer on page 95. The objector denies the impossibility of a first point in an infinite line, claiming that the infinite divisibility of motion and angle is merely a judgment of the estimative faculty (wahm) and is false. The author responds by distinguishing between two types of estimative judgments: those concerning geometry, which are correct and nearly as certain as sensory perceptions because they are guided by reason, and pure estimative judgments that improperly apply categories of sensible objects to intelligibles (e.g., assuming all existents have a position). The former are valid, so the infinite divisibility leading to no first point is a valid geometric truth.

Key Terms
الوهم (al-wahm)
الهندسيات (al-handasiyyāt)
الحسيات (al-ḥissiyyāt)
الوهميات الصرفة (al-wahmiyyāt al-ṣirfah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All judgments of the estimative faculty are false or unreliable.
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows that geometrical estimative judgments are correct and reliable, while only pure estimative judgments that mix sensible and intelligible categories are false.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, when are estimative judgments false?
A) When they concern geometry.
B) When they are pure and apply sensible categories to intelligibles.
C) When they are contrary to reason.
D) When they are based on assumption.

Key Takeaway
Estimative judgments in geometry are sound and almost like sense data, but pure estimative judgments that impose sensible properties on intelligibles are false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
When you encounter a difficult theological concept, do you rely more on sensory analogy or on reasoning under the command of intellect? Reflect on a specific belief you hold that might be based on overextending sensible properties. What step will you take today to correct it?

Direction: Definition, Existence, and Indivisibility
Definition of direction and proof of its existence and indivisibility in motion/indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam concept of direction (jiha) as the edge of extension, its existence as a positioned entity, and why it does not accept division in the locus of motion and indication.

Core Idea
Direction is the indivisible endpoint of extension that motion seeks but cannot enter.

This passage introduces the concept of direction (jiha) in Islamic theology, where it is not a mere direction of the compass but a precise philosophical term. Sanusi defines it as the edge of extension, the goal of motion, and the endpoint of indication. He then establishes its existence as a positioned entity and proves that it cannot be divided, at least in terms of motion and indication. Understanding direction is essential for comprehending how bodies relate to space and the divine.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage from 'طرف الامتداد' to 'فتكون الجهة منتهاها'. Notice the three aspects: edge of extension, endpoint of indication, goal of mover.
2
Understand the existence of direction
Sanusi argues direction is existent and has position because it is the goal of motion and sensory indication. Contrast with motion in quality to see why direction must be existent.
3
Grasp the meaning of 'obtaining in a direction'
The text clarifies that obtaining in a direction means arrival and proximity, not literal containment. This is crucial for the subsequent proof.
4
Follow the proof of indivisibility
The author provides a hypothetical division of direction into two parts and shows contradiction. Work through the argument step by step.
5
Note the clarification about absolute indivisibility
Sanusi ends by stating that direction may be a line or surface; it is only indivisible with respect to motion and indication, not absolutely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (خاتمة) فطرف الامتداد بالنسبة إليه نهاية، ومن حيث كونه منتهى الإشارة، ومقصد المتحرك بالحصول فيه جهة، فتكون موجودة ذات وضع لا يقبل الانقسام وإلا لوقعت الحركة فيها فتكون الجهة منتهاها . جعل بحث الجهة خاتمة بحث تناهي الأبعاد لكونها عبارة عن نهاية الامتداد، وذلك أن طرف الامتداد بالنسبة إليه طرف ونهاية وبالنسبة إلى الحركة والإشارة جهة، ثم إنها موجودة، ومن ذوات الأوضاع لأنها مقصد المتحرك بالحصول فيه. ومنتهى الإشارة الحسية ، والمعدوم أو المجرد يمتنع الحصول فيه أو الإشارة إليه . وهذا بخلاف الحركة في الكيف كحركة الجسم من البياض إلى السواد، فإن السواد مقصد المتحرك بالتحصيل فلا يجب بل يمتنع أن يكون موجوداً لامتناع تحصيل الحاصل، ثم لا يخفى أن معنى الحصول في الجهة الحصول عندها وصولاً وقرباً، كما أن معنى التحرك في جهة كذا التحرك في سمت يتأتى إليها، وذلك لأن كلاً من المتحرك والحركة منقسم ، فلا تقع حقيقة إلا في منقسم، والجهة لا تقبل الانقسام أعني في مأخذ الحركة والإشارة إذ لو انقسمت إلى جزئين مثلاً، فالجزء الذي يلي المتحرك، إما أن لا يتجاوزه المتحرك بحركته إذا وصل إليه فيكون هو الجهة من غير مدخل للجزء الآخر، وإما أن يتجاوزه فتلك الحركة، إما حركة عن الجهة فالجهة هي الجزء الأول فقط وإما إلى الجهة فهي الثاني فقط لا يقال بل في جهة لأنا نقول الحركة في الشيء المنقسم لا محالة تكون إما عن جهة، أو إلى جهة، ويعود المحذور للقطع بأن الجهة هي مقصد المتحرك لا المسافة التي تقطع بالحركة، وهذا يدل على أنها لا تقبل الانقسام في مأخذ الحركة والإشارة وهي كاف في إفادة المطلوب. أعني امتناع وقوع الجسم أو الحركة فيها، ولا يدل على أنها لا تقبل الانقسام أصلاً حتى لا تكون إلا نقطة، بل ربما تكون خطاً أو سطحاً، بقي الكلام في أن طرف كل امتداد، ومنتهى كل إشارة جهة حتى تكون جهات كل جسم أطراف امتداداته فيكون على سطحه أم الجهة
He said: (Conclusion) The edge of extension is, relative to it, an end, and in terms of being the endpoint of indication and the goal of the mover in obtaining it, a direction. So it is existent, possessing position, not accepting division; otherwise motion would occur in it, and then the direction would be its end. He placed the discussion of direction as a conclusion to the discussion of the finitude of dimensions because it is an expression of the end of extension. That is, the edge of extension is, relative to it, an edge and an end, and relative to motion and indication, a direction. Then it is existent and of those possessing positions because it is the goal of the mover in obtaining it and the endpoint of sensory indication. And non-existent or abstract is impossible to obtain in it or indicate to it. This is unlike motion in quality, like the motion of a body from whiteness to blackness, for blackness is the goal of the mover in acquisition, so it is not necessary, rather impossible, that it be existent because of the impossibility of acquiring what is already obtained. Then it is not hidden that the meaning of obtaining in a direction is obtaining at it by arrival and proximity, just as the meaning of moving in such a direction is moving in a direction that leads to it. That is because both the mover and motion are divisible, so they do not actually occur except in something divisible, and the direction does not accept division, I mean in the locus of motion and indication, because if it were divided into two parts, for example, then the part that is next to the mover, either the mover does not go beyond it by his motion when he reaches it, so it is the direction without involvement of the other part, or he goes beyond it, so that motion is either motion from the direction, so the direction is only the first part, or motion to the direction, so it is only the second part. It cannot be said: rather, it is in a direction, for we say: Motion in a divisible thing necessarily is either from a direction or to a direction, and the problem returns because it is certain that the direction is the goal of the mover, not the distance traversed by motion. And this indicates that it does not accept division in the locus of motion and indication, and this is sufficient for the required, i.e., the impossibility of the occurrence of body or motion in it. And it does not indicate that it does not accept division absolutely, so that it is only a point; rather, it may be a line or a surface. The remaining discussion is that the edge of every extension and the endpoint of every indication is a direction, so that the directions of every body are the edges of its extensions, so it is on its surface or the direction...
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion (khatima) on the topic of direction (jiha). It begins by defining direction: it is the edge of extension, the endpoint of indication, and the goal of a mover in obtaining it. Therefore, direction is existent and has position, and it does not accept division; otherwise motion into it would occur, and direction would be its end. The author explains why the discussion of direction is placed as a conclusion to the discussion of the finitude of dimensions: because direction is essentially the end of extension. Then he clarifies that direction is existent and has a position because it is the goal of the mover and the endpoint of sensory indication; the non-existent or abstract cannot be obtained or indicated. This is contrasted with motion in quality, like a body changing from white to black—the blackness, as a goal, cannot be existent because it would be acquiring what is already acquired. Next, the meaning of 'obtaining in a direction' is explained: it is obtaining at the direction by arrival and proximity, and 'moving in a direction' means moving in a direction leading to it. Since both the mover and motion are divisible, they can only occur in something divisible. However, direction does not accept division in the locus of motion and indication. The author provides a proof: if direction were divided into two parts, then when the mover reaches the first part, either he does not go beyond it (so direction is just that part) or he goes beyond it, leading to motion either away from or towards the part, each case reducing to one part being the direction. Thus, direction is indivisible in this respect. This suffices to show that body or motion cannot occur in it, but it does not mean direction is indivisible absolutely—it may be a line or a surface. The passage ends with an incomplete statement about the edges of extensions being directions.

Key Terms
جهة
امتداد
نهاية
إشارة
متحرك
قسمة
خلاء
كيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that direction being indivisible in motion means it is a point (a zero-dimensional entity).
Correction: The text clarifies that direction may be a line or a surface; it is only indivisible with respect to motion and indication, not absolutely. So it can have extension.

Checkpoint
Why does the author place the discussion of direction as a conclusion to the discussion of the finitude of dimensions?
A) Because direction is the same as dimension
B) Because direction is an expression of the end of extension
C) Because direction is divisible
D) Because direction is non-existent

Key Takeaway
Direction is the positioned, existent endpoint of extension that is indivisible in the context of motion and indication, though it may be geometrically extended.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your pursuit of closeness to Allah, do you treat that goal as an indivisible endpoint that you can only approach but never fully 'enter', or do you mistakenly think you can traverse it like a distance? How does this shift your understanding of striving?
?
The text says the direction is the goal of the mover, not the path. Is there an area of your life where you have confused the means (motion) with the end (direction)? What concrete step can you take today to realign your focus onto the actual goal?
?
If direction is an indivisible endpoint, then every motion must have a distinct, unyielding target. What is the ultimate direction of your spiritual motion? Does your daily activity reflect that you are truly moving towards it, or are you circling within the divisible distractions?

Proof that Body Cannot Occupy a Direction
Al-Taftazani proves that a body cannot be located in a direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Taftazani argues that a body cannot be located in a direction by analyzing direction as the goal of motion and its indivisibility in that context.

Core Idea
Direction is the goal of motion, not a container for bodies.

Al-Taftazani presents a logical proof (burhan) that a body cannot occupy a direction. The proof hinges on understanding direction as the goal (maqsad) of a moving object, not a physical container. Since motion and the moving object are divisible, they require a divisible medium, but direction, in its relevant sense, does not accept division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the technical terms like 'tahsil' and 'ma'khadh al-haraka'.
2
Understand the key terms
Identify the meanings of 'direction' (jiha), 'attainment' (tahsil), 'domain of motion and indication' (ma'khadh al-haraka wa al-ishara). These are crucial for the argument.
3
Follow the hypothetical division argument
The author imagines dividing the direction into parts. Work through the two possibilities and see why both lead to a contradiction if body were in direction.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Recognize the nuance
The author clarifies that direction is not absolutely indivisible like a point—it may be a line or surface. The indivisibility is only in the context of being a goal of motion.
This prevents a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجهة مقصد المتحرك بالتحصيل فلا يجب بل يمتنع أن يكون موجوداً لامتناع تحصيل الحاصل، ثم لا يخفى أن معنى الحصول في الجهة الحصول عندها وصولاً وقرباً، كما أن معنى التحرك في جهة كذا التحرك في سمت يتأتى إليها، وذلك لأن كلاً من المتحرك والحركة منقسم ، فلا تقع حقيقة إلا في منقسم، والجهة لا تقبل الانقسام أعني في مأخذ الحركة والإشارة إذ لو انقسمت إلى جزئين مثلاً، فالجزء الذي يلي المتحرك، إما أن لا يتجاوزه المتحرك بحركته إذا وصل إليه فيكون هو الجهة من غير مدخل للجزء الآخر، وإما أن يتجاوزه فتلك الحركة، إما حركة عن الجهة فالجهة هي الجزء الأول فقط وإما إلى الجهة فهي الثاني فقط لا يقال بل في جهة لأنا نقول الحركة في الشيء (۱) المنقسم لا محالة تكون إما عن جهة، أو إلى جهة، ويعود المحذور للقطع بأن الجهة هي مقصد المتحرك لا المسافة التي تقطع بالحركة، وهذا يدل على أنها لا تقبل الانقسام في مأخذ الحركة والإشارة وهي كاف في إفادة المطلوب. أعني امتناع وقوع الجسم أو الحركة فيها، ولا يدل على أنها لا تقبل الانقسام أصلاً حتى لا تكون إلا نقطة، بل ربما تكون خطاً أو سطحاً
The direction (al-jiha) is the goal of the moving object (al-mutaharrik) in terms of attainment (al-tahsil), so it is not necessary, rather it is impossible for it to exist, because attaining what is already attained is impossible. Then it is not hidden that the meaning of 'attainment' (al-husul) in the direction is attainment at it (at the direction) by arrival and proximity, just as the meaning of 'moving' (al-taharruk) in a certain direction is moving in a path that leads to it. That is because both the moving object and motion are divisible, so they do not truly occur except in what is divisible. But the direction does not accept division, I mean in the domain of motion and indication (ma'khadh al-haraka wa al-ishara). For if it were divided into, say, two parts, then the part that is adjacent to the moving object: either the moving object does not pass beyond it by its motion when it reaches it, so it (that part) is the direction without any role for the other part; or it does pass beyond it, then that motion is either motion away from the direction, so the direction is only the first part, or motion towards the direction, so it is only the second part. It cannot be said: 'Rather, it is in a direction,' because we say: motion in a divisible thing necessarily is either away from a direction or towards a direction, and the problem returns, since it is certain that the direction is the goal of the moving object, not the distance traversed by motion. This indicates that it does not accept division in the domain of motion and indication, and it is sufficient to convey the desired conclusion, i.e., the impossibility of body or motion occurring in it. It does not indicate that it does not accept division absolutely such that it is nothing but a point; rather, it may be a line or a surface.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhan) that a body cannot be located in a direction (jiha), because direction is the goal of a moving object, and the moving object and its motion are divisible, whereas direction is indivisible in the relevant sense. The author begins by stating that direction is the goal of a moving object in terms of 'attainment' (tahsil), meaning arrival and proximity, not physical containment. Since the moving object and motion are divisible, they can only occur in something divisible. But direction does not accept division in the domain of motion and indication (ma'khadh al-haraka wa al-ishara). He then gives a hypothetical: if direction were divided into two parts, the moving object either stops at the first part (making that the direction) or passes beyond it, in which case the motion is either away from the first part or toward the second, but in either case the direction is only one part, not both. The objection that the moving object is 'in a direction' is refuted by noting that motion in a divisible thing is always either from or to a direction. The conclusion is that body and motion cannot occur in the direction itself. However, the author clarifies that this does not prove direction is absolutely indivisible like a point; it may be a line or surface—the indivisibility is only in the context of being a goal of motion.

Key Terms
الجهة (direction)
الانقسام (division/divisibility)
الحركة (motion)
المتحرك (moving object)
الإشارة (indication)
مأخذ الحركة (domain of motion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That direction is absolutely indivisible like a mathematical point.
Correction: Al-Taftazani clarifies that direction may be a line or surface; its indivisibility is limited to the context of being a goal of motion and indication.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, why can a body not be located in a direction?
A) Because direction is a point and bodies are extended.
B) Because direction is indivisible in the domain of motion and indication, while body and motion are divisible.
C) Because direction has no physical existence.
D) Because direction is infinite.

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani proves that a body cannot be located in a direction by showing that direction, as the goal of motion, is indivisible in the relevant sense, while body and motion are divisible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You habitually imagine Allah as 'above' or 'in the heavens'. After reading Al-Taftazani's proof that direction is the goal of motion, not a container, can you continue to think of Allah as located somewhere? What will you do to train your heart to worship a God who is not confined to any direction?
?
The argument shows that you, as a body, are always in some direction. But Allah is not. How does this affect your feeling of closeness to Him? If He is not in your direction, how can you truly sense His nearness? What concrete step can you take today to cultivate that closeness despite the absence of physical direction?
?
Al-Taftazani says direction is indivisible because motion must be either toward or away from a goal. This logic strips away any mental image you might have of Allah. Are you willing to let go of comfortable mental pictures of God for the sake of sound tawheed? If so, what image are you most attached to, and how will you release it?

Why Are There Only Six Directions?
Al-Taftazani explains the conceptual infinity of directions and the two reasons for the standard enumeration of six.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why directions are conceptually unlimited but conventionally counted as six, based on geometric and human-centered reasoning.

Core Idea
Directions are infinite in principle, but three dimensions give six practical directions.

In kalam cosmology, directions (jihāt) are not fixed categories; they are relative. Al-Taftazani begins by stating that directions are unlimited in number, yet the well-known view counts six. He then provides two distinct reasons—one rooted in geometry, the other in human anatomy. This lesson unpacks those reasons and shows how they shape Islamic theological discussions about God and the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a statement followed by two reasons and a conclusion.
Pay attention to the terms 'khāssī' (specific) and '‘āmmī' (common).
2
Identify the two reasons
Locate the philosophical reason (three dimensions) and the anatomical reason (human body parts). Understand how each yields six directions.
3
Consider the extension to animals
Notice how the anatomical reason is extended analogically to quadrupeds. Reflect on why human anatomy is the starting point.
4
Grasp the conceptual infinity
Understand that the passage emphasizes that directions are unlimited in principle. The six are only a conventional enumeration based on practical considerations.
This prevents a literalist reading that there are only six directions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ثم إنها) الجهات غير محصورة في عدد غير محصورة إلا أنه قد يعتبر قيام الامتدادات بعضها على البعض أو يعتبر ما للإنسان من الرأس والقدم والظهر والبطن واليدين الأقوى والأضعف غالباً) فتنحصر الجهات في الست] . أي الجهات غير محصورة في عدد لجواز فرض امتدادات غير متناهية العدد في جسم واحد، بل بالقياس إلى نقطة واحدة، إلا أن المشهور، أنها ست. وسبب (۲) الشهرة أمران أحدهما خاصي، وهو أنه يمكن أن يفرض في كل جسم أبعاد ثلاثة متقاطعة، ولكل بعد طرفان ، فيكون لكل جسم ست جهات . وثانيهما: عامي وهو اعتبار حال الإنسان فيما له من الرأس والقدم فبحسبهما له الفوق والتحت ومن البطن والظهر فبحسبهما القدام والخلف، ومن الجنبين اللذين عليهما يد أقوى في الغالب وهي اليمنى، وأخرى أضعف وهي اليسرى فبحسبهما اليمين واليسار ، ثم اعتبر ذلك في سائر الحيوانات بحسب المقايسة والمناسبة (۳) ، وكان في ذوات الأربع ) الفوق والتخت ما يلي الظهر والبطن ، والقدام تصح فيه الحركة (راجع كليات أبي البقاء) والجهة نهاية البعد ويمكن أن يفرض في كل جسم أبعاد غير متناهية العدد، فيكون كل طرف منها جهة الا أن المقرر عند عامة الفلاسفة أن الجسم يمكن أن يفرض فيه أبعاد ثلاثة متقاطعة على زوايا قائمة ولكل منها طرفان فلكل جسم إذن ست جهات وهي : : فوق، وأسفل ويمين ويسار، وخلف وقدام
He said: 'Then indeed, directions are unlimited in number, but it is considered that extensions stand upon each other, or what pertains to man from head, foot, back, belly, and the stronger and weaker hands, so the directions are limited to six.' That is, directions are unlimited in number because it is possible to suppose infinite extensions in a single body, even relative to a single point, but the well-known (mashhur) is that they are six. The reason for their fame is two: One is specific (khāssī): it is possible to suppose in every body three intersecting dimensions, and each dimension has two ends, so every body has six directions. The second is common (‘āmmī): considering the state of man regarding what he has of head and foot—by these he has above and below; from belly and back—by these he has front and back; from the two sides on which there is a hand that is stronger mostly, i.e., the right, and another weaker, i.e., the left—by these he has right and left. Then this is applied to other animals by analogy and appropriateness. And in quadrupeds, 'above' and 'below' are what is adjacent to the back and belly, 'front' where motion is valid (see Kulliyyat Abi al-Baqa). And direction is the end of the dimension, and it is possible to suppose in every body infinite dimensions, so each end is a direction, except that what is established among the majority of philosophers is that in a body three dimensions intersecting at right angles can be supposed, each with two ends, so every body has six directions, which are: above, below, right, left, back, and front.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage discusses the enumeration of directions. It begins by quoting the matn: 'Then indeed, directions are unlimited in number, but... are limited to six.' The commentary explains that directions are conceptually infinite because one can imagine endless extensions in a body. However, the well-known (mashhur) view is that there are only six. Two reasons are given. The first reason is philosophical (khāssī, 'specific'): every body has three mutually perpendicular dimensions (length, width, depth), each with two ends, yielding six directions. The second reason is common (‘āmmī): based on human anatomy—head (above), foot (below), belly (front), back (back), right hand (right), left hand (left). These are then extended analogically to animals. The passage also notes that in quadrupeds, above/below are related to back/belly, and front is where motion proceeds. It reiterates that direction is the endpoint of a dimension, and while infinite dimensions are conceivable, the agreed-upon (al-muqarrar) among philosophers is three dimensions with six directions. The six directions are enumerated: above, below, right, left, back, and front.

الأبعاد (dimensions)
الفوق (above)
التحت (below)
اليمين (right)
اليسار (left)
الخلف (back)
القدام (front)

Misconception: Directions are inherently six in number.
Correction: The text states directions are unlimited in number but conventionally counted as six for practical reasons.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Though directions are conceptually infinite, the standard six—based on three dimensions or human anatomy—serve as the foundational reference points in kalam cosmology.
