Why Directions Are Commonly Six
Al-Taftazani explains the two reasons for the common convention of six directions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why directions are commonly considered six despite being theoretically unlimited, and the distinction between the philosophical and anthropomorphic reasons.

Core Idea
Six directions are a convention, not a logical necessity.

Al-Taftazani begins by stating that directions are unlimited in number, yet the well-known view is that they are six. He then provides two distinct reasons for this convention: one based on the three-dimensional structure of any body, and another based on human anatomy. This passage clarifies that neither reason is absolutely necessary, but they suffice for the common understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Taftazani uses a very compressed style in the matn and then expands in the sharh.
2
Identify the two reasons
The passage presents two reasons for the common view that directions are six. Find each one and notice how al-Taftazani labels them: 'specific' (خاصي) and 'general' (عامي).
3
Understand the conclusion
The passage ends by stating that neither consideration is necessary for the confinement of directions to six. Reflect on why this is important: it shows that the six directions are a convention, not a logical requirement.
This connects to the learning objective about convention vs. necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[قال (ثم إنها) الجهات غير محصورة في عدد غير محصورة إلا أنه قد يعتبر قيام الامتدادات بعضها على البعض أو يعتبر ما للإنسان من الرأس والقدم والظهر والبطن واليدين الأقوى والأضعف غالباً) فتنحصر الجهات في الست] . أي الجهات غير محصورة في عدد لجواز فرض امتدادات غير متناهية العدد في جسم واحد، بل بالقياس إلى نقطة واحدة، إلا أن المشهور، أنها ست. وسبب الشهرة أمران أحدهما خاصي، وهو أنه يمكن أن يفرض في كل جسم أبعاد ثلاثة متقاطعة، ولكل بعد طرفان ، فيكون لكل جسم ست جهات . وثانيهما: عامي وهو اعتبار حال الإنسان فيما له من الرأس والقدم فبحسبهما له الفوق والتحت ومن البطن والظهر فبحسبهما القدام والخلف، ومن الجنبين اللذين عليهما يد أقوى في الغالب وهي اليمنى، وأخرى أضعف وهي اليسرى فبحسبهما اليمين واليسار ، ثم اعتبر ذلك في سائر الحيوانات بحسب المقايسة والمناسبة ، وكان في ذوات الأربع الفوق والتخت ما يلي الظهر والبطن ، والقدام والخلف ما يلي الرأس والذنب، وليس شيء من الاعتبارين بواجب ليصح انحصار الجهات في الست .
He said: (Then indeed) directions are not limited in number except that the standing of extensions upon each other may be considered, or what the human has of head, foot, back, belly, and the two hands, the stronger and weaker usually, are considered, so the directions are confined to six. That is, directions are not limited in number because it is permissible to suppose infinite extensions in one body, even relative to a single point. However, the well-known (view) is that they are six. The reason for the fame is two matters: one specific, which is that in every body three intersecting dimensions can be supposed, and each dimension has two ends, so every body has six directions. The second is general, which is considering the state of man regarding what he has of head and foot – according to these he has up and down; from belly and back – according to these he has front and back; and from the two sides on which there is a hand that is stronger usually, which is the right, and another that is weaker, which is the left – according to these he has right and left. Then this was analogized to other animals by comparison and proportion. And in quadrupeds, the above and below are what are towards the back and belly, and the front and back are what are towards the head and tail. And neither of the two considerations is necessary for the confinement of directions to six to be correct.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage explains why directions are commonly considered six despite being theoretically unlimited. It begins by stating that directions are not limited in number because one can imagine infinite extensions in a single body. However, the famous convention is six directions. The reason for this convention is given as two matters: first, a specific philosophical reason: in every body, three mutually perpendicular dimensions can be assumed (length, width, depth), and each dimension has two ends, resulting in six directions. Second, a general reason based on human anatomy: the head and foot give up and down; the belly and back give front and back; the two sides, with the stronger hand usually on the right, give right and left. This human-based classification is extended to other animals by analogy. For quadrupeds, up/down corresponds to back/belly, and front/back to head/tail. The passage concludes that neither of these two considerations is absolutely necessary (i.e., they are not logically required for the definition of directions), but they suffice for the common confinement to six.

Classification of Directions into Natural and Positional
Al-Taftazani classifies directions into natural (up/down) and positional (the other four).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani classifies the six directions into natural (up/down) and positional (front/back/left/right) and why this distinction matters in kalam.

Core Idea
Up and down are fixed by nature; front, back, left, right change with orientation.

In this concise passage, al-Taftazani explains a foundational classification used in theological and cosmological discussions: not all directions are alike. Two are natural and absolute, while four are relative to the observer's stance. This distinction helps clarify arguments about God's attributes and the nature of spatial relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's classification come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two types of directions
The passage states that up (علو) and down (سفل) are natural (طبيعي) and the other four are positional (وضعية). Notice that natural directions are defined by reference to the human head and feet.
'Natural' here means inherent to the human constitution, not arbitrary.
3
Understand the example of orientation change
The example of a person facing east then west shows how front, back, right, and left swap. This illustrates that positional directions are not fixed.
4
Contrast with the inverted person
The inverted person example shows that even if you stand on your head, up and down remain the same. This proves their absolute nature.
This example shows the absolute nature of up/down even when inverted.
5
Apply to the Earth context
The passage concludes that two people at opposite ends of the Earth's diameter both have their heads up, reinforcing that up and down are not relative to individual orientation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ قال ( والطبيعي منها) العلو والسفل، والبواقي وضعية، تتبدل كالمواجه للمشرق إذا واجه المغرب بخلاف المنكوس]. أي من الجهات جهة العلو وهي ما يلي رأس الإنسان بالطبع ، والسفل ما يلي قدميه بالطبع حيث لا تتبدلان أصلا، والأربعة الباقية وضعية تتبدل بتبدل الأوضاع ، كالمتوجه إلى المشرق يكون المشرق قدامه والمغرب خلفه، والجنوب يمينه والشمال شماله، ثم إذا توجه إلى المغرب صار المغرب قدامه والمشرق خلفه، والشمال يمينه والجنوب شماله بخلاف ما إذا صار القائم منكوساً، فإنه لا يصير ما يلي رجله تحتاً، وما يلي رأسه فوقاً، بل يصير رأسه من تحت، ورجله من فوق، والفوق والتحت بحالهما، فالشخصان القائمان على طرفي قطر الأرض يكون رأس كل منهما فوق ورجلهما تحت .
He said: (And the natural of them) are the upward and the downward, and the remaining are positional, changing like the one facing east when he faces west, contrary to the inverted. That is, among the directions is the upward direction, which is what follows the head of a human by nature, and the downward, which follows his feet by nature, such that they do not change at all. The remaining four are positional, changing with the change of positions, like the one facing east: east is in front of him and west behind him, south on his right and north on his left. Then when he faces west, west becomes in front of him and east behind him, north on his right and south on his left. Contrary to when a standing person becomes inverted: what follows his foot does not become downward, nor what follows his head become upward. Rather, his head becomes from below and his foot from above, while up and down remain as they are. So two persons standing at opposite ends of the earth's diameter each has his head above and his feet below.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage classifies the six directions into two types: natural and positional. The natural directions are up (ʿuluww) and down (sufl), which are defined relative to the human head and feet by nature and do not change regardless of orientation. The remaining four directions (front, back, right, left) are positional (waḍʿiyya), meaning they change when the person changes his orientation. For example, a person facing east has east in front and west behind; if he turns to face west, west becomes front and east becomes behind, and his right and left also swap accordingly. However, the natural directions are absolute: even if a person is inverted (e.g., standing on his head), what was his foot does not become down; rather, his head is now down and his feet up, but the concepts of up and down remain fixed relative to the Earth's axis. The passage concludes by noting that two people standing at opposite ends of the Earth's diameter both have their heads up and feet down, confirming that up and down are not relative to individual orientation but are natural absolutes.

Misconception: Front, back, right, left are fixed directions like up and down.
Correction: Only up and down are fixed by nature; the other four change with orientation.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani's classification distinguishes absolute natural directions (up/down) from relative positional ones, a key concept in kalam cosmology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Up Does Not Require a Down
Al-Taftazani explains that the upward direction is independent of a downward counterpart.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of 'up' does not depend on a corresponding 'down' in Islamic cosmology, using the example of the Earth's extensions.

Core Idea
'Up' is absolute toward the sky, not relative to a hypothetical 'down'.

This passage from al-Taftazani tackles a subtle but important point: the direction 'up' does not logically require a paired 'down'. Using the example of the Earth, he explains that the Earth has no actual downward direction because all its real extensions point toward the sky. This clarifies a key idea in understanding divine attributes like elevation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation to grasp the literal meaning.
Notice the concise style of al-Taftazani.
2
Understand the claim
The passage states that 'up' does not necessarily require 'down' as a counterpart. Reflect on why this might be counterintuitive.
This is a logical point, not just a physical one.
3
Analyze the example
The Earth is given as evidence: it has no actual down, only hypothetically. All its actual extensions go outward toward the sky. Consider how this supports the claim.
'Actual extensions' refers to the direction away from the Earth's surface.
4
Connect to theology
Relate this to Allah's attribute of 'uluww (highness). How does this passage help avoid misunderstanding Allah's transcendence?
Allah is above without spatial limitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ قال والعلو لا يلزم أن يكون بالإضافة إلى السفل) فإن الأرض لا سفل لها إلا بالوهم لأن جميع أطراف امتداداتها الفعلية إلى السماء ] .
He said: And the upward does not necessarily have to be in relation to the downward. For the earth has no downward except hypothetically, because all its actual extensions are towards the sky.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage addresses a subtle point: the direction 'up' does not logically require a corresponding 'down' opposite to it. The example given is the Earth: the Earth has no actual 'down' because all its actual extensions (i.e., the direction from any point on the Earth's surface outward) are towards the sky. The notion of a 'down' for the Earth is only hypothetical (bi-l-wahm), presumably referring to the direction toward the Earth's center, but the text emphasises actual extensions, which go outward. This reinforces the idea that 'up' is an absolute direction (toward the sky) and does not depend on a paired 'down', especially for a spherical Earth.

Classification and Nature of Directions
Al-Taftazani defines the six directions and distinguishes natural from positional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how directions are classified into natural (up/down) and positional, and why up and down are not necessarily correlative.

Core Idea
Directions are not all relative; up and down are fixed by nature, not by orientation.

Al-Taftazani explains the six directions and clarifies a common confusion. This lesson will help you grasp the distinction between natural and positional directions and why up and down are absolute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's argument unfold.
2
Identify natural vs positional
Notice that up and down are called 'natural' because they do not change with orientation, while the other four are 'positional'.
3
Understand the inversion example
An inverted person does not swap up/down; up remains at the head. This shows up/down are not body-relative.
4
Grasp the rebuttal of correlation
The author argues that up and down are not correlative like front/back. The earth has only up, proving independence.
This counters a common misconception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخلف ما يلي الرأس والذنب، وليس شيء من الاعتبارين بواجب ليصح انحصار الجهات في الست. أقسام الجهات علو وسفل وأربعة وضعية. قال ( والطبيعي منها) العلو والسفل، والبواقي وضعية، تتبدل كالمواجه للمشرق إذا واجه المغرب بخلاف المنكوس. أي من الجهات جهة العلو وهي ما يلي رأس الإنسان بالطبع، والسفل ما يلي قدميه بالطبع حيث لا تتبدلان أصلا، والأربعة الباقية وضعية تتبدل بتبدل الأوضاع، كالمتوجه إلى المشرق يكون المشرق قدامه والمغرب خلفه، والجنوب يمينه والشمال شماله، ثم إذا توجه إلى المغرب صار المغرب قدامه والمشرق خلفه، والشمال يمينه والجنوب شماله بخلاف ما إذا صار القائم منكوساً، فإنه لا يصير ما يلي رجله تحتاً، وما يلي رأسه فوقاً، بل يصير رأسه من تحت، ورجله من فوق، والفوق والتحت بحالهما، فالشخصان القائمان على طرفي قطر الأرض يكون رأس كل منهما فوق ورجلهما تحت. قال والعلو لا يلزم أن يكون بالإضافة إلى السفل. يريد دفع ما سبق إلى كثير من الأوهام وهو أن الفوق والتحت متضايفان لا يعقل كل منهما إلا بالقياس إلى الآخر، وكذا القدام والخلف واليمين والشمال، والحق أن التضايف إنما هو بين الفوق وذي الفوق، وكذا البواقي، وأما الجهتان فقد تنفكان في التعقل بل في الوجود كما في الأرض، فإنه لا تحت لها إلا بالوهم، فإن جميع أطراف امتدادتها الفعلية إلى السماء فلها الفوق فقط.
And the back is what follows the head and the tail, and neither of the two considerations is necessary for the six directions to be confined. The divisions of directions: up, down, and four positional. He said: 'The natural ones are up and down; the rest are positional, changing, like one facing east when he faces west, unlike the inverted.' That is, among the directions, the direction of up is what follows a person's head by nature, and down is what follows his feet by nature, where they do not change at all. The remaining four are positional, changing with change of positions, like one facing east: east is in front of him, west behind him, south to his right, north to his left; then if he faces west, west becomes in front, east behind, north to his right, south to his left. Unlike when the standing person is inverted: what follows his foot does not become down, nor what follows his head become up; rather his head becomes down and his foot up, while up and down remain as they are. Thus two persons standing at opposite ends of the earth's diameter each have their head up and their feet down. He said: 'Up does not necessarily require being in relation to down.' He intends to refute a notion that occurs to many minds, namely that up and down are correlatives, each not conceivable except in relation to the other, and likewise front and back, right and left. The truth is that correlation is only between 'up' and 'that which is above', and likewise for the rest. As for the two directions themselves, they can be separated in conception and even in existence, as in the earth: it has no down except by imagination, for all its actual extensions terminate at the sky, so it has only up.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage classifies the six directions (al-jiha) and clarifies the nature of up and down. The author begins by stating that the six directions (front, back, up, down, right, left) are exhaustive, without assuming any particular orientation. He divides them into natural (tabiʿiyya)—up and down—which are fixed relative to a person's head and feet and do not change, and positional (waḍʿiyya)—the remaining four—which change with orientation. He illustrates by reversing direction: if a person faces east, east is front and west is back; if he turns to face west, the assignments swap. The exception is an inverted person: even upside down, the direction of his head remains 'up' and his feet 'down' semantically, showing that up and down are not relative to the body's orientation but to a fixed natural reference. He then addresses a common misunderstanding: up and down are not correlatives (mutadāyifān) like father and son; rather, the correlation is between 'up' and 'that which is above' (dhu al-fawq). The directions themselves can be conceived independently. Proof: the earth has no 'down' in reality, only in imagination, because all its actual extensions reach toward the sky—thus it has only 'up'. This reinforces the objective reality of these directions.

Correction: The passage argues that up and down are not correlative; the true correlation is between 'up' and 'that which is above'. The earth has only up, showing independence.

Key Takeaway

Ibn Sina's Logical Definition of Genus
Ibn Sina defines genus with exclusions and mentions its opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand ibn Sina's definition of genus and what it excludes, as well as the logical opposite of genus.

Core Idea
Genus is a universal answering 'what is it?' for many species.

This lesson focuses on ibn Sina's precise definition of jins (genus) in logic. The definition is carefully crafted to exclude other predicables like species, differentia, and accidents. Understanding this definition is foundational for Aristotelian logic and its use in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and exclusions
Read the Arabic original and translation. Note that ibn Sina defines jins as 'predicated of many differing in species' and then lists what is excluded.
The phrase 'fi jawab ma huwa' is crucial for exclusions.
2
Identify the excluded terms
Identify the four terms excluded: species (naw'), property (khassah), proximate differentia (fasl qareeb), remote differentia (fasl ba'eed), and general accident (arad 'amm). Understand why each is excluded.
The definition excludes both proximate and remote differentia by different phrases.
3
Understand the logical opposite
The passage also mentions the opposite of genus: the specific (naw'i), which is predicated of the species. Grasp this distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن سينا الجنس هو المقول على كثيرين مختلفين بالأنواع أي بالصور والحقائق الذاتية وهذا يخرج النوع، والخاصة، والفصل القريب وقوله (في) جواب ما هو يخرج الفصل البعيد والعرض العام. والجنس هو المقول على الجنس ويقابله النوعي وهو المقول على النوع والجنس عند الفقهاء هو المقول على كثيرين مختلفين بالأحكام قول أبي يوسف أو المقول على كثيرين مختلفين صورة ومعنى قول أبي حنيفة والجنس في علم الأحياء جماعة انواع نباتية أو حيوانية لها صفات مشتركة (معجم الألفاظ الزراعية للأمير مصطفى الشهابي وهو قسم من الفصيلة والجنس إما قريب أو بعيد، فإن كان الجواب عن الماهية وعن كل ما يشاركها في ذلك الجنس واحد فهو قريب كالحيوان بالنسبة الى الانسان فإنه جواب عن الانسان وعن كل ما يشاركه في الحيوانية وإن كان الجواب عنها وعن جميع مشاركاتها في ذلك الجنس متعدداً فهو بعيد كالجسم النامي بالنسبة الى الانسان فإنه جواب عن الانسان وعن بعض ما يشاركه فيه كالنبات
Ibn Sina said: The genus (jins) is the universal predicated of many differing in species, i.e., in forms and essential realities. This excludes species (naw'), property (khassah), and proximate differentia (fasl qareeb). And his saying 'in' (fi) as an answer to 'what is it?' excludes the remote differentia (fasl ba'eed) and general accident ('arad 'amm). The genus is predicated of the genus, and its opposite is the specific (naw'i), which is predicated of the species. The genus according to the jurists is the universal predicated of many differing in rulings, according to Abu Yusuf, or the universal predicated of many differing in form and meaning, according to Abu Hanifah. The genus in biology is a group of plant or animal species that have common characteristics (from the lexical dictionary). The genus is either proximate (qareeb) or remote (ba'eed). If the answer to the quiddity and everything sharing that genus is one, it is proximate, like 'animal' with respect to man, for it answers for man and everything sharing in animality. If the answer to it and all its participants in that genus is multiple, it is remote, like 'growing body' with respect to man, for it answers for man and some things sharing in it, like plants.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the term jins (genus) as used in logic, fiqh, and biology. It begins with Ibn Sina's definition, then explains what it excludes. It then gives the legal definition from Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifah, and ends with examples of proximate and remote genus. This is an explanatory note, likely added by the author or editor to clarify the term used in the previous discussion.

Key Terms

نوع
خاصة
فصل قريب
فصل بعيد
عرض عام
نوعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that genus is simply any universal term, like 'man' is a genus of individuals.
Correction: Genus must be predicated of many differing in species; 'man' is a species, not a genus, because it differs only in number, not in species.

Key Takeaway

Juristic and Biological Genus; Proximate vs Remote
Juristic and biological definitions of genus, and the classification into proximate and remote.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how genus is defined in fiqh and biology, and the distinction between proximate and remote genus with examples.

Core Idea
Genus is defined differently in law and biology, and can be proximate or remote.

After ibn Sina's logical definition, the author presents alternative definitions used by jurists and biologists, then explains the classification of genus into proximate and remote. This lesson shows that the term 'jins' has context-dependent meanings, yet the logical distinction of proximity is crucial for clear definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the juristic definitions
Read the definitions from Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifah. Note how they differ from the logical definition by using 'rulings' or 'form and meaning' instead of 'species'.
2
Read the biological definition
The passage gives a modern biological definition: a group of plant or animal species with common characteristics. This is close to modern taxonomy.
This definition is from an agricultural lexicon.
3
Understand proximate vs remote genus
The passage explains that if the answer to 'what is it?' is the same for the species and all its participants, the genus is proximate (e.g., animal for man). If the answer varies (e.g., body for man vs. plant), it is remote.
Note the examples: animal (proximate) vs. growing body (remote).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن سينا الجنس هو المقول على كثيرين مختلفين بالأنواع أي بالصور والحقائق الذاتية وهذا يخرج النوع، والخاصة، والفصل القريب وقوله (في) جواب ما هو يخرج الفصل البعيد والعرض العام. والجنس هو المقول على الجنس ويقابله النوعي وهو المقول على النوع والجنس عند الفقهاء هو المقول على كثيرين مختلفين بالأحكام قول أبي يوسف أو المقول على كثيرين مختلفين صورة ومعنى قول أبي حنيفة والجنس في علم الأحياء جماعة انواع نباتية أو حيوانية لها صفات مشتركة (معجم الألفاظ الزراعية للأمير مصطفى الشهابي وهو قسم من الفصيلة والجنس إما قريب أو بعيد، فإن كان الجواب عن الماهية وعن كل ما يشاركها في ذلك الجنس واحد فهو قريب كالحيوان بالنسبة الى الانسان فإنه جواب عن الانسان وعن كل ما يشاركه في الحيوانية وإن كان الجواب عنها وعن جميع مشاركاتها في ذلك الجنس متعدداً فهو بعيد كالجسم النامي بالنسبة الى الانسان فإنه جواب عن الانسان وعن بعض ما يشاركه فيه كالنبات
Ibn Sina said: The genus (jins) is the universal predicated of many differing in species, i.e., in forms and essential realities. This excludes species (naw'), property (khassah), and proximate differentia (fasl qareeb). And his saying 'in' (fi) as an answer to 'what is it?' excludes the remote differentia (fasl ba'eed) and general accident ('arad 'amm). The genus is predicated of the genus, and its opposite is the specific (naw'i), which is predicated of the species. The genus according to the jurists is the universal predicated of many differing in rulings, according to Abu Yusuf, or the universal predicated of many differing in form and meaning, according to Abu Hanifah. The genus in biology is a group of plant or animal species that have common characteristics (from the lexical dictionary). The genus is either proximate (qareeb) or remote (ba'eed). If the answer to the quiddity and everything sharing that genus is one, it is proximate, like 'animal' with respect to man, for it answers for man and everything sharing in animality. If the answer to it and all its participants in that genus is multiple, it is remote, like 'growing body' with respect to man, for it answers for man and some things sharing in it, like plants.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the term jins (genus) as used in logic, fiqh, and biology. It begins with Ibn Sina's definition, then explains what it excludes. It then gives the legal definition from Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifah, and ends with examples of proximate and remote genus. This is an explanatory note, likely added by the author or editor to clarify the term used in the previous discussion.

Correction: The passage shows that genus is used in fiqh (legal rulings) and biology (taxonomy), demonstrating its practical applications.

Checkpoint

The Origin and Meaning of 'Inniyya'
A footnote defines 'inniyya' and discusses its etymology and variant readings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the term 'inniyya' in Islamic philosophy and the debates about its linguistic origin.

Core Idea
'Inniyya' is a philosophical coinage, not a classical Arabic word, possibly influenced by Greek.

This footnote from a theological commentary defines the term 'inniyya' and explores its linguistic roots. The author presents different scholarly views on whether it derives from the Arabic particle 'inna' or is an Arabicized form of the Greek word 'ein'. You'll see how the term's etymology connects to its philosophical use.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Begin by reading the opening definition of 'inniyya' as 'the realization of concrete existence'.
2
Examine the derivation from 'inna'
Consider Abu al-Baqa's argument that 'inniyya' comes from the particle 'inna' for emphasis, and why he thinks it applies to God.
3
Explore the Greek origin and voweling errors
Learn about the modern claim that 'inniyya' is Arabicized from Greek, and why alternative voweling suggestions are mistaken.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإنية : اصطلاح فلسفي قديم معناه تحقيق الوجود العيني زعم أبو البقاء : أنه مشتق من (إن) التي تفيد في اللغة العربية التأكيد والقوة في الوجود لذاته، لكونه اكمل الموجودات في تأكيد الموجود قال : ولهذا أطلقت الفلاسفة لفظ (الإنية) على واجب الوجود لذاته لكونه اكمل الموجودات في تأكيد الوجود وفي قوة الموجود وهذا لفظ محدث ليس من كلام العرب (كليات أبي البقاء) وزعم بعض المحدثين أن الإنية لفظ معرَّب عن كلمة (اين) اليونانية التي معناها كان أو وجد، واختلفوا في ضبط هذه الكلمة فقرأها بعضهم آنية كما في تعريفات الجرجاني وهذا خطأ لأن الآنية نسبة الى الآن بعضهم أنية نسبة الى ان المخففة وضبطها آخرون بالأيية والأينية وهذا كله خطأ لأن الأينية نسبة الى الأين والايية نسب الى أي ونعتقد ان اشتقاق اللفظ من (ان) لا يمنع ان يكون بينه وبين اين اليونانية تشابه .
al-Inniyya: an old philosophical term meaning the realization of concrete existence. Abū al-Baqā claimed that it is derived from 'inna' which in the Arabic language indicates emphasis and strength in existence itself, because it is the most perfect existent in the emphasis of existence. He said: For this reason, the philosophers applied the term 'al-inniyya' to the Necessary Existence in itself, because it is the most perfect existent in the emphasis of existence and in the power of existence. And this is a coined word, not from the speech of the Arabs (Kulliyyāt Abī al-Baqā). Some moderns claimed that 'al-inniyya' is an Arabicized word from the Greek word 'ein' meaning 'was' or 'existed'. They differed in the voweling of this word: some read it as 'āniyya' as in al-Jurjānī's definitions, and this is a mistake because 'āniyya' is a relation to 'al-ān' (the now); some read it as 'inniyya' as a relation to the lightened 'in'; others vowelled it as 'ayyiya' and 'ayniyya'. All this is wrong because 'ayniyya' is a relation to 'ayn' (essence) and 'ayyiya' is a relation to 'ayy' (which). And we believe that the derivation of the word from 'in' does not prevent resemblance between it and the Greek 'ein'.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This footnote explains the philosophical term 'inniyya' (often translated as 'quiddity' or 'existence'). It begins by defining it as 'the realization of concrete existence' (taḥqīq al-wujūd al-ʿaynī). The commentator then cites Abū al-Baqā, who claimed the term is derived from the Arabic particle 'inna', which conveys emphasis and strength. Because the Necessary Existence (Wājib al-Wujūd) is the most emphatic in existence, philosophers applied 'inniyya' to God. However, Abū al-Baqā notes that this is a coined term (muhdath), not from classical Arabic. Other modern scholars claimed it is Arabicized from the Greek 'ein' (to be). The footnote then discusses variants in voweling: some read it as 'āniyya' (relating to 'al-ān' = the now), others as 'inniyya' (relating to the lightened 'in'), others as 'ayyiya' or 'ayniyya' (relating to 'ayy' or 'ayn'). The author dismisses these as mistakes, as they confuse the root. He concludes that deriving it from 'in' does not preclude a resemblance to the Greek word. The passage shows the terminological debates among Muslim scholars about the origin of a key philosophical term.

أبو البقاء
واجب الوجود
لفظ محدث
معرب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: 'Inniyya' is an authentic classical Arabic word.
Correction: The footnote clearly states that it is a coined philosophical term (muhdath) and not from classical Arabic.

Checkpoint

B) من 'إنّ'
C) من 'أيّ'

Key Takeaway
The term 'inniyya' is a philosophical coinage with debated etymology, but clearly denotes the concrete existence of the Necessary Being.

Proof from Universality and Its Refutation
Author presents a proof and its refutation using universality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof for the impossibility of an infinite regress using the universality argument and why it fails due to the fallacy of composition.

Core Idea
What is true of each part is not always true of the whole.

Theologians have offered various proofs for the impossibility of an infinite chain of past events. One such proof relies on a logical move from the property of each individual event to the entire series. However, as the refutation shows, this move is not always valid. Let's examine the proof and its critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the author uses an analogy about black people.
2
Identify the proof
The proof states: since every event is preceded by non-existence, the totality of all events must also be preceded by non-existence. This is like saying: if every black person is black, then the group of all black people is black.
The key move is from each part to the whole.
3
Examine the refutation
The refutation denies the universality of the judgment. It points out that 'every black person is an individual' is true, but the group is not an individual. Thus, the analogy fails.
This is an example of the fallacy of composition.
4
Reflect on the fallacy
Consider why the proof fails: a property of each part does not necessarily apply to the whole. Apply this insight to other contexts.
This logical mistake is common in theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لبيان امتناع تعاقب الحوادث لا إلى بداية ونهاية وجوه أخرى منها : أنه لما كان كل حادث مسبوقاً بالعدم، كان الكل كذلك، فإنه إذا كان كل زنجي أسود، كان الكل أسود ضرورة وردّ بمنع كلية هذا الحكم، ألا ترى أن كل زنجي فرد، وبعض من المجموع بخلاف الكل.
To demonstrate the impossibility of succession of events without beginning or end, there are other aspects: that since every event is preceded by non-existence, the totality is likewise. For if every black person is black, the totality is necessarily black. It is refuted by denying the universality of this judgment. Do you not see that every black person is an individual, while some of the collective are not like the totality?
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents one of the proofs for the impossibility of an infinite regress of past events, using an analogy. The author states that since each individual event is preceded by non-existence, the entire set of events must also be preceded by non-existence. This is analogous to saying if every individual black person is black, then the totality (all black persons) is black. The refutation points out that the universal judgment does not hold: a property of each individual (being an individual) does not apply to the whole. So the proof fails.

Key Terms
حادث
عدم
زنجي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if a property belongs to every member of a set, it must also belong to the set as a whole.
Correction: This is the fallacy of composition. The refutation shows that 'being an individual' is true of each black person but not of the group.

Checkpoint
How does the refutation object to the proof from universality?
A) It denies the existence of black people.
B) It points out that a property of each part may not apply to the whole.
C) It claims events are eternal.
D) It asserts that non-existence is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The proof from universality fails because a property of each individual does not necessarily apply to the collective.

Eternity is Not a Container for Non-Existences
Objection that eternity is not a container; infinity prevents simultaneity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that eternity is not a container for non-existences and why infinite non-existences need not be simultaneous.

Core Idea
Eternity does not gather all non-existences; infinity prevents simultaneity.

This lesson presents a subtle objection to arguments that assume all eternal non-existences must coexist. The objector clarifies what 'eternal non-existence' truly means and explains why infinity breaks the apparent necessity of simultaneity. By carefully distinguishing between finite and infinite cases, the passage defends the possibility of infinite past non-existences without logical contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the key distinction
The objector distinguishes between two interpretations of 'eternity of non-existences': as a container or as simply not being preceded by existence. Find where this distinction is made.
Notice the phrase 'بل معنى أزلية العدمات أنها ليست مسبوقة بالوجودات'.
3
Understand the role of infinity
The objector responds to a potential counterargument about succession. See why the counterargument only applies to finite numbers and how infinity explains the lack of simultaneity.
Focus on the sentence: 'إنما يستقيم فيما يتناهى عدده'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض بأن الأزل ليس عبارة عن حالة مخصوصة شبيهة بالظرف يجتمع فيها عدمات الحوادث حتى لو وجد فيها شيء من وجوداتها لزم اجتماع النقيضين بل معنى أزلية العدمات أنها ليست مسبوقة بالوجودات، وهذا لا يوجب تفاوتها في شيء من الأوقات وما يقال أنها لو لم تكن متقارنة في حين ما لكان حصول بعضها بعد آخر فلا يكون قديماً، إنما يستقيم فيما يتناهى عدده، فالعدمات لا تتقارن في حين ما لعدم تناهيها لا لتعاقبها.
And it was objected that al-azal (eternity) is not a particular state resembling a container in which the non-existences of temporal events come together, such that if something of their existences occurred in it, the coexistence of opposites would be necessitated. Rather, the meaning of the eternity of non-existences is that they are not preceded by existences, and this does not necessitate their being distinguished [by being together] in any of the times. And what is said that if they were not simultaneous in some instant, then some of them would occur after others and thus not be eternal, is only valid for a finite number. So the non-existences are not simultaneous in any instant because of their infinity, not because of their succession.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is an objection to arguments that rely on the impossibility of simultaneous eternal non-existences. The objector argues that eternity (al-azal) is not a container or specific state where non-existences gather; rather, the meaning of 'eternal non-existence' is simply that the non-existence is not preceded by existence. This does not require all non-existences to be together at one time. Regarding the claim that if non-existences are not simultaneous at a single moment, some would occur after others and thus not be eternal, the objector says this only holds for finite numbers. For infinitely many non-existences, they are not simultaneous not because they are successive, but because they are infinite. Thus, the objection denies the reductio argument that assumed all non-existences must coexist in eternity.

Key Terms
الأزل
عدمات
أزلية العدمات
التقارن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If non-existences are eternal, they must all be simultaneous.
Correction: Eternity of non-existence means not preceded by existence, not that they are together in a container. Infinity prevents simultaneity.

Checkpoint
What does the objector say is the meaning of the eternity of non-existences?
A) They are all simultaneous in a container called eternity.
B) They are not preceded by existences.
C) They are infinite in number and thus impossible.
D) They occur after each other in an infinite series.

Key Takeaway
The objection shows that eternal non-existences need not be simultaneous; their infinity accounts for the lack of simultaneity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you willing to accept that your human reasoning has limits when it comes to the infinite? Will you humbly submit to revelation even when it defies your finite categories?

Philosophical Definition of Substance (Jawhar)
Editor presents philosophical definitions and classification of jawhar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how philosophers define substance as the existent not in a subject and its classification into form, matter, body, soul, or intellect.

Core Idea
Substance is what exists not in a subject; it is the philosophical bedrock of all else.

The philosophers (falasifa) had a precise technical definition of substance (jawhar) that differs from everyday usage. This passage presents Ibn Sina's definition and Aristotle's tradition, showing how substances are classified into five types based on whether they are forms, matter, bodies, souls, or intellects. Understanding this classification is key to grasping philosophical cosmology and its relation to kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic continuous passage once and then the English translation. Let the definitions settle before analysing.
Notice the technical terms like 'موجود قائم بنفسه' and 'محل'.
2
Identify the four preliminary meanings
The author lists four initial meanings of jawhar. Find each one and notice how they all centre on independence from a subject.
These meanings prepare for the precise definition.
3
Study Ibn Sina's definition
Read Ibn Sina's definition: 'الجوهر هو كل ما وجود ذاته ليس في موضوع' – substance is everything whose existence is not in a subject. Understand what 'not in a subject' means.
This is the authoritative definition.
4
Understand the classification of substances
The passage ends with a conditional classification: if substance is in another, it is a form; if it hosts another, it is matter; if composite, it is a body; else soul or intellect. Trace each case.
This classification shows the hierarchy of existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويطلق الجوهر عند الفلاسفة على معان منها الموجود القائم بنفسه حادثاً كان أو قديماً ويقابله العرض، ومنها الذات القابلة لتوارد الصفات المتضادة عليها ومنها الماهية التي اذا وجدت في الأعيان كانت لا في موضوع ، ومنها الموجود الغني عن محل يحل فيه . قال ابن سينا الجوهر هو كل ما وجود ذاته ليس في موضوع اي في محل قريب قد قام بنفسه دونه لا بتقويمه النجاة ص (١٢٦) وقال أيضاً ويقال : لكل ذات وجوده ليس في موضوع وعليه اصطلح الفلاسفة القدماء من عهد أرسطو (رسالة الحدود) والخلاصة أن الجوهر هو الموجود لا في موضوع ويقابله العرض بمعنى الموجود اى في محل مقوم لما حل فيه، فإن كان الجوهر حالاً في جوهر آخر كان صورة اما جسمية أو نوعية، وان كان محلاً لجوهر آخر كان هيولي، وان كان مركباً منهما كان جسماً وان لم يكن كذلك كان نفساً أو عقلاً
And substance is used by the philosophers for meanings, among them: the existent subsisting by itself, whether temporal or pre-eternal, and its opposite is accident; and among them: the essence receptive to the succession of opposite attributes; and among them: the quiddity which, when it exists in external individuals, is not in a subject; and among them: the existent independent of a locus in which it inheres. Ibn Sina said: Substance is everything whose existence of its essence is not in a subject, i.e., in a proximate locus that subsists by itself without it, not by constituting it (al-Najat, p. 126). And he also said: And it is said: For every essence its existence is not in a subject, and upon this the ancient philosophers from the time of Aristotle agreed (Risalah al-Hudud). The summary is that substance is the existent not in a subject, and its opposite is accident in the sense of the existent in a locus constituting what inheres in it. So if the substance is indwelling in another substance, it is a form, either corporeal or specific; and if it is a locus for another substance, it is matter; and if it is composed of both, it is a body; and if it is not like that, it is a soul or an intellect.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed philosophical definition of the term 'jawhar' (substance) from the perspective of the philosophers (falasifa). It begins by listing four meanings: (1) that which subsists by itself, as opposed to accident; (2) the essence receptive to opposite attributes; (3) the quiddity that when it exists externally is not in a subject; (4) the existent independent of a locus. Then it quotes Ibn Sina's definition from al-Najat: substance is that whose existence of its essence is not in a subject, meaning it is not in a proximate locus that subsists by itself without it, nor is it constituted by that locus. It also mentions that the ancient philosophers from Aristotle agreed on the definition of substance as that which is not in a subject. The summary concludes that substance is the existent not in a subject, opposed to accident which is the existent in a locus that constitutes what inheres in it. The passage further classifies substances: if a substance dwells in another substance, it is a form (corporeal or specific); if it is a locus for another substance, it is matter; if composed of both, it is a body; otherwise, it is a soul or an intellect.

Key Terms
الجوهر
العرض
الموجود القائم بنفسه
الماهية
الهيولي
الصورة
الجسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'jawhar' only means atom in kalam, but here it includes broader philosophical meanings such as soul and intellect.
Correction: In philosophy, jawhar is any existent not in a subject, including forms, matter, bodies, souls, and intellects.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina's definition, what is the distinguishing characteristic of a substance (jawhar)?
A) It is pre-eternal.
B) Its existence is not in a subject.
C) It is composed of matter and form.
D) It is receptive to opposite attributes.

Key Takeaway
For philosophers, substance is defined as that which is not in a subject, and its types reveal the hierarchy of existence from matter to intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Philosophical Definition of Substance
Sanusi quotes Ibn Sina defining jawhar and listing its types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical definition of substance (jawhar) as a self-subsistent entity and its classification into form, hyle, body, soul, and intellect.

Core Idea
Substance is what exists not in a subject; accidents depend on substances.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the definition of substance (jawhar) as understood by the Muslim philosophers (falasifa), particularly Ibn Sina. Unlike the kalam concept of atom, the philosophers define substance broadly as any self-subsistent entity. Understanding this definition is crucial for grasping later discussions about the soul, intellect, and the physical world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall structure. Then read the English translation to understand the meaning without overanalysis.
The passage uses technical terms from Aristotelian philosophy.
2
Identify the core definition of jawhar
Locate the definition from Ibn Sina: 'al-jawhar huwa kull ma wujud dhatihi laysa fi mawdu''. Understand how it opposes 'arad (accident).
The definition emphasizes self-subsistence.
3
Explore the classification of substances
Notice how Ibn Sina classifies substances based on inherence: form, hyle, body, soul, intellect. Relate each to its role.
This classification is a key part of the philosophical worldview.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويطلق الجوهر عند الفلاسفة على معان منها الموجود القائم بنفسه حادثاً كان أو قديماً ويقابله العرض، ومنها الذات القابلة لتوارد الصفات المتضادة عليها ومنها الماهية التي اذا وجدت في الأعيان كانت لا في موضوع ، ومنها الموجود الغني عن محل يحل فيه . قال ابن سينا الجوهر هو كل ما وجود ذاته ليس في موضوع اي في محل قريب قد قام بنفسه دونه لا بتقويمه النجاة ص (١٢٦) وقال أيضاً : ويقال : لكل ذات وجوده ليس في موضوع وعليه اصطلح الفلاسفة القدماء من عهد أرسطو (رسالة الحدود) والخلاصة أن الجوهر هو الموجود لا في موضوع ويقابله العرض بمعنى الموجود اى في محل مقوم لما حل فيه، فإن كان الجوهر حالاً في جوهر آخر كان صورة اما جسمية أو نوعية، وان كان محلاً لجوهر آخر كان هيولي، وان كان مركباً منهما كان جسماً وان لم يكن كذلك كان نفساً أو عقلاً
And the term 'substance' (jawhar) is used by the philosophers for meanings, among them: the self-subsistent existent, whether originated or eternal, and its opposite is accident; and [among them] the essence capable of receiving opposite attributes; and [among them] the quiddity which, when it exists in individuals, exists not in a subject; and [among them] the existent independent of a locus in which it inheres. Ibn Sina said: Substance is everything whose existence of its essence is not in a subject, i.e., in an immediate locus that subsists by itself without it, not by constituting it (al-Najat, p. 126). And he also said: It is said for every essence whose existence is not in a subject, and upon this the ancient philosophers from the time of Aristotle agreed (Risalah al-Hudud). The summary is that substance is the existent not in a subject, and its opposite is accident in the sense of the existent that is in a locus constituting that in which it inheres. Then if the substance inheres in another substance, it is a form, either corporeal or specific; and if it is a locus for another substance, it is hyle; and if it is composed of both, it is a body; and if it is not like that, it is soul or intellect.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage provides a definition of 'substance' (jawhar) from the perspective of the falasifa (philosophers), particularly Avicenna (Ibn Sina). It lists multiple meanings of jawhar: (1) the self-subsistent existent (al-mawjud al-qa'im bi-nafsihi), whether originated or eternal, opposite to accident; (2) the essence capable of receiving opposite attributes (al-dhat al-qabila li-tawarud al-sifat al-mutadaddah); (3) the quiddity which, when instantiated, exists not in a subject; (4) the existent independent of a locus. Then it quotes Ibn Sina's definition from al-Najat: substance is everything whose existence of its essence is not in a subject (i.e., not in an immediate locus). It also notes that this is the definition agreed upon by ancient philosophers since Aristotle. The summary is that substance is the existent not in a subject, and accident (arad) is the opposite (the existent in a subject). The passage then classifies substances based on inherence: if one substance inheres in another, it is a form (surah) – either corporeal (jismiyyah) or specific (naw'iyyah); if it is a locus for another substance, it is hyle (hayula); if composed of both (form and matter), it is a body (jism); otherwise it is soul (nafs) or intellect ('aql). This classification follows the Aristotelian–Neoplatonic framework adopted by the falasifa.

عرض
موضوع
صورة
هيولي
نفس
عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The kalam concept of substance (al-jawhar al-fard) is the same as the philosophers' definition.
Correction: The philosophers define substance broadly as any self-subsistent entity, including souls and intellects, while kalam uses 'jawhar' to mean the indivisible atom.

Definition of Burhan (Demonstrative Proof)
The footnote defines burhan and its two types in philosophical logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the lexical and technical definition of burhan and its two main types in philosophical logic: burhan limmi and burhan inni.

Core Idea
Burhan is a certain syllogism that reveals the cause of a conclusion.

This lesson explores the term 'burhan', which is central to Islamic logic and kalam. The passage defines burhan as a decisive proof, and distinguishes between the juristic and philosophical understandings. For philosophers, burhan is a syllogism composed of certainties, and it splits into two types based on whether the middle term is the cause. This distinction is foundational for assessing demonstrations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and definitions
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the lexical roots and the hadith citation.
Notice the range of meaning from 'decisive proof' to 'syllogism'.
2
Distinguish the two usages
Notice how the jurists and philosophers define burhan differently. Focus on the philosophical definition.
For jurists, burhan separates truth from falsehood; for philosophers, it is a certain syllogism.
3
Identify the two types
Examine the description of burhan limmi and burhan inni. Understand the role of the middle term as cause in burhan limmi.
Burhan limmi gives the cause of the relation; burhan inni is the other type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان هو الحجة الفاصلة البينة يقال برهن يبرهن برهنة اذا جاء بحجة قاطعة للدد الخصومة، وبرهن بمعنى بين وبرهن عليه أقام الحجة وفي الحديث: «الصدق «برهان» البرهان هنا الحجة او الدليل والبرهان عند الأصوليين ما فصل الحق عن الباطل وميز الصحيح عن الفاسد بالبيان الذي فيه (تعريفات الجرجاني) وهي أما عند الفلاسفة : فهو القياس المؤلف من اليقينيات سواء كان ابتداء وهي الضروريات أو بواسطة النظريات تعريفات الجرجاني) قال ابن سينا البرهان قياس مؤلف من يقينيات لانتاج يقيني النجاة ص ۱۰۳) والحد الأوسط في هذا القياس لا بد من ان يكون علة نسبة الأكبر الى الأصغر فإذا اعطاك علة اجتماع طرفي النتيجة في الذهن والوجود معاً سمي برهان اللم، قال ابن سينا البرهان المطلق هو برهان اللم وبرهان الان راجع) النجاة ص ١٠٣ - ١٠٤ )
Al-burhan is the decisive, clear proof. One says 'barhana, yubarhinu, barhanatan' when he brings a decisive argument that cuts off dispute. And 'barhana' means to clarify, and 'barhana 'alayhi' means to establish proof. In the hadith: 'Truth is a burhan.' Burhan here means proof or evidence. According to the jurists (usuliyyun), burhan is that which separates truth from falsehood and distinguishes correct from incorrect through clear exposition (from al-Jurjani's Definitions). As for the philosophers, it is a syllogism composed of certainties, whether they are primary (necessary) or theoretical (derived) (from al-Jurjani's Definitions). Ibn Sina said: 'Burhan is a syllogism composed of certainties to produce a certain conclusion' (al-Najat p. 103). The middle term in this syllogism must be the cause of the relation of the major to the minor. If it gives you the cause of the combination of the two terms of the conclusion in both mind and existence, it is called burhan limmi (demonstration of reason). Ibn Sina said: 'Absolute burhan is burhan limmi, and burhan inni (demonstration of fact) is something else' (cf. al-Najat pp. 103-104).
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a lexical and technical definition of the term 'burhan'. It begins by stating that burhan is a decisive, clear proof. The verb forms are given, and a hadith is cited where truth is equated with burhan. Then it distinguishes between the usage of burhan among jurists (usuliyyun) and philosophers. For jurists, burhan is what separates truth from falsehood. For philosophers, it is a syllogism composed of certainties, and it is divided into two types: burhan limmi (demonstration of the reason why) and burhan inni (demonstration of fact). The middle term in burhan limmi must be the cause of the relation. The passage cites Ibn Sina to clarify that absolute burhan is burhan limmi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Burhan is understood as any persuasive argument or proof.
Correction: Burhan is specifically a syllogism composed of certainties that produces a certain conclusion, not merely any argument.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what is a burhan?
A) Any argument that is persuasive
B) A syllogism composed of certainties producing a certain conclusion
C) A proof that separates truth from falsehood
D) A hadith about truth

Key Takeaway
Burhan is a certain syllogism whose middle term reveals the cause, and it is divided into burhan limmi and burhan inni.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Ibn Sina describes burhan limmi as revealing the cause of a conclusion. Do you know the reasons behind your acts of worship, or do you perform them mechanically? How might seeking the 'cause' transform your devotion from ritual to conviction?

Definition of Imkan (Possibility)
The footnote defines imkan linguistically and technically as a modality between necessity and impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of 'imkan' (possibility) as a modality between necessity and impossibility.

Core Idea
Possibility is the middle ground between necessity and impossibility.

In this passage, the author clarifies the term 'imkan' both linguistically and as a technical concept in kalam. The key insight is that possibility is not just 'not impossible,' but a distinct modality that lies between the necessary and the impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the author's concise style.
The passage ends with a book reference.
2
Identify the linguistic definition
Understand the linguistic root: 'imkan' is the verbal noun of 'amkana', like 'ikram' from 'akrama'.
3
Understand the technical definition
For early scholars, imkan is the manifestation of potentiality into actuality. Grasp this conceptual shift.
This connects to the broader kalam framework.
4
Analyze the analogy
The author uses an analogy of a line: Necessary at one end, Impossible at the other, and Possible in the middle. Note how the possible can be near or far from the ends.
This clarifies that possibility admits degrees.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this threefold distinction is foundational for rational theology. It provides a clear logical framework.
This is a key building block for later arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإمكان في اللغة مصدر أمكن امكانا كما تقول اكرم اكراماً وهو أيضا مصدر امكن الشيء من ذاته . والامكان في الشيء عند المتقدمين هو إظهار ما في قوته الى الفعل وذلك انك اذا تصورت طبيعة الواجب كان طرفاً وازائه في الطرف الآخر طبيعة الممتنع وبينهما طبيعة الممكن والمسافة التي بين الواجب والممتنع اذا لحظت وسطها على الصحة فهو أحق شيء واولاه بطبيعة الممكن وكلما قربت هذه النقطة التي كانت وسطاً الى أحد الطرفين كان ممكنا بشرط وتقييد، فقيل ممكن قريب من الواجب وممكن بعيد عنه أبو حيان التوحيدي ومسكويه كتاب) الهوامل والشوامل ص (۱۰۰)
Al-imkan linguistically is the verbal noun of 'amkana' meaning 'to make possible', just as you say 'akrama' for 'to honor'. It is also the verbal noun of 'amkana' meaning 'the thing is possible in itself'. As for imkan in a thing according to the early scholars (al-mutaqaddimun), it is the manifestation of what is in its potentiality to actuality. That is because when you conceive the nature of the Necessary, it is on one side, and opposite it on the other side is the nature of the Impossible, and between them is the nature of the Possible. The distance between the Necessary and the Impossible, when you observe its middle point correctly, is the most deserving and appropriate for the nature of the possible. And whenever this point, which was the middle, approaches one of the two sides, it becomes possible under a condition and restriction. So it is said: possible close to the Necessary and possible far from it. (Abu Hayyan al-Tawhidi and Miskawayh, Book of al-Hawamil wa al-Shawamil, p. 100)
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'imkan' (possibility). Linguistically, it is the verbal noun of 'amkana'. Technically, according to the early scholars, imkan is the manifestation of potentiality into actuality. The author then explains the concept using a spatial analogy: the Necessary Being is at one extreme, the Impossible at the other, and the Possible lies in the middle. The middle point, when observed correctly, best represents the nature of the possible. As this point moves closer to either extreme, it becomes qualified possibility. Thus, possibilities can be 'close to the Necessary' or 'far from it'.

Key Terms

واجب
ممتنع
ممكن

Correction: The passage defines possibility as a distinct modality between necessity and impossibility, with a specific technical meaning and degrees.

B) At the extreme of the impossible
C) In the middle between them
D) Outside both extremes

Key Takeaway
Imkan is a distinct modality that lies between the necessary and the impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What step can you take this week to explore that possibility?

What fear or false assumption is preventing you from bringing that potential into reality? Commit to one concrete action to actualize it.
?
If the middle point of the spectrum is most deserving of the nature of possibility, reflect: are you living in the balanced middle, or have you drifted toward an extreme of rigid necessity or hopeless impossibility? Where is your soul's 'midpoint' today, and what would it take to return to it?

Ibn Sina's Two Meanings of Possibility
Ibn Sina distinguishes two senses of possibility and identifies genuine possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's two definitions of possibility (imkan) and why the first sense actually refers to impossibility.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's 'possibility' has two meanings—only one is genuine contingency.

Ibn Sina, a towering figure in Islamic philosophy, offered a precise definition of possibility that differs from everyday usage. His distinction between two senses of imkan is crucial for understanding later debates about contingency and necessity. This passage lays out those two meanings and explains what genuine possibility entails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Let the author's terminology sink in before interpreting.
Pay attention to the terms 'ضرورة العدم' and 'ضرورة الوجود'.
2
Identify the two meanings of imkan
Find the two definitions of possibility: one negates necessity of non-existence, the other negates necessity in both non-existence and existence. Note that the first is identified as impossibility.
The first meaning is actually imtina' (impossibility), not genuine possibility.
3
Understand the consideration of essence
Notice that when considering the essence alone, it either necessitates existence (obligatory) or necessitates non-existence (impossible). This sets up the background for genuine contingency.
Genuine possibility requires that the essence does not necessitate either.
4
Reflect on 'potency of existence'
Ibn Sina calls genuine possibility 'potency of existence' (quwwat al-wujud), indicating the capacity for existence without necessity.
This term connects to Aristotelian potentiality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن سينا والامكان إما أن يعني به ما يلازم سلب ضرورة العدم وهو الامتناع . وأما أن يعني به ما يلازم سلب الضرورة في العدم والوجود جميعاً . فاعتبار الذات وحدها لا يخلو اما أن يكون مقتضياً لوجوب الوجود أو مقتضياً لامتناع الوجود . ونحن نسمي إمكان الوجود قوة الوجود .
Ibn Sina said: Possibility sometimes means what necessitates the negation of the necessity of non-existence, which is impossibility. And sometimes it means what necessitates the negation of necessity in both non-existence and existence. So considering the essence alone, it is either requiring the necessity of existence or requiring the impossibility of existence. And we call the possibility of existence the potency of existence.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage records Ibn Sina's definition of possibility (imkan) in two senses. The first sense is possibility that negates the necessity of non-existence but not the necessity of existence—this is actually impossibility (imtina') because the thing is necessary in existence. The second sense is possibility that negates both necessities, indicating contingency in both directions. Then Ibn Sina states that when considering the essence alone (without external factors), it either necessitates existence (i.e., is necessary) or necessitates non-existence (i.e., is impossible). The final sentence notes that they (the philosophers) call the possibility of existence 'the potency of existence' (quwwat al-wujud), referring to the capacity or potentiality for existence.

Key Terms
إمكان
ضرورة العدم
ضرورة الوجود
امتناع
قوة الوجود
اعتبار الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'imkan' always means 'possible' in the sense of contingent, but Ibn Sina's first meaning shows that it can refer to impossibility.
Correction: Ibn Sina explicitly states that the first meaning (negation of necessity of non-existence) is actually impossibility (imtina'), not contingency.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, which sense of possibility is actually impossibility?
A) The sense that negates the necessity of non-existence
B) The sense that negates the necessity of both existence and non-existence
C) The sense that affirms the necessity of existence
D) The sense that affirms the potency of existence

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina teaches that genuine possibility is the negation of necessity in both existence and non-existence, while the first sense (negation of necessity of non-existence) is actually impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Ibn Sina says that the genuinely possible thing has no inherent necessity. When you look at your own soul, do you sense that you are only 'potency of existence'—a fragile capacity that God actualizes? Or do you live as though your existence is a given?
?
What would it look like for you today to live from the reality that you are contingent—that without God's constant act of creation, you would cease to be? Name one specific habit or attitude you would change if you truly believed this.

Lexical and Mythological Definitions of Time
Lexical and mythological definitions of time (zaman).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the lexical meaning of time in Arabic and its personification in Greek mythology.

Core Idea
Time is a duration between events, and a god in mythology.

This lesson begins with the simplest and most familiar definition of time: the duration between two events. You'll see how Arabic uses 'zaman' for seasons, eras, and periods. Then, an unexpected twist: in Greek myths, time was a god who ripens all things. This contrast sets the stage for deeper philosophical debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the lexical definition
Read the Arabic and translation for the first part: time as the duration between two events, and its plural forms.
Notice the examples: time of harvest, youth, ignorance.
2
Explore the mythological definition
Read how the Greeks personified time as an aging god.
This is not a literal belief but a cultural metaphor.
3
Reflect on the shift
Ask yourself: how does the lexical definition differ from the mythological? Why would scholars include mythology?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الزمان : الوقت كثيره وقليله ، وهو المدة الواقعة بين حادثتين أولاهما ،سابقة، وثانيتهما لاحقة، ومنه زمان الحصاد وزمان الشباب وزمان الجاهلية، وجمع الزمان أزمنة اي أقسام وفصول، وتقول أيضاً : الأزمنة القديمة، والأزمنة الحديثة.
والزمان في اساطير اليونانيين: هو الاله الذي ينضج الأشياء ويوصلها الى نهايتها . والفرق بين الزمان والدهر ،والسرمد أن نسبة المتغير الى المتغير هي الزمان، ونسبة الثابت الى المتغير هي الدهر، ونسبة الثابت الى الثابت هي السرمد . وقد زعم (أرسطو) أن الزمان مقدار حركة الفلك الأعظم وذلك لأن الزمان متفاوت زيادة ونقصاناً، فهو إذن كم، وليس كماً منفصلاً لامتناع الجوهر الفرد فلا يكون مركباً من آنات متتالية فهو اذن كم متصل، الا انه غير قار، فهو اذن مقدار لهيئة غير قارة وهي الحركة . وقد أخذ معظم فلاسفة العرب بهذا المعنى الأرسطي الا أن المتكلمين زعموا أن الزمان أمر اعتباري موهوم، وعرفه الأشاعرة بقولهم : انه متجدد معلوم يقدر به متجدد آخر موهوم . وقال الرازي في المباحث المشرقية ان للزمان كالحركة معنيين أحدهما أمر موجود في الخارج، غير منقسم وهو مطابق للحركة، وثانيهما أمر متوهم لا وجود له في الخارج.
Time: time, its many and its few, and it is the duration occurring between two events, the first of which is prior and the second posterior; from it are the time of harvest, the time of youth, and the time of ignorance. The plural of time is 'azmina', meaning divisions and seasons. You also say: ancient times and modern times. And time in Greek mythology: it is the god that ripens things and brings them to their end. The difference between time (zaman), eternity (dahr), and perpetuity (sarmad) is that the relation of the changing to the changing is time, the relation of the unchanging to the changing is dahr, and the relation of the unchanging to the unchanging is sarmad. Aristotle claimed that time is the measure of the motion of the outermost sphere, because time varies in increase and decrease; thus it is quantity, but not discrete quantity due to the impossibility of the atom, so it is not composed of successive instants; thus it is continuous quantity, but it is non-stationary; thus it is a measure for a non-stationary form which is motion. Most Muslim philosophers adopted this Aristotelian meaning, except the mutakallimun who claimed that time is a mental consideration and imaginary. The Ash'aris defined it as: a renewing known thing by which another imaginary renewing thing is measured. Al-Razi said in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya that time, like motion, has two meanings: one is an existent thing in the external world, indivisible, corresponding to motion; the second is something imaginary with no existence in the external world.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive definition of the term 'zaman' (time). It begins with a lexical definition from Arabic, clarifying that zaman is the duration between two events and listing typical uses. It then moves to Greek mythology, where time is personified as a god. Next, it distinguishes three concepts: zaman (relation of changing to changing), dahr (relation of unchanging to changing), and sarmad (relation of unchanging to unchanging). Aristotle's definition is presented: time is the measure of celestial motion, a continuous but non-stationary quantity. Most Muslim philosophers followed Aristotle, but the mutakallimun considered time imaginary. The Ash'ari definition is given as 'a renewing known thing by which another imaginary renewing thing is measured.' Al-Razi's dual-aspect view concludes the passage: time has an external, indivisible aspect corresponding to motion, and an imaginary aspect with no external existence.

Distinguishing Zaman, Dahr, Sarmad, and Aristotle's Definition
Distinction of zaman, dahr, sarmad and Aristotle's definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the relational definitions of zaman, dahr, sarmad, and Aristotle's definition of time as the measure of motion.

Core Idea
Zaman relates changing to changing; dahr relates unchanging to changing; sarmad relates unchanging to unchanging. Aristotle called time the measure of celestial motion.

Now the discussion moves from simple definitions to more precise conceptual distinctions. The author presents a tripartite scheme based on what is changing or unchanging. Then Aristotle's influential definition is introduced: time is a quantity, continuous but non-stationary, tied to the motion of the outermost sphere.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three relations
Read the distinction: zaman (changing to changing), dahr (unchanging to changing), sarmad (unchanging to unchanging).
Think of examples: our daily time vs. timeless eternity.
2
Analyze Aristotle's definition
Read the Arabic and translation for Aristotle's claim: time is the measure of the outermost sphere's motion.
Notice the reasoning: time varies, so it's quantity; it's continuous because atoms are impossible; it's non-stationary because it measures motion.
3
Identify the key properties
List the properties: quantity (continuous, non-stationary), measure for a non-stationary form (motion).
This sets the stage for later critiques.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الزمان : الوقت كثيره وقليله ، وهو المدة الواقعة بين حادثتين أولاهما ،سابقة، وثانيتهما لاحقة، ومنه زمان الحصاد وزمان الشباب وزمان الجاهلية، وجمع الزمان أزمنة اي أقسام وفصول، وتقول أيضاً : الأزمنة القديمة، والأزمنة الحديثة.
والزمان في اساطير اليونانيين: هو الاله الذي ينضج الأشياء ويوصلها الى نهايتها . والفرق بين الزمان والدهر ،والسرمد أن نسبة المتغير الى المتغير هي الزمان، ونسبة الثابت الى المتغير هي الدهر، ونسبة الثابت الى الثابت هي السرمد . وقد زعم (أرسطو) أن الزمان مقدار حركة الفلك الأعظم وذلك لأن الزمان متفاوت زيادة ونقصاناً، فهو إذن كم، وليس كماً منفصلاً لامتناع الجوهر الفرد فلا يكون مركباً من آنات متتالية فهو اذن كم متصل، الا انه غير قار، فهو اذن مقدار لهيئة غير قارة وهي الحركة . وقد أخذ معظم فلاسفة العرب بهذا المعنى الأرسطي الا أن المتكلمين زعموا أن الزمان أمر اعتباري موهوم، وعرفه الأشاعرة بقولهم : انه متجدد معلوم يقدر به متجدد آخر موهوم . وقال الرازي في المباحث المشرقية ان للزمان كالحركة معنيين أحدهما أمر موجود في الخارج، غير منقسم وهو مطابق للحركة، وثانيهما أمر متوهم لا وجود له في الخارج.
Time: time, its many and its few, and it is the duration occurring between two events, the first of which is prior and the second posterior; from it are the time of harvest, the time of youth, and the time of ignorance. The plural of time is 'azmina', meaning divisions and seasons. You also say: ancient times and modern times. And time in Greek mythology: it is the god that ripens things and brings them to their end. The difference between time (zaman), eternity (dahr), and perpetuity (sarmad) is that the relation of the changing to the changing is time, the relation of the unchanging to the changing is dahr, and the relation of the unchanging to the unchanging is sarmad. Aristotle claimed that time is the measure of the motion of the outermost sphere, because time varies in increase and decrease; thus it is quantity, but not discrete quantity due to the impossibility of the atom, so it is not composed of successive instants; thus it is continuous quantity, but it is non-stationary; thus it is a measure for a non-stationary form which is motion. Most Muslim philosophers adopted this Aristotelian meaning, except the mutakallimun who claimed that time is a mental consideration and imaginary. The Ash'aris defined it as: a renewing known thing by which another imaginary renewing thing is measured. Al-Razi said in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya that time, like motion, has two meanings: one is an existent thing in the external world, indivisible, corresponding to motion; the second is something imaginary with no existence in the external world.

?

Kalam and Ash'ari Views on Time
Kalam, Ash'ari, and Razi definitions of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of time as a 'renewing known thing' and Razi's dual-aspect theory.

Core Idea
The mutakallimun considered time imaginary; Ash'aris defined it as a measuring renewing thing; Razi split it into external and imaginary aspects.

After examining the Aristotelian view, the passage turns to the theological perspective. Most Muslim philosophers accepted Aristotle, but the mutakallimun (theologians) rejected the reality of time, calling it a mental construct. The Ash'aris offered a precise definition: time is a renewing known thing used to measure another renewing (imaginary) thing. Finally, Al-Razi proposes a compromise: time has two aspects, one real and indivisible (corresponding to motion) and one imaginary.

Guided Path
1
Contrast philosophers and mutakallimun
Read that most Muslim philosophers adopted Aristotle, but the mutakallimun claimed time is imaginary (i'tibari mawhum).
This is a major split: reality vs. mental construct.
2
Learn the Ash'ari definition
Read the Ash'ari definition carefully: time is a renewing known thing by which another imaginary renewing thing is measured.
Think of what this means: time itself is real (known), but the duration it measures is imaginary?
3
Understand Razi's dual-aspect view
Read Razi's two meanings: one external and indivisible (corresponding to motion), the other imaginary with no external existence.
This harmonizes the two camps: there is a real aspect and an imaginary aspect.
