Non-Existence: Relative Privation, Not Absolute Nothingness
Defines 'adam and its derivatives, distinguishing absolute from relative non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of 'adam (non-existence) as relative privation of expected perfections, its distinction from absolute nothingness, and its derivatives in logic and kalam terminology.

Core Idea
Non-existence is the lack of expected perfections, not mere nothingness.

In Islamic kalam and logic, the term 'adam (non-existence) has a precise meaning that is often misunderstood. It does not refer to absolute nothingness but to the absence of attributes that a thing's nature requires. This lesson examines Sanusi's definition of 'adam and its derivatives, clarifying how logicians use it for negative propositions and how 'adami applies to privative concepts and transient things.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of 'adam
Read the first sentence carefully: 'adam is defined as the lack of perfections that a thing's nature requires. Notice the phrase 'relative non-existence' which distinguishes it from absolute nothingness.
This is the core conceptual move.
2
Understand the logicians' usage
The passage then explains how logicians use 'adam for a negative relation between predicate and subject that does not imply any deficiency in the subject's essence. Example: 'Zayd is not sitting' does not mean Zayd lacks a necessary attribute.
This separates logical negation from ontological privation.
3
Explore the derivative 'adami
The term 'adami (non-existent) applies to two things: (1) terms indicating privation of a required attribute (like blindness), and (2) things that inevitably perish (like the sky, earth, wealth). Both share the idea of lacking something expected.
This extends the concept beyond mere language to ontology.
4
Contrast with common misunderstanding
Many mistakenly think 'adam means absolute nothingness. The passage explicitly says it is relative non-existence. Reflect on the difference between 'Zayd is not sitting' (simple negation) and 'Zayd is blind' (privation of a required faculty).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العدم فقدان الشيء ما تقتضيه طبيعته من الكمالات الثابتة لنوعه وطبيعته وهو عدم إضافي لا عدم مطلق ويطلق عند المنطقيين على وقوع النسبة بين محمول وموضوع ليس من شأنه أن يكون له ذلك المحمول، ولا أن يؤدي انتفاؤه عنه الى نقص في ماهيته كقولنا (ليس زيد جالساً) والعدمي هو المنسوب الى العدم، ويطلق على كل حد يدل على فقدان الشيء لاحدى الصفات التي تقتضيها طبيعته كالعمى للانسان وكل شيء مصيره الى الزوال كالسماء المظلة، والأرض، والمال والجاه والملك فهو عدمي.
Non-existence (al-ʿadam) is the thing's lack of what its nature requires of perfections established for its species and nature, and it is relative non-existence, not absolute non-existence. It is used by the logicians for the occurrence of a relation between a predicate and a subject that is not such that it should have that predicate, nor does its negation from it lead to a deficiency in its quiddity, like our saying 'Zayd is not sitting.' And al-ʿadamī (the non-existent) is that which is attributed to non-existence, and it is used for every term that indicates a thing's lack of one of the attributes that its nature requires, such as blindness for man. And everything whose end is perishing, like the overarching sky, earth, wealth, status, and dominion, is non-existent (ʿadamī).
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the term 'adam (non-existence). The author first gives a technical definition: it is a thing's lack of perfections that its nature requires (relative privation), not absolute nothingness. Then he explains the logicians' usage: 'adam is a negative relation between predicate and subject that does not imply a deficiency in the subject's essence (e.g., 'Zayd is not sitting'). Next, he defines 'adamī: that which is ascribed to non-existence, including terms denoting a lack of required attributes (like blindness) and transient things that inevitably perish (like the sky, earth, etc.). Thus, the passage distinguishes absolute non-existence (nothingness) from relative non-existence (privation of a perfection) and negative propositions from privative concepts.

Key Terms
عدم (non-existence)
عدم إضافي (relative non-existence)
عدمي (non-existent)
منطقيون (logicians)
محمول (predicate)
موضوع (subject)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many students believe that in Islamic philosophy, 'adam always means absolute nothingness (non-being).
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'adam is relative non-existence—the absence of perfections expected from a thing's nature—not absolute non-existence. It is a privation, not pure nothingness.

Checkpoint

A) Absolute nothingness.
B) The lack of all perfections.
C) A negative relation between predicate and subject that does not imply a deficiency in the quiddity.
D) A term for everything that will perish.

Key Takeaway
Non-existence ('adam) in Islamic kalam and logic is a relative concept meaning privation of perfections expected by a thing's nature, not absolute nothingness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Classification and Definition of Simple and Composite Bodies
Al-Taftazani classifies bodies and defines simple and composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani classifies bodies into simple and composite, and how he defines 'simple' by its quantitative part, excluding other types of parts.

Core Idea
A simple body is one whose quantitative part shares its name and definition.

This passage from al-Taftazani sets the stage for discussing bodies in detail. He first divides bodies into simple and composite, then further categorizes each. The key definition clarifies that 'simple' refers to a body whose quantitative part is like the whole in name and definition, which excludes parts like genus or matter. Understanding this classification is essential for later discussions on celestial, elemental, and mixed bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the four sections al-Taftazani mentions and how he defines simple and composite.
Pay attention to the term 'quantitative part' (جزء مقداري).
2
Identify the classification tree
Map out the divisions: body → composite/simple → simple further into celestial/elemental, composite into mixed/non-mixed. This tree structure underlies the discussion.
3
Understand the definition of simple
Focus on the definition: the simple body's quantitative part is like the whole in name and definition. Contrast this with composite, where the part is not like the whole.
The definition uses 'quantitative part' to exclude intellectual parts (genus, differentia) and material parts (matter, form).
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself why al-Taftazani starts with this classification. It provides a framework for investigating particular states of bodies, such as celestial spheres or elemental mixtures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني الجسم مركب وبسيط وفلكي وعنصري قال الفصل الثاني فيما يتعلق بالاجسام على التفصيل والكلام فيه مرتب على أربعة أقسام، لأن الجسم إما مؤلف من أجسام مختلفة الطبائع فمركب، وإلا فبسيط، والبسيط إما فلكي أو عنصري والمركب إما ممتزج أو لا وقد يرسم البسيط بأنه الذي يكون جزءه المقداري كالكل في الاسم، والحد، والمركب بخلافه. مثل البحث عن خصوص أحوال البسائط الفلكية أو العنصرية، أو المركبات المزاجية، أو غير المزاجية، أو حال ما هو من أقسام بعض هذه الأربعة. قال (جزئه المقداري) احترازاً عن الجزء العقلي كالجنس والفصل، أو العيني كالهيولي والصورة، فإنه لا يكون مثل الكل في الاسم والحد لا في البسيط ولا في المركب.
The second chapter: the body is composite, simple, celestial, and elemental. The second chapter said regarding bodies in detail, and the discussion is arranged in four sections, because the body is either composed of bodies of different natures — so it is composite — or else it is simple. The simple is either celestial or elemental, and the composite is either mixed or not. The simple may be defined as that whose quantitative part is like the whole in name and definition, and the composite is the opposite. For example, the investigation of the particular states of celestial or elemental simples, or of mixed or non-mixed composites, or of what belongs to some of these four. He said 'its quantitative part' as a precaution against the intellectual part, like genus and differentia, or the material part like matter and form, for it is not like the whole in name and definition, neither in the simple nor in the composite.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition. It begins by stating that the discussion of bodies is arranged in four sections. The primary division is composite (murakkab) and simple (basit). Composite bodies are composed of bodies of different natures; simple are not. Simple is further divided into celestial (falaki) and elemental (unsuri); composite is divided into mixed (mumtazij) and non-mixed. The definition of simple is given: its quantitative part is like the whole in name and definition. The author then clarifies that 'quantitative part' excludes the intellectual part (like genus and differentia) and the material part (like matter and form), because those are not equal to the whole in name and definition. The passage ends by giving examples of what can be investigated under each category.

Key Terms
جسم

مركب

Correction: The author specifies 'quantitative part' to exclude intellectual parts (like genus and differentia) and material parts (like matter and form), because those are not like the whole in name and definition.

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani classifies bodies into four main types and defines 'simple' as a body whose quantitative part shares its name and definition, distinguishing it from composite bodies.

Proving the Determiner of Directions
Provides the kalam proof for a single spherical determiner of directions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the directions of above and below necessitate a single spherical celestial sphere that determines them, and how the kalam proof establishes its necessity.

Core Idea
Directions are not self-standing; they require a physical determiner — a single spherical body encompassing all.

The author begins the discussion of celestial simple bodies by establishing that the real directions (above and below) are not arbitrary but fixed. This requires a determiner — a physical body that defines these directions. In this passage, he presents the proof that this determiner must be a single spherical body encompassing the whole cosmos.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
Directions like above and below are real and fixed; they need a physical basis. Read the opening sentence to see how the author sets up this necessity.
Notice the phrase 'لا بد من تحديدهما' — they must be determined.
2
Identify the proposed solution
The determiner is a single spherical body; nearness is defined by its periphery, distance by its center. This is the core claim.
3
Examine the arguments for unity and sphericity
For unity: if multiple bodies, either one encompasses the others or they are separate; neither works. For sphericity: non-spherical shapes would allow rectilinear motion, contradicting the role as source of direction.
These are two distinct arguments presented with 'أما الوحدة' and 'وأما الكرية'.
4
Consider the objection and conclusion
An objection suggests a single non-encompassing body could determine both directions, but the chosen view is the first one (the encompassing sphere). Reflect on why this objection is considered but not accepted.
The text says 'وفيه نظر' — there is a consideration, then 'فلذا كان المختار هو الوجه الأول'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول إثبات المحدد قال (القسم الأول في البسائط الفلكية) وفيه مباحث : المبحث الأول: في إثبات المحدد قد سبق أن من الجهات ما هو حقيقي بتوجه إليه بعض الأجسام بالطبع وهو العلو والسفل، فلا بد من تحديدهما بجسم واحد كري محيط بالكل، يتحدد بمحيطه القرب، وبمركزه البعد، أما الوحدة فلأنه لو تعدد فمع مخالطة البعض بالبعض يتعين المحيط للتحديد، وبدونها كان كل في جهة من الآخر، فلا تكون الجهة به قبله أو معه على أن المتحدد بكل منهما يكون هو القرب منه لا البعد، وأما الكرية فلامتناع تركيبه، أو زواله عن الاستدارة لاقتضائهما جواز الحركة المستقيمة التي لا تكون إلا من جهة إلى جهة، فيتنافى كون الجهة به . جعل أول المباحث في ثبات فلك محيط بجميع ما سواه من الأجسام يسمى محدد الجهات وتقرير البرهان أنه قد سبق أن الجهات موجودات ذوات أوضاع ، وأنها حدود ونهايات للامتدادات وأن العلو والسفل منها جهتان متعينتان لا تتبدلان وهذا يستلزم وجود محدد به يتعين ،وضعهما، ويلزم أن يكون جسماً واحداً كرياً محيطاً بالكل ليتعين العلو بأقرب حد من محيطه، والسفل بأبعد حد منه وهو المركز. أما الجسمية فلوجوب كونه ذا وضع ، وأما الوحدة فلأنه لو تعدد بأن يكون جسمين مثلاً. فإما أن يحيط أحدهما بالآخر أو لا. فإن أحاط كان المحدد، إذ إليه الانتهاء دون المحاط، وإن لم يحط كان كل منهما في جهة من الآخر، فيكون متأخراً عن الجهة أو مقارناً لها سابقاً عليها، ليصلح محدداً لها، وأيضاً كل منهما . إنما يحدد جهة القرب منه دون البعد فإنه غير متحدد. والمطلوب إثبات ما يحدد الجهتين المتقابلتين معاً وفيه نظر لجواز أن يكون الجسماني بحيث يكون غاية القرب من كل منهما غاية البعد من الآخر، فيتحدد بهما الجهتان، فلذا كان المختار هو الوجه الأول.
The first investigation: proving the determiner. He said: (The first section on celestial simple bodies) and it contains investigations: The first investigation: on proving the determiner. It has already preceded that among the directions are those that are real towards which some bodies move naturally, and they are above and below. So they must be determined by a single spherical body encompassing the whole, by whose periphery nearness is determined, and by whose center distance is determined. As for unity, because if it were multiple, then with intermingling of some with others the encompassing one is determined for determination, and without it each would be in a direction from the other, so direction would not be before it or with it; moreover that which is determined by each of them would be nearness from it, not distance. As for sphericity, because its composition [would be impossible] or its deviation from circularity [would be impossible] because they would necessitate the possibility of rectilinear motion, which only occurs from one direction to another, so direction would be incompatible with it. He made the first investigation on establishing a sphere encompassing all other bodies called the determiner of directions. And the exposition of the proof: it has already preceded that directions are existents possessing positions, and that they are boundaries and endpoints of extensions, and that above and below are two determined directions that do not change. This necessitates the existence of a determiner by which their positions are determined, and it necessitates that it be a single spherical body encompassing the whole so that above is determined by the nearest limit from its periphery, and below by the farthest limit from it, which is the center. As for being a body, because it must possess position. As for unity, because if it were multiple, as in being two bodies, then either one encompasses the other or not. If it encompasses, then it is the determiner, since the end is to it not to the encompassed; if it does not encompass, then each is in a direction from the other, and thus would be posterior to direction or simultaneous with it or prior to it, to be suitable as a determiner for it. Also, each one only determines the direction of nearness from it, not the distance, for it is not determined. And what is required is to prove what determines both opposite directions together. And there is an objection: it is possible that a body is such that the closest distance from each of them is the farthest distance from the other, so both directions are determined by them. Therefore the chosen view is the first one.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage presents the first investigation (al-mabḥath al-awwal) on proving the existence of the determiner (al-muḥaddid) of directions, i.e., the celestial sphere that encompasses all bodies. The author begins by recalling that among directions (jihāt), some are real (ḥaqīqī), such as above (ʿuluww) and below (sufl), towards which certain bodies move naturally. These two directions require a determiner to fix their positions. The determiner must be a single spherical body (jism wāḥid kuriy) encompassing the whole universe, because nearness is determined by its periphery and distance by its center. The argument for unity: if there were multiple bodies, they would either intermingle, in which case the one that encompasses the others is the determiner, or they would be separate, in which case each would lie in a direction relative to the other, making direction prior to the body and thus unsuitable as a determiner. Additionally, a determiner must define both nearness and distance, but separate bodies would each only define nearness from itself. The argument for sphericity: composition or deviation from circularity would allow rectilinear motion, which requires a direction before the motion, contradicting the role of the sphere as the source of direction. The author then restates the proof more formally: directions are existents with positions (awḍāʿ) and are boundaries of extensions; above and below are fixed, so they require a determiner that defines them. This determiner must be a body (because it has position), one (as argued), and spherical (as argued). An objection is considered: could a single body have a shape such that the closest point from one direction is the farthest from the opposite direction, thus determining both? The author notes this possibility but states that the chosen view remains the first argument (al-wajh al-awwal), i.e., the necessity of a spherical encompassing body.

الجهات
العلو
السفل
الجسم الواحد الكري
المحيط
الحركة المستقيمة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The determiner (muḥaddid) is often mistaken to refer to God.
Correction: The text explicitly states the determiner is a physical body (jism) — the celestial sphere — not the Creator.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must the determiner be a single body (not multiple)?
A) Because multiple bodies would require an infinite regress.
B) Because if there were two bodies, they would either be intermingled or separate, and neither would properly determine both directions.
C) Because the determiner must be simple.
D) Because direction is prior to the body.

Key Takeaway
The directions of above and below are determined by a single spherical body (the celestial sphere) that encompasses all; without it, direction would be undefined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the universe requires a single spherical body to give meaning to 'above' and 'below', what does that say about your need for a single, absolute reference point for your moral and spiritual life? Where have you been acting as if you can define 'up' and 'down' on your own terms?
?
The objector thought a non-encompassing body could determine both directions, but the author dismissed it. Are there areas in your life where you try to find direction from partial sources (people, trends, achievements) that do not encompass the whole of your existence? What would it mean to submit to an all-encompassing determiner?
?
The proof insists that direction cannot be defined without a concrete, encompassing reality. When you face moral confusion, do you retreat to relative standards, or do you anchor your decisions in a fixed, absolute reference? How does your daily prayer—facing a single qibla—train you to recognize that ultimate orientation?

The Argument for Sphericity from Simplicity and Its Refutation
Presents a cosmological argument and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the determiner must be spherical because of its simplicity, and why the objection shows this argument is unsound.

Core Idea
The sphericity argument confuses direction priority for parts with priority for the whole.

This passage presents a physical-theological argument for the sphericity of the determiner (muḥaddid) based on its simplicity. After stating the argument, an objection is raised that reveals a logical flaw. By studying this passage, you will see how careful reasoning can dismantle a seemingly intuitive proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow from the argument to the objection.
The passage is dense; take your time with the translation.
2
Identify the argument's structure
Break down the argument: premise (determiner is simple), conclusion (must be spherical), and the reasoning linking them. Note the hypothetical scenarios (composite or deviating simple).
3
Understand the objection
The objection shows that the argument only proves direction is prior to the motion of parts, not prior to the whole determiner. This undercuts the necessity of sphericity.
This is the key refutation.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes the argument is unsound. Ask yourself why simplicity alone does not force sphericity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما وجوب كونه كرياً فلأنه بسيط يمتنع زواله عن مقتضى طبعه أعني الاستدارة، إذ لو كان مركباً أو بسيطاً زال عن استدارته لزم جواز الحركة المستقيمة على أجزائه وهو محال ضرورة أنها لا تكون إلا من جهة إلى جهة فتكون الجهة قبله أو معه فلا تكون متحددة به، وجه اللزوم، أما في البسيط الزائل عن الاستدارة فظاهر، وأما في المركب فلأن تألفه لا يتصور إلا بحركة بعض الأجزاء إلى البعض، ولأن من لوازمه جواز الانحلال، لأن كل واحد من بسائطه يلاقي بأحد طرفيه شيئاً غير ما يلاقيه بالطرف الآخر مع تساويهما في الحقيقة، فيجوز أن يلاقي ذلك الشيء بالطرف الآخر، وذلك بالحركة من جهة إلى جهة، وفي هذا نظر لأنه إنما يستدعي تقدم الجهة على حركة الأجزاء لا على نفس المركب، وبهذا يظهر أن الاستدلال بهذا الوجه على بساطة المحدد ليس بنام .
And as for the necessity of it being spherical, because it is simple, it is impossible for it to deviate from what its nature requires, i.e., circularity. For if it were composite or simple deviating from its circularity, then rectilinear motion would be possible on its parts, which is impossible necessarily because it only occurs from one direction to another, so direction would be before it or with it, and thus it would not be determined by it. The implication: as for the simple deviating from circularity it is obvious; as for the composite, because its composition cannot be imagined except by motion of some parts toward others, and because among its necessary consequences is the possibility of dissolution, because each of its simple parts meets at one of its ends something other than what it meets at the other end, while they are equal in reality, so it is possible that it meets that thing at the other end, and that is by motion from one direction to another. And there is an objection to this: it only necessitates the priority of direction over the motion of the parts, not over the composite itself. And by this it appears that the inference by this argument for the simplicity of the determiner is not sound.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents a further argument for the sphericity of the determiner (muḥaddid) based on its simplicity (basāṭa). The argument begins: the determiner must be simple (basīṭ) because it is impossible for it to deviate from its natural shape, which is circularity (istidāra). If it were composite (murakkab) or simple but non-circular, then rectilinear motion (ḥaraka mustaqīma) would be possible on its parts. Rectilinear motion is impossible for the determiner because such motion is always from one direction to another, implying that direction is prior to or simultaneous with the object, which contradicts the determiner's function as the source of direction. The author explains the implication (wajh al-luzūm): for a simple body deviating from circularity, the case is obvious; for a composite, composition requires motion of parts toward each other, and dissolution (inḥilāl) is possible because each simple part meets at one end something different from the other end, allowing motion from one direction to another. However, an objection (naẓar) is raised: this argument only shows that direction is prior to the motion of the parts, not that it is prior to the composite itself. Therefore, the author concludes that this argument for the simplicity (and by extension sphericity) of the determiner is not sound (laysa bi-nām).

Key Terms
بسيط
استدارة
حركة مستقيمة
مركب
انحلال
بساطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the argument from simplicity conclusively proves sphericity because a simple thing cannot deviate from its nature.
Correction: The objection shows that the argument conflates direction priority for parts with priority for the whole; thus simplicity does not force sphericity.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the argument for sphericity from simplicity is considered unsound?
A) The determiner is not actually simple.
B) The argument only shows direction is prior to the motion of parts, not to the whole determiner.
C) The argument never mentions circularity.
D) The argument was made by a unreliable authority.

Key Takeaway
The argument that the determiner must be spherical because it is simple fails because it mistakenly assumes that direction must be prior to the whole body rather than just its parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung to a belief about yourself or others because it seemed logically airtight, only to discover that you had mistaken a part for the whole? Will you honestly examine that belief today, even if it means letting it go?
?
The objection in this lesson shows that even sophisticated reasoning can be flawed when it confuses parts with the whole. When was the last time you admitted that your own reasoning was unsound? Are you willing to do so now, even if it is uncomfortable?
?
The determiner is beyond full human comprehension; our arguments are limited and fallible. How does this humility change the way you approach your understanding of Allah's attributes? Will you stop trusting your own intellect alone and submit to revelation?

The Shape of the Determiner of Direction: Refuting the Objection
Author refutes an objection about the shape of the determiner of direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the determiner of direction can be oval or lenticular and must encompass all directions.

Core Idea
The determiner need not be spherical; it must be encompassing.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection that the determiner's convex and concave must both be circular. He refutes this by showing that the shape can be oval or lenticular, and the essential condition is that the determiner encompasses the possessors of directions. This clarifies a key point in kalam cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objection and the author's immediate response.
The author says the objection is self-evident.
2
Understand the shapes
The author defines oval (baydi) and lenticular (adasi) shapes. Both are surfaces bounded by arcs, and when rotated produce solids.
Oval has arcs smaller than a circle; lenticular larger.
3
Encompassing condition
The argument concludes that the determiner must be encompassing (muhit) because a non-encompassing object can only determine proximity, not both directions.
This is the key point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : طبيعة المحدد واحدة لما سيجيء، فيكون محدبه مستديرا كمقعره . قلنا : فيكون ذلك استدلالاً برأسه لا يفتقر إلى ذكر الحركة، ولزوم الخلاء، والشكل البيضي سطح يحيط به قوسان متساویتان، كل منهما أصغر من دائرة. والعدسي ما هما أعظم. وكل منهما إذا أدير على نفسه حصل مجسمه، وأما كون المحدد محيطاً بذوات الجهات، فلأن غير المحيط إنما يتحدد به القرب منه وهو ظاهر، فلا يكون محدداً للجهتين. هذا خلف.
If it is said: The nature of the determiner is one for what will come, so its convex would be circular like its concave. We say: That would be a self-evident argument, not requiring mention of motion or the impossibility of void. The oval shape is a surface bounded by two equal arcs, each smaller than a circle. The lenticular shape is one where they are larger. Each of them, when rotated around itself, yields its solid. As for the determiner encompassing the possessors of directions, because the non-encompassing is only determined by proximity to it, and that is obvious, so it would not be a determiner for both directions. This is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the shape of the determiner (muhaddad) of direction. The objector argues that since the determiner's nature is one, its convex and concave must both be circular (spherical). The author responds that this is a self-evident argument that does not rely on motion or the impossibility of void, then explains that the determiner can have an oval (baydi) or lenticular (adasi) shape. However, the key point is that the determiner must be encompassing (muhit) of the possessors of directions (dhawat al-jahat), because a non-encompassing object can only determine proximity, not both directions simultaneously. Thus the objection fails, and the conclusion is that the determiner must be encompassing.

Key Terms
محدد (muhaddad)
محيط (muhit)
جهة (jiha)
شكل بيضي (shakl baydi)
شكل عدسي (shakl adasi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the determiner must be spherical.
Correction: The passage shows it can be oval or lenticular as long as it encompasses all directions.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the shape of the determiner of direction in response to the objection that its convex and concave must both be circular?
A) It must be spherical.
B) It can be oval or lenticular but must be encompassing.
C) It is always lenticular.
D) It is always oval.

Key Takeaway
The determiner of direction must be encompassing; its shape can be oval or lenticular, not necessarily spherical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the determiner must encompass all directions, what does that imply about your own life? Are you living as though you are truly encompassed by the One who holds all directions?
?
The objector assumed a spherical shape due to the uniformity of nature. Where in your faith do you impose human assumptions on divine realities? Will you repent from that and submit to what the revelation actually says?
?
The author did not rely on the impossibility of void to refute the objection. Are you willing to set aside your own proofs and simply trust that the determiner is encompassing? What would change in your heart if you truly believed nothing is outside His encompassing?

The Determiner of Direction
Definition and proof of the determiner of direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the determiner of direction is and why it is the outermost sphere, not the earth.

Core Idea
Direction is fixed by the encompassing sphere, not the earth.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid tackles a key question in natural theology: what fixes directions like above and below? The author defines the 'determiner of direction' and then proves, through a careful argument, that it is the convex of the outermost sphere, not the earth. This has implications for understanding the physical universe as a sign of the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once slowly, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the definitions and the logical flow.
The passage is dense; read it at least twice.
2
Understand the definition of determiner
Identify what is meant by 'determiner of direction' (muhaddad al-jiha). Note that it is that by which the position (wad') of a direction is fixed.
3
Follow the argument against the earth
The author argues that the earth does not determine direction because it is the sphere that determines its center, not the other way around. Trace this reasoning step by step.
Contrast the roles of agent (fa'il) and recipient (qabil).
4
Examine the objection and answer
Read the objection about agent and recipient. See how the author clarifies that the determiner must have position, and that the earth's role is only as center of the sphere.
This confirms the sphere as the true determiner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المراد بمحدد الجهة ما يتعين به وضع الجهة قال ثم معنى تحديده) الجهات تعيين أوضاعها به وإلا فالفاعل لا يلزم أن يكون جسماً، والقابل ليس إلا واحداً، لأن العلو نقطة من الفلك، والسفل من الأرض، لكن من حيث أنها مركز للمحيط، ومتحددة به المحيط بتعين مركزه ولا عكس. ولهذا لم يكن للأرض دخل في التحديد، وإنما تعين المحيط بالكل، لأن المحاط قد يمتد الإشارة منه، فلا يكون هو المنتهى، وعلى هذا يكون المحدد بالحقيقة، هو محدب المحيط، ويكون مقعره تحت كما في سائر الأفلاك بحسب الأجزاء المفروضة، وبعضهم على أنه نفس المحيط حتى يكون كله فوق لذاته . جواب سؤال تقريره أن المراد بمجرد الجهة إن كان فاعلها، فلا نسلم لزوم كونه ذا وضع فضلاً عن الإحاطة، وإن كان قابلها، فمحدد العلـو والسفـل لا يكون واجداً ضرورة أن المركز لا يقوم بالمحدد وتقرير الجواب : أن المراد به ما يتعين به وضع الجهة، وظاهر أن تعين الوضع لا يكون إلا بذي الوضع، وتعين السفل بوسط الأرض ليس من حيث أنه نقطة من الأرض ليكون للأرض دخل في التحديد، فيتعدد المحدد ، بل من حيث أنه مركز لمحيط فلك الأفلاك، ومتحدد به ضرورة أن المحيط يتعين مركزه والمركز لا يتعين محيطه لجواز أن يحيط به دوائر غير متناهية، فبهذا الاعتبار كان المحدد للجهات هو الفلك دون الأرض ودون كليهما .
What is meant by the determiner of direction (muhaddad al-jiha) is that by which the position (wad') of the direction is determined. He said: then the meaning of its determination of the directions is the determination of their positions by it, otherwise the agent (fa'il) does not necessarily have to be a body, and the recipient (qabil) is only one, because 'above' is a point of the sphere, and 'below' is a point of the earth, but from the perspective that it is a center for the encompassing (muhit), and determined by it: the encompassing determines its center, not vice versa. For this reason the earth has no role in the determination; rather the encompassing is determined by the whole, because the encompassed (muhat) may extend indication from it, so it is not the final end. On this basis, the determiner in reality is the convex of the encompassing, and its concave is below, as in other spheres according to the assumed parts. Some hold that it is the encompassing itself so that it is all above in itself. An answer to a question whose formulation is: If by direction is meant its agent, we do not concede that it must have position, let alone encompassing; if by it is meant its recipient, then the determiner of above and below is not existent necessarily because the center does not subsist in the determiner. The answer's formulation: What is meant is that by which the position of the direction is determined, and it is obvious that the determination of position is only by something that has position. The determination of below by the center of the earth is not insofar as it is a point of the earth for the earth to have a role in the determination, for then the determiner would be multiple; rather insofar as it is a center for the encompassing sphere of spheres, and determined by it, necessarily because the encompassing determines its center, and the center does not determine its encompassing because it is possible for infinite circles to surround it. So in this regard, the determiner of directions is the sphere, not the earth, nor both together.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof concerning the determiner (muhaddad) of direction. It begins by defining the determiner as that by which the position (wad') of a direction is fixed. The author explains that direction (jiha) is not determined by the agent (e.g., God) because the agent need not be a body. The recipient (e.g., a body) is only one, but the determination of above and below requires a determiner. The author argues that above is a point on the sphere and below is a point on the earth, but only insofar as the earth's center relates to the encompassing sphere. The sphere determines its center, not the reverse, so the earth has no role in determining direction. The actual determiner is the convex of the outermost sphere; its concave is below. Some even say the sphere itself is the determiner. Then an objection is raised: if by 'direction' we mean the agent, it need not have position; if we mean the recipient, the determiner of above and below would not be necessary. The answer clarifies that the determiner is what fixes the position of a direction, and that requires something with position. The determination of below by the earth's center is not because it is a point of the earth, but because it is the center of the encompassing sphere (falak al-aflak). The sphere determines its center, but the center does not determine the sphere, because infinite circles could surround it. Thus the determiner of directions is the sphere alone, not the earth nor both.

Key Terms
محدد الجهة (muhaddad al-jiha)
وضع (wad')
فاعل (fa'il)
قابل (qabil)
مركز (markaz)
محيط (muhit)
فلك الأفلاك (falak al-aflak)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The earth determines directions because it is at the center.
Correction: The earth is only the center of the encompassing sphere, and the sphere determines its center, not the other way around.

Checkpoint
Why does the earth not have a role in determining directions?
A) Because the earth is spherical.
B) Because the earth is not a body.
C) Because the sphere determines its center, not vice versa.
D) Because the earth is at the center of all spheres.

Key Takeaway
The determiner of direction is the convex of the outermost sphere, because only it fixes the positions of above and below.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Do you rely on physical directions to orient your worship, or do you recognize that the true determiner of all direction is beyond the physical? How does that change your focus in prayer?
?
If the sphere determines direction, not the earth, then your sense of 'above' is relative to a cosmic structure. Does that humble you? How does it affect your understanding of God's transcendence?
?
The passage argues that the agent (God) does not need to have position. Are you comfortable with a God who is not bound by any direction? Or do you secretly wish He were 'above' in a literal sense? What does this lesson teach you about tawhid?

Could a Lower Sphere Determine Direction? The Answer
Author refutes the objection that a lower sphere could determine direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the determiner of direction must be the outermost sphere, not a lower one like the moon's sphere, and how this relates to the concepts of place (makan) and spatial volume (hayyiz).

Core Idea
Direction is the ultimate endpoint; only the outermost sphere qualifies.

In this passage, the author addresses a subtle objection: could a lower sphere, such as the moon's sphere, serve as the determiner of a direction like 'up' for fire? The objector argues that just as a place is defined by its immediate container, fire's natural motion toward the moon's concave suggests that sphere is its directional determiner. The author responds by distinguishing between place and direction, showing that direction must be the final boundary beyond which no further indication is possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector accepts the need for a single encompassing determiner but questions why it must be the outermost sphere. Notice the analogy to places, where the immediate container defines the place.
The objector uses the example of fire seeking the concave of the moon's sphere.
2
Understand the author's key distinction
The author argues that an encompassing thing that is itself encompassed cannot be the endpoint of indication. Direction is the extreme of all extensions, so only the ultimate container works.
This is unlike place, which is just the immediate tangent surface.
3
Grasp the difference between natural place and direction
Fire seeks the concave of the moon's sphere as its natural place (makan), not its ultimate direction (jiha). If fire could break through that sphere, it would continue moving upward, proving the true direction is beyond.
The author illustrates with the thought experiment of fire cutting through the moon's sphere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : سلمنا أن المحدد يكون واحداً محيطاً بذي الجهة، لكن من أين يلزم أن يكون هو المحيط بالكل، ولم لا يجوز أن يكون محدد جهة النار هو فلك القمر مثلاً. كما هو حكم الأمكنة، فإن محدد كل مكان إنما هو المحيط به، وإن كان محاطاً للغير، بل اطباقهم على كون النار خفيفة على الإطلاق بمعنى أنها تطلب جهة الفوق، مع أنها لا تطلب إلا مقعر فلك القمر، ربما يدل على أنه محدد جهتها . قلنا : المحيط إذا كان محاطاً للغير لم يكن منتهى الإشارة ضرورة امتدادها إلى الغير، فلم يكن محدداً للجهة التي هي طرف الامتدادات، ومنتهى الإشارات، وهذا بخلاف المكان، فإنه سطح المحيط المماس لسطح ذي المكان، فطلب النار بالطبع مقعر فلك القمر إنما يدل على أنه مكانه الطبيعي لا جهتها. فإن العنصر إنما يطلب بالطبع حيزه لاجهته بأن يصل إلى الجسم المشتمل على حقيقة الجهة، بل لا يكون ذلك إلا في الماء الطالب للأرض . ألا ترى أن النار لو فرضت قاطعة لفلك القمر كانت متحركة إلى فوق لا من فوق ولهذا اتفقوا على أن فوق النار فلك كذا
If it is said: We grant that the determiner is one and encompassing the possessor of direction, but why must it be the one encompassing the whole? And why is it not permissible that the determiner of the direction of fire be, for example, the sphere of the moon, just as is the rule for places, for the determiner of every place is only what encompasses it, even if it is encompassed by another. Moreover, their consensus that fire is absolutely light, meaning it seeks the upward direction, while it only seeks the concave of the moon's sphere, perhaps indicates that it is the determiner of its direction. We say: An encompassing thing, if it is encompassed by another, is not the endpoint of indication, necessarily because indication extends to the other, so it is not a determiner for the direction which is the extreme of extensions and the endpoint of indications. This is unlike place, for it is the surface of the encompassing tangent to the surface of the thing having place. So fire's natural seeking of the concave of the moon's sphere only indicates that it is its natural place, not its direction. For an element naturally seeks its spatial volume (hayyiz), not its direction, by reaching the body that contains the reality of the direction. Indeed, that only occurs in water seeking the earth. Do you not see that if fire were assumed to cut through the moon's sphere, it would move upward, not from above? And for this reason they agreed that above fire is such-and-such sphere.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector concedes that the determiner must be one and encompassing, but questions why it must be the outermost sphere. Why could a lower sphere, like the moon's sphere, determine the direction of fire? The objector argues that just as a place is determined by its immediate container, fire's natural motion toward the moon's concave suggests that sphere is its direction-determiner. The author responds that if the encompassing thing is itself encompassed by another, it is not the final endpoint of indication, because one can point beyond it. Direction is the extreme of all extensions, so only the outermost sphere qualifies. The author distinguishes between natural place (makan) and direction (jiha). Fire's motion to the moon's concave is for its natural place, not its ultimate direction. If fire could pass the moon's sphere, it would continue upward, proving that the ultimate direction is beyond. Thus the determiner of all directions is the outermost sphere.

Key Terms
جهة النار (jiha al-nar)
فلك القمر (falak al-qamar)
مكان (makan)
حيز (hayyiz)
منتهى الإشارة (muntaha al-ishara)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the immediate container of an object defines its direction, just as it defines its place.
Correction: Direction is defined by the ultimate boundary of possible indication, not the immediate container. The moon's sphere is not the final endpoint because one can point beyond it.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the moon's sphere as the determiner of fire's direction?
A) Because fire is light and seeks the moon's concave.
B) Because the moon's sphere is itself encompassed and not the endpoint of indication.
C) Because the moon is not a sphere.
D) Because fire does not move upward.

Key Takeaway
Direction is the utmost endpoint of indication; only the outermost sphere can serve as its determiner, not a lower sphere like that of the moon.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What 'moon spheres' in your life—immediate goals or comforts—do you mistake for your ultimate direction? How can you test whether you would continue moving beyond them if they were removed?
?
The author distinguishes between seeking a place (hayyiz) and seeking a direction (jiha). In your spiritual journey, are you content finding a 'natural place' (routine, status) instead of pressing toward the ultimate direction of seeking Allah's pleasure? What would it take to break through that sphere?
?
Fire, if it could break through the moon's sphere, would continue upward. Is there a sphere of limited vision in your life that you assume is the boundary, but which actually is not the ultimate end? How can you push beyond it?

Celestial Equator, Ecliptic, Poles, and Orbits
Defines the ecliptic, equator, poles, and orbits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of the celestial equator, ecliptic, poles, and daily motion circles as used in kalam cosmology.

Core Idea
The celestial sphere's geometry is defined by the ecliptic, equator, and poles.

This passage establishes the foundational terms for describing celestial motions in kalam cosmology. Before we can discuss the sun's path and the seasons, we need a clear definition of the ecliptic, the equinoctial circle, and the poles. The author provides these definitions in a concise manner.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage uses a quotation style for the definition of the equinoctial circle.
2
Identify each defined term
The passage defines five key terms: مدارات (orbits), معدل النهار (equinoctial circle), منطقة (ecliptic), قطبا المنطقة (poles of the ecliptic), and قطبا العالم (poles of the world). Find each definition.
3
Understand the relationship between ecliptic and equator
Note that the ecliptic is the great circle from the sphere's rotation, while the equinoctial circle is where day and night are equal when the sun is on it. They are different circles.
The common misunderstanding is confusing these two.
4
Note the poles' definitions
The poles of the ecliptic are fixed points during rotation, while the poles of the world are the northern and southern poles. Identify which is which.
5
Connect to the concept of orbits
The small circles parallel to the ecliptic are called orbits (مدارات). These represent the daily motion paths of celestial bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدوائر الموازية للمنطقة تسمى مدارات تلك الحركة. قال: (يسمى معدل النهار) وقطبان يسميان قطبي العالم. لتعادل الليل والنهار في جميع البقاع عند كون الشمس عليها. والمراد بالمنطقة أعظم الدوائر الحادثة من حركة الكرة على نفسها، وبقطبيها النقطتان الثابتتان عند حركة الكرة، والدوائر الصغار الموازية للمنطقة تسمى مدارات تلك الحركة، وأحد قطبي العالم وهو الذي يلي شمال المواجه للمشرق ويسمى الشمالي والآخر الجنوبي.
The circles parallel to the ecliptic are called the orbits of that motion. He said: (It is called the equinoctial circle) and two poles that are called the poles of the world. Because the night and day are equal in all regions when the sun is on it. And by 'ecliptic' is meant the greatest circle generated from the sphere's rotation on itself, and by its two poles the two fixed points during the sphere's rotation, and the small circles parallel to the ecliptic are called the orbits of that motion, and one of the two poles of the world, which is the one facing the north of the one facing the east, is called the northern, and the other the southern.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage defines key celestial circles and poles. It states that circles parallel to the ecliptic (المنطقة) are called مدارات (orbits). Then a quotation: 'It is called the equinoctial circle (معدل النهار) and two poles are called the poles of the world' – because day and night are equal everywhere when the sun is on it. The commentary explains: the ecliptic is the largest circle produced by the sphere's rotation on itself; its two poles are the fixed points during rotation. The smaller circles parallel to the ecliptic are the orbits of that motion. One pole of the world is the northern (facing the north of the east-facing direction) and the other is the southern. This provides the basic geometric framework for describing celestial motions in kalam.

Key Terms
منطقة
معدل النهار
قطب العالم
مدارات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the ecliptic and the equator are the same circle.
Correction: The passage distinguishes them: the ecliptic is the greatest circle from the sphere's rotation, while the equinoctial circle is where day and night are equal; they are different circles in the sky.

Checkpoint
What is the 'ecliptic' (المنطقة) defined as in this passage?
A) The circle of equal day and night.
B) The greatest circle generated from the sphere's rotation on itself.
C) The small circles parallel to the equator.
D) The line connecting the northern and southern poles.

Key Takeaway
The ecliptic is the largest circle from the sphere's rotation, the equator is where day equals night, and the poles are fixed points; small circles parallel to the ecliptic are orbits.

The Daily Rotation and the Sun's Motion
Explains why the daily rotation is stated as 'approximately' a day and night.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the celestial sphere's daily rotation is described as 'approximately' a day and a night, and the role of the sun's proper motion in this distinction.

Core Idea
The solar day is longer than the sphere's rotation due to the sun's eastward motion.

This lesson unpacks a subtle point in classical cosmology: the difference between a full rotation of the celestial sphere and a solar day. The author of the matn uses the word 'approximately,' and the commentary explains the astronomical reason. By understanding this, you'll see how careful the classical scholars were in their descriptions of natural phenomena.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Note the key term 'approximately' (قريب).
The passage is short but contains an important distinction.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on دورة (rotation), اليوم (day), and الحركة الخاصة (proper motion). Understand each term's role.
The sun's 'proper motion' is its annual eastward movement along the ecliptic.
3
Understand the reason for 'approximately'
The commentary explains that the rotation is slightly less than a full day-night cycle because of the sun's own motion. This is the difference between sidereal and solar days.
The sidereal day is about 23h56m, while the solar day is 24h.
4
Reflect on the precision
Consider why the author used 'approximately' instead of an exact number. This shows awareness of astronomical nuances.
Classical scholars were precise even in summary texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: (ويتم دورة في قريب من اليوم بليلة). إنما قال في قريب لأنها تنقص من اليوم بليلته بمقدار الحركة الخاصة للشمس من المغرب إلى المشرق.
He said: (And it completes a rotation in approximately a day and a night). He only said 'approximately' because it falls short of a day and its night by the amount of the sun's own motion from west to east.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage explains a statement about the daily rotation of the celestial sphere. The original text says the sphere completes one rotation in approximately a day and a night. The commentator notes that 'approximately' is used because the actual rotation period is slightly less than a full solar day. The reason is the sun's own proper motion from west to east (which is the annual motion along the ecliptic). This eastward motion of the sun means that after one full rotation of the sphere, the sun appears slightly behind its previous position, requiring a small additional rotation to align again, thus making the solar day longer than the sidereal day by about 4 minutes.

Key Terms
دورة
اليوم
الحركة الخاصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the daily rotation is exactly 24 hours.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it is slightly less due to the sun's motion; the solar day is longer than the rotation period.

Checkpoint
Why did the author say 'approximately' for the daily rotation period?
A) Because the sphere rotates irregularly.
B) Because it is shorter than a day by the sun's own motion.
C) Because it varies with the seasons.
D) Because the night is sometimes longer.

Key Takeaway
The daily rotation of the celestial sphere is not exactly a solar day; it is slightly less due to the sun's eastward motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The sun's motion from west to east is opposite to what we see daily. This teaches that our immediate perceptions can mislead us. In what area of your faith or life might you be relying on appearances rather than the deeper reality that Allah has revealed? What will you do this week to seek that deeper truth?
?
The scholar used the word 'approximately' with precision. How does this reflect the Islamic tradition of careful observation and humility in knowledge? Is there an area of your deen where you are content with vague understanding, when you could seek more precise knowledge? Commit to one concrete step to increase your precision.

The Ninth Sphere's Motion of Lower Spheres
Explains the motion of the ninth sphere with a condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the ninth sphere is said to move all lower spheres and the condition under which an enclosed sphere necessarily moves with its enclosure.

Core Idea
The outer sphere moves the inner ones only if they are in its thickness.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid examines the claim that the ninth sphere moves the eight lower spheres. The author explains that although observation suggests the lower spheres move together with the ninth, the necessary physical relationship only holds under a specific condition. Let's read the text to grasp this nuanced argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the structure: a claim, a reason, and a conditional exception.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the word 'إلا' which signals the exception.
2
Identify the main claim and reason
The claim is that the ninth sphere moves all eight lower spheres. The reason given is that they are 'like a part of it' because it encompasses them.
The commentary elaborates: observation shows this, but it's not a necessary law.
3
Understand the conditional exception
The passage states that in rotational motion (حركة وضعية), the enclosed is not necessarily moved by the enclosing sphere unless the enclosed lies within the thickness (ثخن) of the enclosure.
This is the key nuance: only when the enclosed is embedded like an eccentric sphere in a homocentric sphere does it move necessarily.
4
Connect to the astronomical model
Relate this to the Ptolemaic model: the ninth sphere is the outermost, and the lower spheres are nested within it. The condition about thickness refers to how the lower spheres are embedded.
The example of the eccentric sphere within the homocentric sphere illustrates when the enclosed is literally a part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول بأن الفلك التاسع يحرك الأفلاك الثمانية. قال: (ويحرك الكل) لأنها كالجزء منه. يعني أن التاسع يحرك جميع الأفلاك الثمانية التي تحته بحكم المشاهدة لكونها بمنزلة جزء منه حيث أحاط بها، وقوي عليها حتى صار المجموع بمنزلة واحدة، وإلا ففي الحركة الوضعية تحرك المحاط بتحرك المحيط ليس بلازم إلا إذا كان المحاط في ثخن المحيط كالخارج المركز من الممثل على ماسيجيء إن شاء الله تعالى فإنه جزء منه على الحقيقة.
The statement that the ninth sphere moves the eight spheres. He said: (And it moves all) because they are like a part of it. Meaning that the ninth moves all the eight spheres beneath it by observation, because they are like a part of it since it encompasses them and has strength over them until the whole becomes like one thing; otherwise, in local motion, the enclosed being moved by the moving enclosure is not necessary unless the enclosed is in the thickness of the enclosure, like the eccentric sphere in the homocentric, as will come if God wills, for then it is a part of it in reality.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage addresses the claim that the ninth sphere (the outermost sphere) moves all eight lower spheres. The quoted statement says it moves all because they are like a part of it. The commentary explains: observation shows that the ninth sphere moves the eight beneath it because it surrounds them and has power over them, making the whole act as one unit. However, the commentator notes a nuance: in rotational motion, the enclosed object is not necessarily moved by the motion of the enclosing sphere unless the enclosed is embedded within the thickness of the enclosure, like an eccentric sphere within a homocentric sphere – in that case it is literally a part. Otherwise, the ninth sphere's motion is not a necessary physical cause but a direct divine act or result of its encompassing relation.

Key Terms
الفلك التاسع
الحركة الوضعية
المحاط
المحيط
ثخن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the ninth sphere mechanically causes motion in lower spheres as a necessary physical law.
Correction: The passage clarifies it is not a necessary law – it depends on whether the enclosed is within the thickness of the enclosure. If not, the motion is not necessitated.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, when is the enclosed sphere necessarily moved by the enclosing sphere?
A) Whenever the enclosing sphere rotates.
B) Only when the enclosed is within the thickness of the enclosure.
C) Only when the enclosed is smaller in size.
D) Never, because motion is always direct from God.

Key Takeaway
The ninth sphere's motion of lower spheres is not automatic; it is necessary only when the lower spheres lie within its thickness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there aspects of your worship where you rely on 'being encompassed' by Allah's mercy without actively embedding yourself in His obedience? What would it mean for you to be truly 'within His thickness'?
?
The ninth sphere moves the lower spheres because they are like a part of it. In your life, are you merely 'like a part' of the Muslim ummah, or are you genuinely united with it in purpose and action? What one change would make you a real part?
?
The passage warns against assuming automatic causation. Where have you assumed that good deeds automatically bring reward without the condition of sincerity? How will you ensure your actions are truly embedded in the thickness of ikhlas?

The Sphere of Fixed Stars and Planetary Order
Describes the sphere of fixed stars, its motion, rotation periods, planetary order, and reasons for naming.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the location, motion, and rotation periods of the sphere of fixed stars, and the order of the planetary spheres below it.

Core Idea
Fixed stars move too slowly to see—their constancy reveals the structure of the cosmos.

Medieval astronomy placed the fixed stars in their own sphere below the ninth sphere. This lesson explains that sphere's motion, its debated rotation periods, and the layout of the planetary spheres beneath it. Understanding this helps us see how pre-modern thinkers reasoned about the heavens based on observation and philosophical principles.

Guided Path
1
Locate the sphere of fixed stars
The passage begins by placing the sphere of fixed stars directly under the ninth sphere. Notice it moves from west to east on its own ecliptic and poles, distinct from the ninth.
This is a key point: different spheres have different axes of rotation.
2
Note the rotation periods
Three different figures are given for its full rotation: 36,000 years, 23,760 years, or 25,200 years. This shows the uncertainty in ancient estimates.
The long timescales explain why the stars appear fixed to the naked eye.
3
List the planetary order
Below the sphere of fixed stars are seven planetary spheres in order: Saturn, Jupiter, Mars, Sun, Venus, Mercury, Moon. The Sun is placed between the upper and lower planets as a preferred arrangement.
This order follows Ptolemaic geocentric astronomy.
4
Understand why they are called 'fixed'
Two reasons are given: either their motion is extremely slow so it's only detectable by precise observation, or the distances between them and their latitudes relative to the ecliptic remain constant.
The second reason is more philosophical—constancy implies stability.
5
Grasp the argument for distinct ecliptics
The passage ends with a logical argument: if the container (ninth sphere) and contained (eighth sphere) moved on the same circles and poles, their relative motion would not be perceived. Thus they must have different axes.
The text cuts off here, but the intended conclusion is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلك الثوابت تحت الفلك التاسع. قال: (وتحته فلك الثوابت يتحرك من المغرب إلى المشرق على منطقة وقطبين غير منطقة التاسع، وقطبية ويتم دورة في ستة وثلاثين ألف سنة، أو في ثلاث وعشرين ألف سنة، وسبعمائة وستين سنة، أو في خمسة وعشرين ألف سنة، ومائتي سنة، على اختلاف الآراء، ثم تحته فلك زحل، ثم المشتري، ثم المريخ، ثم الشمس، ثم زهرة، ثم عطارد، ثم القمر، استحساناً فيما بين الشمس والسفليين). سمي بذلك لكونه مكاناً للكواكب الثوابت أعني ما عدا السبعة السيارة، وتسميتها ثوابت إما لبطء حركتها في الغاية بحيث لم تدرك إلا بالنظر الدقيق، وإما لثبات ما بينها من الأبعاد على وتيرة واحدة وثبات عروضها عن منطقة حركتها، وحكموا بكون حركة الثامن على منطقة وقطبين غير منطقة التاسع وقطبيه، لأن حركة الحاوي والمحوى إذا كانت على مناطق وأقطاب بأعيانها، لا تحس.
The sphere of fixed stars is under the ninth sphere. He said: (And under it is the sphere of fixed stars, moving from west to east on an ecliptic and two poles other than the ecliptic of the ninth and its poles, and it completes a rotation in thirty-six thousand years, or in twenty-three thousand seven hundred sixty years, or in twenty-five thousand two hundred years, according to differing opinions. Then under it is the sphere of Saturn, then Jupiter, then Mars, then the Sun, then Venus, then Mercury, then the Moon, as a preference regarding what is between the Sun and the lower [planets].) It is named thus because it is the place for the fixed stars, meaning other than the seven planets. And they are called fixed either because of the extreme slowness of their motion such that it is only perceived by precise observation, or because of the constancy of the distances between them in a single manner and the constancy of their latitudes relative to the ecliptic of their motion. And they ruled that the motion of the eighth is on an ecliptic and two poles other than the ecliptic of the ninth and its poles, because the motion of the container and the contained, if they were on the same ecliptics and poles, it would not be perceived.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage describes the sphere of fixed stars (فلك الثوابت) located directly below the ninth sphere. The quoted text states its motion is from west to east on its own ecliptic and poles, distinct from the ninth sphere's. Its rotation period is given with three different figures from different opinions: 36,000 years, 23,760 years, or 25,200 years. Below it are the spheres of the seven planets in order: Saturn, Jupiter, Mars, Sun, Venus, Mercury, and Moon, with the Sun placed between the upper and lower planets as a preferred arrangement. The commentary explains that it is called the sphere of fixed stars because it contains stars other than the seven moving planets. These stars are called 'fixed' either because their motion is extremely slow (only detectable through precise observation) or because the distances between them and their latitudes relative to their own ecliptic remain constant. Finally, the passage notes that the eighth sphere's motion must be on its own distinct ecliptic and poles, because if the container (ninth sphere) and the contained (eighth sphere) moved on the same circles and poles, their relative motion would not be perceptible (the text cuts off: 'لا تحس' – it would not be perceived). This is a key argument for the existence of multiple spheres with independent motions.

Key Terms

الكواكب السيارة
منطقة
قطب
بطء الحركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the fixed stars are completely immobile and never move.
Correction: The passage clarifies that fixed stars do move, but their motion is extremely slow—taking tens of thousands of years to complete a cycle—so it is not noticeable without precise observation.

Checkpoint
Why are the fixed stars called 'fixed' (ثوابت)?

B) Because their motion is very slow or their relative positions remain constant.

D) Because they are all the same size.

Key Takeaway
The sphere of fixed stars lies below the ninth sphere, moves extremely slowly on its own ecliptic, and contains stars that appear fixed due to their slow motion or constant relative positions; beneath it are the seven planetary spheres.

Defining ممثل: The Deferent Sphere of a Planet
Defining the technical term 'ممثل' for planetary spheres

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical term 'ممثل' as used for planetary spheres, distinguishing the deferent from the epicycle.

Core Idea
The mumaththil is the deferent sphere, concentric with the zodiac.

This passage introduces a key term in Ptolemaic astronomy as used by Muslim scholars. Before we can discuss planetary motions, we need precise vocabulary. Here, the author defines 'ممثل' (mumaththil) for the spheres of the seven planets, clarifying that it refers to the total sphere that matches the zodiac in center and poles.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's own phrasing set the context.
Notice the phrasing 'يعني أن' signals an explanation.
2
Identify the definition and its explanation
The passage has two parts: a statement (the spheres are mumaththilat) and an explanation (the total sphere is called mumaththil because it agrees with the zodiac).
3
Contrast with the common misunderstanding
Many think mumaththil refers to the small epicycle. The passage shows it is the deferent. Note the keywords 'الفلك الكلي' (total sphere).
This distinction is crucial for understanding planetary models.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأفلاكها الكلية ممثلات لكون مناطقها على منطقة البروج . يعني أن الفلك الكلي لكل من السبعة السيارة يسمى ممثل ذلك الكوكب بمعنى كونه ممثلاً لفلك البروج ، أي موافقاً له بالمركز والمنطقة والقطبين.
He said: 'And their total spheres are epicycles (mumaththilāt) because their circles are on the circle of the zodiac.' Meaning that the total sphere of each of the seven wanderers is called the mumaththil of that planet, in the sense of being an epicycle of the zodiac, i.e., agreeing with it in center, circle, and poles.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'ممثل' (mumaththil, literally 'simulator' or 'epicycle'). It states that the total (deferent) sphere of each planet is called its mumaththil because it is concentric with the zodiac sphere, sharing the same center, plane (circle), and poles. This is a technical term in Ptolemaic astronomy.

Correction: In this context, 'ممثل' denotes the total (deferent) sphere that is concentric with the zodiac, not the small epicycle.

The Celestial Divisions: Zodiac Signs, Equinoxes, and Solstices
Division of the zodiac and definition of equinoxes and solstices

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how ancient astronomers divided the zodiac into twelve signs, subdivided them into degrees and minutes, and defined the equinoxes and solstices based on the sun's motion.

Core Idea
The zodiac is a coordinate system defined by the sun's annual path and its relation to the celestial equator.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid explains the mathematical foundation of the zodiac. Ancient astronomers used two intersecting circles—the ecliptic and the celestial equator—to divide the sky into twelve equal sections. They then established the four cardinal points that mark the start of seasons: equinoxes and solstices. Understanding these divisions is essential for grasping how premodern astronomy tracked celestial motions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the complete Arabic text once to get a feel for its technical style. Then read the translation to ensure you understand the literal meaning.
The passage uses specialized terms like منطقة البروج (zodiac) and معدل النهار (celestial equator).
2
Identify the two reference circles
Notice that the division starts from two circles. Identify what they are (the ecliptic and the celestial equator) and how they intersect to create four quadrants.
3
Follow the subdivision chain
Trace the subdivision: four parts → two points per quarter → six circles → twelve signs. Then each sign into 30 degrees, each degree into 60 minutes, and so on.
This recursive halving/grouping is a standard method for dividing a circle.
4
Learn the four cardinal points
Find where the two equinoxes and two solstices are placed along the ecliptic, and note how they relate to the sun's position relative to the equator.
The names are connected to seasons: spring, summer, autumn, winter.
5
Reflect on the star names
Understand that the names of the signs come from imagined star figures that once aligned with the sections. Precession shifted the stars, but the names were kept for convenience.
This shows the practical nature of astronomical conventions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فبحسب هاتين الدائرتين ينقسم فلك البروج أربعة أقسام متساوية، فتوهموا على كل واحد من ربعين متلاصقين منها نقطتين، بعد كل منهما عن الأخرى كبعد الأخرى عن أقرب طرفي الربع إليها. ثم توهموا أربع دوائر تمر بالنقط الأربع، وبمقابلاتها من الربعين الباقيين، وفرضوا الدوائر الست قاطعة للعالم، فانقسم الفلك الأعظم، وسائر الأفلاك الممثلة اثني عشر قسماً، سموا كل قسم منها برجاً، وجعلوا كل برج ثلاثين قسماً، سموا كل قسم درجة، وكل درجة ستين قسماً سموا كل قسم دقيقة، وهكذا، جعلوا كل دقيقة ستين ثانية، وكل ثانية ستين ثالثة بالغاً ما بلغ، وسموا من نقطتي تقاطع المنطقتين النقطة التي تجاوزها الشمس إلى شمال معدل النهار، أعني مائل القطب الشمالي اعتدالاً ربيعياً، والنقطة التي تجاوزها الشمس إلى جنوب المعدل اعتدالاً خريفياً، وسموا نقطة تقاطع منطقة البروج، والدائرة المارة بالأقطاب الأربعة في جانب الشمال انقلاباً صيفياً، وفي جانب الجنوب انقلاباً شتوياً، لأن الربيع اسم لمدة حركة الشمس من الاعتدال الآخذ في الشمال إلى الانقلاب الشمالي، أعني زمان قطعها للحمل والثور والجوزاء، والصيف لمدة حركتها منه إلى الاعتدال الآخذ في الجنوب، أعني زمان قطعها للسرطان والأسد والسنبلة، والخريف لمدة حركتها منه إلى الانقلاب الجنوبي، أعني زمان قطعها للميزان والعقرب والقوس، والشتاء لمدة حركتها منه إلى الاعتدال الربيعي أعني زمان قطعها للجدي والدلو والحوت، وهذه الأسامي مأخوذة من صور توهمت من كواكب وقعت عند القسمة بحذاء الأقسام، وحين انتقلت عن محاذاتها بحركة الفلك الثامن آثروا بقاء الأسماء تيسيراً للأمر في ضبط الحركات.
So according to these two circles, the sphere of the zodiac is divided into four equal parts. They imagined on each one of two adjacent quarters two points, the distance of each from the other being like the distance of the other from the nearest end of the quarter to it. Then they imagined four circles passing through the four points and through their opposites from the remaining two quarters, and they supposed the six circles cutting the universe. So the greatest sphere and the rest of the spheres were divided into twelve sections; they called each section a sign (burj), and they made each sign thirty sections, calling each section a degree (daraja), and each degree sixty sections, calling each section a minute (daqiqa), and so on; they made each minute sixty seconds, and each second sixty thirds, however far it goes. And they named from the two points of intersection of the two circles: the point which the sun passes to the north of the celestial equator, that is, inclining towards the north pole, the vernal equinox, and the point which the sun passes to the south of the equator, the autumnal equinox. And they named the point of intersection of the zodiacal circle and the circle passing through the four poles on the north side the summer solstice, and on the south side the winter solstice. For spring is the name for the duration of the sun's motion from the equinox going north to the northern solstice, that is, the time of its passage through Aries, Taurus, and Gemini; summer is for the duration of its motion from it to the equinox going south, that is, the time of its passage through Cancer, Leo, and Virgo; autumn is for the duration of its motion from it to the southern solstice, that is, the time of its passage through Libra, Scorpio, and Sagittarius; winter is for the duration of its motion from it to the vernal equinox, that is, the time of its passage through Capricorn, Aquarius, and Pisces. And these names are taken from forms imagined from stars that were aligned with the sections at the time of division; and when they shifted from their alignment by the motion of the eighth sphere, they preferred to keep the names for ease in regulating motions.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage describes how the ancient astronomers divided the celestial sphere and defined the equinoxes and solstices. The author begins by explaining that based on two intersecting circles (the ecliptic and the celestial equator), the zodiacal sphere is divided into four equal parts. Then astronomers imagined two points on each of two adjacent quarters, and from these points they drew four circles that, together with the two original circles, divide the universe into twelve sections – these are the twelve signs. Each sign is further subdivided into 30 degrees, each degree into 60 minutes, and so on. Next, the author names the four cardinal points: the vernal equinox (where the sun crosses from south to north), the autumnal equinox (north to south), the summer solstice (northernmost point), and the winter solstice (southernmost). He then links these points to the seasons, specifying which three signs the sun traverses during each season. Finally, he notes that the names of the signs originated from imagined star figures that once coincided with the sections, but even after precession moved the stars, the names were retained for practical convenience.

Key Terms

معدل النهار
اعتدال ربيعي
اعتدال خريفي
انقلاب صيفي
انقلاب شتوي
برج
درجة
دقيقة
ثانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The zodiac signs are fixed physical divisions of the sky that still align with the constellations that gave them their names.
Correction: The signs are defined by the sun's motion and the equinoxes; due to precession, the constellations have shifted away from their original positions, but the definitions based on solar motion remain useful.

Checkpoint

B) Autumnal equinox

Key Takeaway
The zodiac is a coordinate system based on the sun's yearly path, divided into twelve signs, and the equinoxes and solstices are the key points that define the seasons.
