Definition of Mizaj and Its Conditions
Taftazani defines mizaj and explains its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of mizaj (mixture) as a uniform intermediate quality arising from the interaction of the four elements with minute parts, and the conditions necessary for mixing.

Core Idea
Mizaj is a uniform intermediate quality from broken elemental qualities.

Taftazani's preface on mixture lays the groundwork for understanding compound bodies in kalam. He defines mizaj as a uniform intermediate quality that results when the four elements with very small parts interact, breaking the intensity of each quality. This introduction highlights the conditions of smallness of parts, involvement of all four elements, and the role of contact. It also contrasts the views of physicians and philosophers on the forces involved.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of mizaj
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the definition: when elements come together and interact, a uniform intermediate quality arises.
2
Understand the conditions
Identify why smallness of parts is necessary (contact through surfaces) and why all four elements are required (no mixture from some).
3
Compare the views on forces
Notice that physicians consider qualities as the forces, while philosophers posit specific forms acting through qualities as intermediaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال القسم الرابع في المركبات التي لها مزاج وفيه مقدمة ومباحث. أما المقدمة ففي المزاج: إذا اجتمعت العناصر المتصغرة الأجزاء جداً فتفاعلت بقواها فانكسرت سورة كل من الكيفيات الأربع حدثت كيفية متوسطة متشابهة في الكل هي المزاج. واعتبر تصغر الأجزاء لأن تأثير الجسم وإن أمكن بدون المماسة إلا أنه في الامتزاج بالمماسة وهي تتكثر بتكثر السطوح التابع لتكثر الأجزاء. والمراد بالعناصر أربعتها إذ لا مزاج عن البعض عند الجمهور فلا بد من الكون والفساد ليحصل النار. وبالقوى الكيفيات عند الأطباء والصور النوعية عند الفلاسفة حيث أثبتوها وجعلوا الكيفيات واسطة في فعلها لا فاعلة. لأن تفاعل الكيفيتين إن كان معاً كان الشيء مغلوباً عن شيء غالباً عليه وإن كان على التعاقب كان المغلوب عن الشيء غالباً عليه وبالعكس. وأورد مثله على توسط الكيفية
He said: The fourth section is on compounds that have a mixture, and it contains a preface and investigations. As for the preface, it is about mixture: When the elements [with] very small parts come together, and they interact with their forces, and the intensity of each of the four qualities is broken, a middle quality similar in all arises, which is the mixture. And smallness of parts is considered because the effect of a body, although possible without contact, yet in mixing it is through contact, and it increases with the increase of surfaces that follow the increase of parts. And what is meant by elements is all four, for there is no mixture from some according to the majority, so generation and corruption are necessary for fire to occur. And by forces, [they mean] the qualities according to the physicians, and the specific forms according to the philosophers where they posit them, and they make the qualities intermediaries in their action, not agents. Because the interaction of two qualities if it is simultaneous, the thing is overcome by something that overcomes it, and if it is successive, the overcome is overcome by something that overcomes it, and vice versa. And he mentioned the like of it regarding the intermediacy of quality.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a preface defining mizaj (mixture). Taftazani begins by introducing the fourth section on compounds with mixture. The definition: when the four elements (fire, air, water, earth) with very minute parts come together, their forces interact, breaking the intensity of each quality (hot, cold, wet, dry), resulting in a uniform intermediate quality – the mizaj. The smallness of parts is necessary because mixing occurs through contact, and smaller parts increase surface area, facilitating interaction. All four elements are required; the majority hold that a mixture cannot occur from fewer elements; thus generation and corruption (e.g., producing fire from others) are needed. He then notes two views: physicians say the forces are qualities (kayfiyyāt), philosophers say specific forms (ṣuwar naw‘iyya) act through qualities as intermediaries. He explains that simultaneous interaction leads one quality to dominate, while successive interaction alternates dominance. He ends by mentioning a similar argument concerning the intermediacy of quality.

Key Terms
المزاج
العناصر
الكيفيات الأربع
التفاعل
المماسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mizaj occurs by mere juxtaposition of elements.
Correction: Taftazani emphasizes that mixing requires interaction and breaking of qualities, not just juxtaposition.

Checkpoint
Why does Taftazani insist on the smallness of parts in the definition of mizaj?
A) To ensure purity
B) To increase surface area for contact
C) To reduce weight
D) To avoid heat loss

Key Takeaway
Mizaj is a uniform intermediate quality resulting from the interaction of all four elements with minute parts, requiring contact and the breaking of each quality's intensity.

Critique of Ibn Sina's Definition of Mizaj
Al-Taftazani criticizes Ibn Sina's definition of mizaj for logical flaws.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical flaws in Ibn Sina's definition of mizaj and the role of conditional propositions in logical analysis.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's definition of mizaj fails due to circularity and a problematic conditional structure.

Al-Taftazani begins his critique by noting that Ibn Sina's definition in the Qanun is 'outside the canon.' He then dissects the definition, showing that it either leads to circularity (if the conditional is descriptive) or has a meaningless consequent (if attached to the main verb). This analysis highlights the importance of proper logical structure in definitions. We'll follow his reasoning and also learn about conditional propositions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to get a sense of the argument's flow.
Notice the wordplay on 'Qanun'.
2
Identify the two interpretations of the conditional
Al-Taftazani considers two possibilities for the phrase 'when they interact'. Identify each and see the resulting logical problem.
3
Understand conditional propositions
The passage ends with a definition of conditional propositions (shartiyya). Note the two types: conjunctive and disjunctive.
This is background for the critique.

وعبارة ابن سينا في القانون، خارج عن القانون جداً، وذلك أنه قال: المزاج كيفية تحدث عن تفاعل كيفيات متضادة موجودة في عناصر متصغرة الأجزاء، لتماس أكثر كل واحد منها أكثر الأجزاء إذا تفاعلت بقواها بعضها في بعض حدثت عن جملتها كيفية متشابهة في جميعها هي المزاج، فسلك طريق التعريف منحرفاً إلى طريق التفريع ، وحينئذ فالشرطية (١). أعني قوله إذا تفاعلت الخ أن كانت صفة العناصر وقع تكرار لا حاجة إليه، وكان قوله هي المزاج أجنبياً لا يرتبط بما سبق إلا بأن يجعل صفة كيفية متشابهة ، فيذكر المحدود في الحد، وإن جعل الظرف متعلقاً بقوله يحدث ، كان الواقع في معرض الجزاء أجنبياً لا معنى له . والظاهر أن قوله إذا تفاعلت الخ أخذ في طريق التفريع بعد تمام التعريف، وأسند التفاعل في التعريف إلى الكيفيات على ما هو ظاهر نظر الصناعة، وفي التفريع إلى العناصر بواسطة القوى التي هي الكيفيات أو الصور النوعية على ما هو أقرب إلى التحقيق الفلسفي. فإن قيل : فيدخل في التعريف توابع المزاج. قلنا : وكذلك إذا جعلنا الشرطية من تمام التعريف، لأن إخراجها بقيد المتشابهة تفسيراً لها بما فسروا به المتوسطة تعسف على ما سيجيء إن شاء الله تعالى، ثم لا بد لتوضيح المقام من الكلام في مواضع : القضية الشرطية هي التي تتركب من قضيتين ويحكم فيها على تعلق أحد طرفيها بالآخر، وهي إما متصلة وإما منفصلة، فالشرطية المتصلة هي التي توجب أو تسلب لزوم قضية أخرى. كقولنا: إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود والشرطية المنفصلة : هي التي توجب أو تسلب عناد قضية لاخرى كقولنا إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً وإما أن يكون فرداً.
The statement of Ibn Sina in the Qanun is very much outside the canon. That is because he said: 'Temperament is a quality that occurs from the interaction of opposing qualities present in elements with reduced parts, due to the contact of each one of them with the most parts [of others]; when they interact with their powers on each other, there occurs from their totality a similar quality in all of them, which is the temperament.' Thus he took the path of definition deviating to the path of derivation. Then the conditional proposition (al-shartiyya). I mean his saying 'when they interact' etc.: if it is a description of the elements, then there is a repetition that is unnecessary, and his saying 'it is the temperament' is foreign, not connected to what preceded unless it is made a description of 'similar quality', so he would mention the defined in the definition. And if the circumstance is made attached to his saying 'occurs', then the actual in the position of the consequent becomes foreign without meaning. And the apparent is that his saying 'when they interact' etc. is taken in the path of derivation after the completion of the definition, and he attributed the interaction in the definition to the qualities according to what is apparent in the view of the craft, and in the derivation to the elements by means of the powers which are the qualities or the specific forms, according to what is closer to philosophical verification. If it is said: Then the accidentals of temperament enter into the definition. We say: Likewise if we make the conditional part of the complete definition, because excluding it by the qualification 'similar' interpreting it as they interpreted 'intermediate' is forced, as will come, God willing. Then, to clarify the situation, we must speak on points: The conditional proposition (al-qadiyya al-shartiyya) is that which is composed of two propositions and judges in it on the connection of one of its two parts to the other. It is either conjunctive (muttasila) or disjunctive (munfasila). The conjunctive conditional is that which affirms or denies the entailment of another proposition, like our saying: 'If the sun is rising, then the day exists.' The disjunctive conditional is that which affirms or denies the opposition of another proposition, like our saying: 'Either this number is even, or it is odd.'
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage criticizes Ibn Sina's definition of temperament (mizaj) as presented in his famous medical work al-Qanun. The author begins by remarking that Ibn Sina's definition is 'very much outside the canon' (خارج عن القانون جداً), playing on the title of the book. He then quotes Ibn Sina's definition: 'Temperament is a quality that occurs from the interaction of opposing qualities present in elements with reduced parts... when they interact... there occurs from their totality a similar quality in all of them, which is the temperament.' The author points out that Ibn Sina has deviated from the proper method of definition (ta'rif) into the method of derivation (tafri'). He then analyzes the conditional proposition 'when they interact' (إذا تفاعلت). He considers two possibilities: If this condition is a mere description of the elements, then it is a repetition of what was already said, and the phrase 'it is the temperament' becomes unrelated unless it is taken as a description of 'similar quality' — leading to circularity (mentioning the defined in the definition). If the condition is meant to be attached to the verb 'occurs', then the conditional clause (if... then...) is left without a meaningful consequent. The author suggests that the correct interpretation is that the conditional is part of the derivation after the definition is complete, and the interaction in the definition is attributed to qualities (kayfiyyat) as per the craft, while in the derivation it is attributed to elements via their powers. An objection is raised: if the definition includes the conditional as part of the definition, then accidentals (tawabi') would enter. The author responds that excluding the conditional by the qualification 'similar' interpreted as 'intermediate' is forced. Finally, a footnote defines conditional propositions (al-qadiyya al-shartiyya), explaining the two types: conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila), with examples.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina's definition is logically sound and complete.
Correction: The analysis shows it suffers from either circularity or a meaningless conditional consequent, violating proper definitional method.

Checkpoint
What is the main logical flaw identified in Ibn Sina's definition of mizaj?
A) It defines temperament as a quality but does not mention the elements.
B) It uses a conditional proposition that leads to circularity or a meaningless consequent.
C) It fails to include the qualifier 'intermediate'.
D) It defines temperament as the result of interaction without specifying the number of elements.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's definition of mizaj is logically flawed because its conditional clause either creates circularity or leaves the consequent meaningless, demonstrating the importance of precise logical structure in definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you explain a concept of faith to others, do you ever use words in a way that assumes what you are trying to prove? How would you feel if someone like Al-Taftazani dissected your explanation and found circularity?
?
What is one deeply held belief about Islam that you have never examined for logical consistency? Are you willing to subject it to the same rigorous critique Al-Taftazani applied to Ibn Sina?
?
If a scholar you respect made a logical error in defining a core term, would you have the courage to point it out publicly? What prevents you from being that honest about intellectual flaws in your own community?

7 min
The Meaning of Similarity in Mixture
Definition and distinction of similarity in mixture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of similarity of temperamental quality in a mixture and how it distinguishes true mixture from mere composition or juxtaposition.

Core Idea
True mixture requires a uniform intermediate quality throughout, not just sensory appearance of similarity.

In this passage, the author clarifies a key concept in the theory of mixture: what does it mean for the temperamental quality to be similar throughout? He explains that genuine similarity must be real, not merely perceived, and that this uniformity is what allows a mixture to receive new forms. He also distinguishes his view from an alternative definition found in medical commentaries.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument.
The passage begins with an example, then states the definition, then contrasts with mere composition.
2
Identify the author's definition of similarity
Locate where the author defines similarity of temperamental quality. Note that he requires equality in specific reality across all parts, differing only by locus.
The definition uses terms like 'تماثل' and 'تساوي في الحقيقة النوعية'.
3
Understand the distinction between mixture and composition
The author contrasts true mixture (imtizaj) with mere composition (tarkib) and mixing (ikhtilat). See how he argues that without a uniform intermediate quality, there is no genuine mixture.
Mixture is defined as producing an intermediate similar quality, whereas composition is more general.
4
Examine the critique of the alternative definition
Read the author's rejection of the definition from Sharh al-Qanun, which defines similarity by relativity. Understand why he considers it contradictory to reason and transmitted knowledge.
The alternative definition was meant to refute an objection by the Imam, but the author insists his own definition is correct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصورة النار تكسر رطوبة الماء بيبوستها لا بحرارتها.
الرابع: أن معنى تشابه الكيفية المزاجية في الكل: أن الحاصل في كل جزء من الأجزاء المركبة أو البسيطة للممتزج، تماثل الحاصل في الجزء الآخر أي تساويه في الحقيقة النوعية من غير تفاوت إلا بالمحل، حتى أن الجزء الناري، كالجزء المائي في الحرارة والبرودة، والرطوبة واليبوسة وكذا الهوائي والأرضي، إذ لو اختلفت الكيفيات في أجزاء الممتزج، وكان التشابه في الحس لشدة امتزاج الكيفيات العنصرية الباقية على حالها بحيث لا تتميز عند الحس لما كان هناك فعل وانفعال، ولم تتحقق كيفية وجدانية بها يستعد الممتزج لفيضان صورة معدنية أو نباتية أو حيوانية أو نفس إنسانية عليه. بل كان هذا مجرد تركيب ومجاورة بين العناصر لا امتزاج لأن الامتزاج هو اجتماع العناصر، بحيث تحدث منه الكيفية المتوسطة المتشابهة، والتركيب أعم من ذلك، وكذا الاختلاط، وقد يجعل مرادفاً للامتزاج، كذا في الشفاء، وما ذكر في شرح القانون، من أن معنى التشابه في جميع الأجزاء أن يستحر بالقياس إلى البارد ويستبرد بالقياس إلى الحار، وكذا في الرطوبة واليبوسة قصداً إلى دفع اعتراض الإمام بدخول توابع المزاج في تعريفه، فمخالفة لصريح العقل، وصحيح النقل، وما ذكرنا هو المفهوم من اللفظ والمذكور في كلام القوم.
And the form of fire breaks the moisture of water by its dryness, not by its heat. The fourth: that the meaning of similarity of the temperamental quality in the whole is that what is obtained in every part of the composite or simple parts of the mixture is similar to what is obtained in the other part, i.e., equal in specific reality with no disparity except by the locus, such that the fiery part is like the watery part in heat and cold, moisture and dryness, and likewise the airy and earthy, because if the qualities differed in the parts of the mixture, and the similarity were only in sensation due to the intensity of mixing of the elemental qualities remaining in their state such that they are not distinguished by sense, then there would be no action and passion, and no existential quality would be realized by which the mixture is prepared for the overflow of a mineral, plant, animal, or human soul form onto it. Rather, this would be mere composition and juxtaposition of elements, not mixture, because mixture is the coming together of elements such that an intermediate similar quality arises from it, and composition is more general than that, and likewise mixing; and it may be made synonymous with mixture, as in al-Shifa'. And what is mentioned in Sharh al-Qanun, that the meaning of similarity in all parts is that it is hot relative to cold and cold relative to hot, and likewise in moisture and dryness, intending to refute the objection of the Imam regarding the inclusion of concomitants of temperament in its definition, it contradicts clear reason and sound transmitted knowledge. And what we have mentioned is the understanding from the wording and what is mentioned in the statements of the people.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the concept of similarity of temperamental quality (tashabuh al-kayfiyya al-mizajiyya) in the context of mixture (imtizaj). The author begins with an example: the form of fire breaks the moisture of water by its dryness, not by its heat. Then he states the fourth point: the meaning of similarity is that every part of the mixture, whether composite or simple, has a quality that is equal in specific reality to the quality of any other part, differing only by the locus (i.e., the specific part it inheres in). For example, a fiery part in a mixture will have the same intermediate temperature and moisture as a watery part, because all parts share the same intermediate quality. If the qualities were actually different but appeared similar only due to intense mixing, there would be no real action and passion between the parts, and no genuine existential quality that prepares the mixture to receive a form—whether mineral, plant, animal, or human soul. Thus, true mixture requires a uniform intermediate quality throughout; otherwise it is mere composition (tarkib) or juxtaposition. The distinction is important: mixture (imtizaj) is specifically the production of a uniform intermediate quality, whereas composition is more general. Some make 'ikhtilat' synonymous with mixture, as in Ibn Sina's al-Shifa'. The author then critiques an alternative definition found in Sharh al-Qanun, which defines similarity by relativity (hot relative to cold, cold relative to hot). This alternative was intended to refute an objection by the Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) that such a definition would include the concomitants of temperament. But the author rejects it as contradictory to reason and transmitted knowledge. His own definition is the correct understanding from the wording and the consensus of the scholars.

Key Terms
تشابه الكيفية المزاجية
الامتزاج
الكيفية المتوسطة
التركيب
الاختلاط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that similarity in sensation due to intense mixing constitutes true mixture.
Correction: The author clarifies that true mixture requires actual homogeneous quality throughout, not just sensory appearance.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what distinguishes true mixture (imtizaj) from mere composition (tarkib)?
A) The presence of action and passion
B) The production of a uniform intermediate quality throughout all parts
C) The intensity of mixing such that elements are not distinguished by sense
D) The inclusion of concomitants of temperament

Key Takeaway
True mixture is characterized by a real homogeneous intermediate quality throughout all parts, not merely an appearance of similarity due to intense mixing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Just as true mixture requires real inner homogeneity across all parts, not just an outward appearance of unity – ask yourself: In your own heart, are your intentions, actions, and worship truly aligned with pure faith, or do you merely maintain an external show while inside there is fragmentation and contradiction? What specific step will you take today to seek inner sincerity?
?
The passage warns against mistaking sensory similarity for actual transformation. In your spiritual life, are you confusing outward conformity (like performing rituals) with genuine inner change (like repentance and humility)? Where might you be satisfied with appearance instead of reality, and how will you confront that today?
?
Without a real unified quality, the mixture cannot receive a higher form. Similarly, without sincere and unified devotion, can you expect to receive divine guidance or spiritual elevation? Examine one area where your faith lacks consistency – what is the 'element' that remains unchanged and prevents true transformation, and what will you do to mix it with true submission?
